RATING: 5
Best at 2 and maybe 3, and above that it really drags. I find that I really like the sandbox effect and relaxed feeling of the "feed your people" side-quest compared to Agricola, but you still sit and contest just a couple spaces on the (ever growing) board; the furnishing spot, which is unfortunate (mostly because I'm only passively interested in worker placement as an action draft mechanism). One of my favorite games to solo, I found it best (regardless of solo play or with other people) if you draft in roughly 2/3rds or 3/4ths of the furnishings instead of everything, otherwise the game becomes stale way too quickly. One other thing that I particularly don't like about the game is that it *requires* an organizer, otherwise it takes too long to setup/tear down to justify playing. Second, I found it encourages specialization in the cave so you spend most of your time jockeying for the furnish action.

RATING: 10
An Agricola variant, which means a high strategy, option-filled, "thematic", feeding-tension, worker placement game with great components and great scalability.

If you know Agricola you practically know how to play this game.  There are 12 instead of 14 rounds and each round you select an action (once chosen no one else can choose it), and periodically (with increasing frequency) you have a harvest where you get crop yields, feed your people and have animal husbandry.  This time you're a family of dwarves (odd yes) carving out a home in the mountains and growing a farm outside. 

There are a set number of actions to choose from that differ based on the number of players and a fixed set of actions that come into play each round like in Agricola. 

The main differences from Agricola are:
1) there are no cards. Instead the variability/special abilities/special point generators are in the form of rooms that can be built and these are ALL in play and OPEN to all players from the beginning similar to Agricola: all creatures big and small
2) there are now quests. Quests involve forging weapons using a new resource, ore, and selecting action spaces that allow you to choose free goods from a menu of items. The better your weapon the better the choices and the more you use your weapon the better it gets. 
3) scoring is more open. There are no minimums or maximums for scoring for example 20 vegetables is worth 20 points instead of being capped at 4 points and you don't get minus 1 point for not having any (caveat: you still lose points for having empty spaces on your board and for missing animal types)
4) there is another new resource, rubies, which are like a wild card. All rubies are worth 1 point each, but they can also be used to purchase almost anything at any time
5) family growth is not essential. You can go for growth but since family members are only worth 1 point instead of 3 points but still cost the same amount of food and there are more opportunities to grow and the game rounds are less, yes you can certainly win with only 2 players (after over 250 face to face games of Agricola I don't believe that is true for that game)
6) feeding is more flexible. Notice I said more flexible but contrary to what some people are saying it is NOT easy it is still a grind mainly because harvests occur nearly every round!
7) the game is 2 rounds shorter and scales from 1-7(!) players

I love Agricola it's my favourite game so naturally I really enjoy Caverna it gives me the same/similar flavour and feel but it's different enough that it feels fresh. If you enjoy one you will enjoy the other and most likely vice versa. Is it worth owning both?  Absolutely. I imagine that eventually there will be building expansions similar to how there are the card expansions for Agricola. 

Own the [boardgame=166187]mini-expansion[/boardgame], the [boardgame=183531]Water Features[/boardgame], as well as homemade [boardgame=154204]Christmas Chamber[/boardgame] and [boardgame=160803]LARP and Cosplay[/boardgame] promo tiles.

RATING: 9
Impressions based on first play: thoroughly enjoyed this one. I'd hate to be the one teaching it, but I was taught it and really had almost no questions. I just ended up forgetting a thing or two, but this was really minor given the massive scope of the game. I think that speaks to the game being well-designed. A lot of the game is fairly self-explanatory too, which helps. I was initially overwhelmed by the array of buildings, but it was explained to me that the game is very much a sandbox where you can choose a ton of different valid strategies, so I just randomly chose a strategy (mega sheep pasture + high-level weaponry adventuring + almost exclusively acquiring gold cave tiles). I did lose, but the scores were very close (83, 87, 90). I was originally skeptical of the game, thinking it was a game where a new player would get absolutely crushed. However, while experience is certainly an advantage (due to better knowledge of the value of the buildings and due to being able to better recognize opportunities), this game pleasantly surprised me with just how accessible it is. I also like how the board state & accumulation of resources on spaces forces players to adapt (..and it's what lost me the game). Instantly beloved.

I should note that the friend who taught me had the broken token insert for this game, which is truly a must-have as it reduced set-up down to five minutes and made the ridiculous number of meeples very easy to manage.

RATING: 8
played 5+ games, 3,4,5 players. (5p = 3.5h)
first time play, we were all so excited, that we all had analysis paralysis and had to quit early.

LIKE: Lots of options, like the theme. scales player count well. multiple paths to victory. i generally enjoy "engine" games.
DISLIKE: The number of options is a bit overwhelming at first. Some analysis paralysis (AP). Slightly lower replayability, after optimal build paths are found (but there are a few to explore).
OVERALL: I like stealing other player's end-game bonus rooms, once you know the game well enough. Some people think it's not an interactive game, but the worker placement competition and when to grab certain scoring tiles keeps the game tense. Great game.

RATING: 5
2014-11
Comparison with Agrigola is obvious here. So similar the games are. In Caverna I liked the way you could upgrade your workers to make them more efficient. But that is really the only reason I would play Caverna over Agrigola.

The sheer amount of information the players are exposed to right from the beginning was so overwhelming here, that I almost lost interest in trying to figure the game out. But what amazed me the most, was that towards the end you seemed to have very little to do despite all the options given to you. Oh, I am sure that after a few more plays you would be familiar with the game allowing you to concentrate more on your strategy. 

But why bother? Essentially, Caverna offers very few new experiences to veterans of Agricola and Le Havre. Instead, the game is more cluttered and fiddly lacking the elegance of its predecessors. I also preferred the way, how in Agricola you are trying to find the best way to utilise the randomly drawn cards instead of a prescribed selection of buildings. In short, there are too many components to setup, too much information to digest on your first few plays, too many options to choose from, and still too little to do towards the end of the game.

I would play Caverna if asked, but I won't spend any more time on learning it.

RATING: 8
Great game. For me it doesn't fire Agricola. I feel they're different enough that it's worth it to play both of them.  It's hard for me to pick a favorite between the two, but I guess I would lean slightly in favor of Agricola.

Play Caverna if you prefer: less randomness (Agricola has random cards dealt at the start of the game), to know all your options at the start of the game, easier final scoring, less punitive scoring, more focused strategies.

Play Agricola if you prefer: more variability between games, less up-front info to deal with (reading all the available tiles), more balanced strategies.

RATING: 8.55
Decided to finally give "Agricola 2.0" a chance as I think my wife might prefer its somewhat less punishing approach.

I like how a lot of the Agricola mechanics have been streamlined, and I like the inclusion of the rubies and the adventuring mechanic, and it is possible to have a more variable early and mid game than is possible in [i]Agricola[/i] (read: a more variable early and mid game that can still lead to ultimate success). The variety of buildings is such that multiple different strategies could be pursued, but yet the strategies don't feel as varied as [I]Ora et Labora[/I], for example.  

I prefer this to [i]Agricola[/i] at higher player counts, if for know other reason than that it includes paths to victory that are not as highly-dependent on early round family growth.

RATING: 7
A great game!

I would rate this higher except I knew Agricola, and loved it, first.  Caverna is Agricola with some streamlining.  While Agricola has some flaws (streaky cards, mainly), I do think they can be easily addressed while maintaining the overall fluid nature of the game (draft them).  Caverna squashes that broad decision tree into a static pool of rooms that are always available to everyone (at least initially).  In my mind this inhibits the replay value, though it does make the game a bit more approachable and accessible.

My rating could improve with more plays.  It's just that when I play Caverna I always first ask myself "why aren't we playing Agricola?"
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
+ Mini Expansion.

Caverna is... amazing! For my first three years into the hobby, this has been my favorite game, as it has all the qualities I find enjoyable in a game. It has an excellent strategic depth with many many paths to victory and it's extremely challenging, since you have to manage so much in such a short time. For the 20+ plays this game overwhelms you with all the options and possibilities that offers, giving you a huge deal of re-playability. I have so much fun with this game, that I don't even realize the time running.

I find almost pointless to talk about the components of the game. I've never seen anything like this... just amazing! Yet what really makes this game shine for me is the fact that, when you're finished playing it, you can look at your board and see what you built. Your animals, your mines, your rooms: it's just too cool!

After 50+ I've to say that I'm sadly a little burned out of this game. Now that I've explored every strategy multiple times, I find myself wishing to see something new coming out for this game. I want a nice expansion with lots of new room tiles, that could bring back the feeling of an incredible amount of options that the game gave me in the first place. Until then I'm forced to lower my rating a little... and I can't say anymore that this is my favorite game.

RATING: 9
Definitely seems like there are more viable early game paths in this than in Agricola.  Rather than just an urgent need to grow your family in order to be competitive, you can viably start farming or start breeding animals much earlier on, as well as mining and adventuring.  I really like how adventuring rewards a small family size by forcing you to play your higher level character last.  If you want to be able to play it before other players adventurers, you need to have less people than them.  This gives a few reasons why you wouldnt just go all in on family growth.  Additionally, the harvests come fast and furious, so you really need to develop an early food engine.

RATING: 9
Very fun game, but since I usually play this at big gatherings and 6 players, rules explanation can take 30 - 60 mins alone and the game will go on for most of an afternoon.  I am okay with that, as the game feels like it has enough heft and enough arc to support that.

New players (and even people who have not played it recently) may be overwhelmed by the number of unique buildings.  There is a 'simple' version of the game that has many less buildings, but that version feel TOO simple.  I have not yet tried a setup where I take the simple game and then add a random X tiles from the 'advanced' ones.

I tend to find that the person sitting with the best view of the building tiles in a 6 player game will win, as others don't want to slow the game down by constantly walking around the table to see it well.  Sane humans would probably do best playing this a 4 or less players, heh.  It scales well for mechanics, but downtime becomes a serious issue.

RATING: 7
I played this game with the expectation that it would be everything my heart could desire in a cave farming game. I was not disappointed. Firstly, I was surprised at how very "euro" it all felt (though I shouldn't have been). It's nice to see that after repeated playthroughs, my group couldn't find a "best" strategy. Caverna's piece quality was pretty through the roof, just like its pricing and that made a difference during play. It didn't feel too much like point salad but there was enough of that to sometimes turn a game's ending bitter (the typical predictable last turn of a eurogame). The art however needs serious work. I would love to see a less "storybook" style version of this game in the future as a remaster. Overall very good, very fun, bad art, predictable ending, great component quality.

RATING: 8.4

RATING: 9
Not as tense as agricola, and doesn't quite carry the sublime feeling the former game has of pulling everything together just at the right time, nor does it have a card draft which really elevates the 'gric into gamer nirvana. But it is highly entertaining, and much more accessible for non gamers. My non gamer gf loves it (and loathes agricola) and though the box can be as intimidating as hell, the rules are VERY well written. It doesn't take that long to play, the components are fantastic, and it gives a very satisfying sense of creation (more so than even agricola) as you watch your farm/cave take three dimensional shape in front of you. The price point is the only thing keeping me from recommending this wholeheartedly to everyone.

Played multiple times solo as part of the 2015 10x10. Not as good as agricola solo because it lacks the legacy element of keeping an occupation and building on your previous score, but still good fun and enough options to keep things interesting.

If you like any other Uwe game, you're probably gonna really like this too

RATING: 10
1-6(4)

Okay, so this game is gigantic, and has all sorts of crazy components.

It is very much a challenge to know if I prefer this to Agricola. I think they both do have a place.

Maybe I'm just too seasoned an Agricola player, but Caverna does feel quite a bit less tense than Agricola with the feeding and the need to grow your family, among other things.

And so, by its very nature, I guess Uwe had to compress many of the Agricola actions (getting fields and pastures going is easier, as is expanding your cave, and etc.) so that he could add something new and different (the adventuring). The game is fluid and takes out a bit of the mess of Agricola and it's nice that players can transition between the different tracks (adventuring and farming), but it doesn't feel much *more* than Agricola, just different. Enjoyable, though.

The big thing, of course, is the overload in getting the hundred community improvements upfront versus the cards. I understand Uwe's gradual move toward no luck, static setup games, but there is indeed something fun in looking at your initial hand of cards trying to suss out the synergies and the interactions and trying to build a game plan. That part of Agricola DID add a lot of interesting direction to any particular game session.

RATING: 6
Caverna is in a way Agricola on steroids. You do more, faster, the game is less punishing and it takes longer. Overall it's a good game, perhaps great, but I cannot overlook the fact that yet again a designer is re-engineering his own past games with little innovation.

If this was the first game in the series I would have rated it a clean 10 for what it offers, but in the light of Agricola, Ora et Labora, Glass Road and so on I simply see it with a more critical eye.

The game is quite long in 4-5 players, so a 7-player game would bring a huge amount of down time. IMHO, anything more than five players will be an exercise of patience.

The exploration is the new thing in Caverna and it works really well. Besides that, the rules are written very well, the setup is not that long and the game flows well, it's streamlined and has a somewhat epic finish. I will play it again, most likely retiring Agricola.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
November 2013:  Picked it up at Essen, without having played it, with first play of Caverna when I got home.

A solid, interesting to play sequel to Agricola that is about 75% Agricola and 25% new material, including buildings, dogs, mines, rubies and expeditions.  Interestingly, this sequel has more harvests, but less stress in feeding your family.  

And Caverna features many more ways to easily earn victory points, every turn. Thus, curiously, Caverna pushes the Agricola franchise in a somewhat Feldian direction: everything earns you victory points! Hey, players undoubtedly prefer it. 

(And for dog lovers, ha, the dogs are fun to have as well, because they can live anywhere on your board and watch sheep very nicely, without benefit of fences!  Excellent.) 

If played competitively, Caverna should become a much more complex, competitive brain burner than Agricola, because there are no hidden cards, or surprising card plays to disrupt your best laid plans or to blame for your loss, and there are more moving parts to play with and more ways to earn VP's.  All the information is right there in front of all the players, in the form of action spaces and various permutations of buildings that can be built to provide interesting resource or VP engines.  So good luck figuring out what the absolute best play is...at each moment in the game.  (I know, but I am not telling.)

In addition, it seems like there are more "combination actions spaces" in Caverna, with dual and sometimes triple actions involved.  Thus, you end up weighing combination against combination a bit more in Caverna than you do in Agricola, a slightly more complex decision than many of the "single action versus single action" decisions in Agricola.  Bottom line: this is a good euro to play, aside and apart from any (obvious) relationship it has with Agricola.  Giving it an 8 of 10.

May 2014:  Going to raise this to a 9 of 10 for its sheer weight of design, replayability, and euro-worker placement complexity.  This is a euro gamers' game of the highest order, or at least complexity of decision making, with a cornucopia of things to get in this game.  

I particularly love the rubies and the ridiculously complicated decisions on what combinations of goods to take on high armament quests late in the game.  For my first action on the last turn of the game, I will use my ruby to violate the placement order rule, so that I have to figure out the optimal combination of 4 goods to take on this list of 12?  Are you kidding me?! : ) Love it.

RATING: 1
This game is like taking a 5,000 piece puzzle, placing all the pieces face up, round robin picking up one piece. Where each person works on his own section in front of him, like a private mat, the rule being each piece has to fit in with one of his previous pieces. 

This was one waste of money. The artificial high price by MayFairGames and the imposed restrictions forced on resellers not to offer discounts beyond a token amount you have to think hard about buying this game.

Like most of this guy's designs his player count is way off.
5 players is a drag, I can not fathom anyone playing 6 or 7. 

Interestingly you don't hear from the fanboy's reviews anything about this point. 

The price of this game could have been greatly reduced if the player count had been adjusts 3-4. Solo play seems pointless.

For $98.89 plus shipping this was the biggest mistake I have ever made. 

I will avoid both Rosenberg and Outlook/MayFairGames in the future. I don't care for MayFairGames interference with supply and demand.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ugyanannyi pontot adok rá, mint a másik 3 nagy Uwe játékra. Ahogy az Ora = Le Havre 2.0, úgy Caverna = Agricola 2.0. Nehéz lenne megmondani, hogy melyik a jobb.

A törpés felfedezős rész tetszik: egy plusz dimenzió, ami bonyolítja, kombókirályok élvezkedhetnek rajta. Rubin szintén bonyolít és kombólehetőséget ad. Az állatos rész is színesebb, kidolgozottabb, mint az Agricolában. És a kismillió épület szintén, szinte végtelenek a lehetőségek.
Ahol egyszerűsítés történt: nyersanyagok ételre váltása, és a kártyák: gyakorlatilag nincs kártya a játékban, csak a technikai lebonyolítást segítő néhány kártya meg segédlet.
A két játék nehézsége és változatossága másban rejlik. Az Agricolában két parti sokkal inkább eltérhet a sok kártya miatt. Itt ez nincs, de olyan sokféle az épület, hogy szintén nagy az újrajátszhatóság.
Nekünk a 2 személyes volt 2 óra, szerintem a 7 személyes kb. 7 órás lehet ennek megfelelően:) Tuti nem próbálom ki:)
Ami fura volt, hogy az egyik partiban mindketten meg voltunk győződve róla, hogy ki nyer, és mellélőttünk. Nem nagyon lehet érezni szerintem.

A játék közben felmerült egyébként bennem, hogy a kevesebb talán több lenne. Kicsit erőltetett, hogy van arany is meg rubin is.

A játék sokkal kevésbé szűkös, mint az Agricola. Itt a pálya beépítése semmi kihívást nem jelent (2 főnél legalább is), mint ahogy az etetés sem: el nem tudom képzelni, hogy lehet -3 pontot szerezni.

Összességében: jó játék, de nekem egyenértékű az Agricolával élvezeti szintjét tekintve.
---
Kicsit elnagyolt lett a tegnapi, kimaradtak a külsőségek: bazi nagy doboz bazi sok cuccal. Sose láttam még egy játékban ennyi kinyomót... Ennek megfelelően az elő- és elpakolás macera. Ez is olyan, amit nem szívesen pakolnék elő 1 partiért, na de ha 4-5 órás egy parti, nincs mit tenni. A táblákon lévő információk profik. A lapkák sajnos nyelvfüggőek. Egyébként szép.
---
Kipróbáltam a lapka kilógatós taktikát. Sajnos nekem ezzel se sikerül a legendás 140+ pont, de így is stabilan +20-at hoztam vele a szokásos eredményeimhez képest. Durva hogy ennyire erős épületek vannak... Meg kéne kérdezni azoktól, akik sokat játszanak vele, hogy aki megszerzi pl. ezt az épületet, az nem nyer túl nagy eséllyel?

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Caverna, as far as what you get in the box, is the german response to FFG's coffin games. The box is literally choke full of stuff: tokens, boards, animeeples, vegemeeples, and much more fill a box that's almost twice deeper than Agricola's!

Like any Uwe Rosemberg's big box game released after 2007, Caverna is said to be the new Agricola. But this time the comparison is deserved: Caverna is the closest in kin to the farming game, and they share many mechanics.

Caverna takes many of the problems of Agricola and solves them in an elegant way.
There's no more need for wooden sticks to build fences, and basically there's not any "big" one-shot action at the end (no 15-wood-at-once fences, no maxi sowing).
There's less pressure to grow the family early, and there are more family growth spaces (with one "fixed" at turn 4 - great idea!).
There's no obligation to build any cooking improvement to feed: almost everything can be freely converted to food without constraint.

There is lots of new stuff: new resources (coins, ore, rubies), the need to dig the mountain and shape the cave, the weapons and the expeditions. And a new scoring system, more straightforward and that probably allows more specialization (without punishing too much for not having some kind of stuff).

Overall, the game has less "choke-points" than Agricola, and it feels more open and puts less pressure on players with feeding. The competition with other players is more central than against the game itself, even if competition for some places may not be as fierce as the development paths of players diverge during the curse of the game.

I don't know if Caverna will replace Agricola in the gaming world. And I don't know if it is a better game than its predecessor. But I know they are different enough games to stay both in a player's collection.
And I know Caverna is a darn good brainburning game, that I can't wait to explore in every way...

RATING: 7
First Play: I didn't buy this at Essen 2013 because from what I could see this didn't seem that different from Agricola and I saw no point in having both games in one collection. I was happy to play it when offered the chance and as the game was explained I kept saying, 'like in Agricola...like in Agricola....'. The cavern tiles are like the Agricola cards, the boards are alike but with a cave section, the farming of wheat and veg and the breeding of sheep boars and cows are near identical except there are dogs and donkeys, there is the same competition for the key actions and new actions are revealed each turn, you need to harvest and feed with a slight random mechanic, etc etc etc, its all 90% Agricola with a slight twist for all the mechanics.

Sure its a good game, it works, but Agricola did it all first 5 years before in a near identical format. Its the same car with a different paint job. 7.5 is my threshold to buy a game so this has to be 7. Not because its average, its just a near clone of a game I already own and my first impressions were right, I don't need two in my collection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Interesting re-implementation of Agricola.  It basically replaces the occupation cards and minor improvement cars with more major improvements cards (furnished rooms) while limiting the amount of them you can play to the number of playable spaces in your cave (caverns).

Another key difference between this and Agricola is that it is much easier to feed your family in Caverna because you are not required to convert your resources into food.  This change results in a less cutthroat experience.

I have been enjoying learning the nuances of Caverna and would recommend it to anyone who is a fan of Agricola or any heavy euro game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pro's:

1)  Getting your dwarves and etching out your little cave and farm feels great. Here is a thing you have built and these are the things you specialised in. Almost a style of roleplaying.
2) Aesthetically has some of the most pleasing components in any board game ever.
3) Variety in strategies and the customization options of your cave and farm feels very personal.
4) Surprisingly easy to teach.
5) It feels just as satisfying to win or to lose.

Con's:

1) End point scoring can be confusing sometimes.
2) Due to the nature of the game, being a winner or loser can feel totally separated from the aspects that make the game fun to play. Often times I find I simply don't care if I win or lose and thus find myself playing the game in a very different way to other competitive games.

RATING: 3
How people do find games like Caverna fun still beats me: rather than rewarding you for what you’ve done, such games enjoy punishing you for what you failed to achieve; at the same time, they revel in bullying their players by allowing them to take just a limited number of actions per turn, some of which cannot be chosen at all since someone else simply came before you in the turn order and anticipated your very first move.

Thus you keep taking half-needed, half-satisfying actions until the liberating end you yearned for so long comes at last, no matter the final score.

Playing these games is simply frustrating and quickly turns into an optimization exercise that feels the same as the most annoying office chores: the fun is the same but at least the latter grant you a reward - in the form of a wage - that the former fail to deliver as well. They are just a waste of your precious time and patience.

RATING: 9
Yeah, it's Agricola, but it's also a little bit better. Does it get minus for reinventing the wheel a bit? I suppose. But, I had a great time. Taking the special effects and putting them on the buildings was a great idea, and making the personal board play more interesting and varied with the animal types and caves is a good method of introducing variation in a game with little setup changes from game to game. We also found that where Agricola had huge cliffs related to certain actions, Caverna never completely shut anyone out. The frustration is lower, at the expense of a little bit of tension. I focused more on "making my plan work" then making sure I was starting player in Round X to get a new farmer. Oh, and extra dwarves are still good, but not the end-all that they are in Agricola. So, yeah, Agricola, but a little better. Ill take that.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Ewe Rosenburg. As with Touko Tahkokallio, Vlaada Chvatil and Martin Wallace, anything from this designer gets my full attention. Our first play (five players but aided by familiarity with "Agricola") clocked in just over three hours, which is good news. We weren't stepping on each others toes to any great degree but with more familiarity that could change. Thank heaven for "Imitation!" I think that four players will be the sweet spot here but I'm encouraged that five wasn't as bad (or as long) as I feared. However, six or seven looks to be too many.

After several plays now the elegance of the design is starting to really come through - I won't yet say for sure that there is a sweet spot for this game: each of my three, four and five player experiences were exciting and enjoyable, and the time length of the game does not appear to be linear as a function of the number of players as often is the case. The arrangement of the action boards for the different player numbers makes each "version" its own game by presenting an interesting balance of action options in each case. 

I had read a little about the "ore strategy, for lack of a better term, and had lots of ore and ruby mines down (complete with donkeys), which I then combined with the furnishing that knocks your food bill down by one food for every donkey in your mines. Needless to say food was no problem and I could use what I had to pay for other things (particularly Imitation). At the same time, I was able to fill all my outdoor spaces with plowed fields and pastures and I had a little of everything by the game end so lost no points for missing things. As well, I armed all my dwarves and built the tile that gives a bonus for each armed dwarf. My final score was 91 which I didn't think was too bad for a first outing. Enough to win? Not quite. Another player scored 96 with a better smattering of furnishings (such as bonuses for animals of which he had many). This is a great game but one which needs to be fully explored as there appear to be many fruitful lines of play to be combined with key furnishings which yield bonus points at the end. I've concluded that the judicious furnishing of your cave is the key to victory in this game along with a key specialization (ideally one that only you are pursuing). I finished with only three dwarves to the winner's four and, as in any worker placement game, actions are golden so his extra actions could have spelled the difference. I would shoot for a minimum of four in future. (Ed note: we just finished a three player game which the winner won with 71 points - making me think we did something wrong to come up with such high scores in the previous game).

The Agricola lineage shines through (not so much "Le Havre") but there are many important differences. I'm thinking that this game is to "Agricola" as "Steam" is to "Age of Steam." There will be those that prefer the tighter and arguably more random "Agricola" for a "gamer's game," while I think the more forgiving nature of "Caverna" lets players concentrate on various lines of play without having the threat of "begging cards" dog their every step. It may be that "the" winning line is out there - we've seen it happen before - but for now I just want to play this again and again. Hands down my favorite worker placement game!

RATING: 6
I was a bit disappointed in this one... It's impossible not to compare it with Agricola (the instructions even say, "Skip the first 12 pages if you've played Agricola!"), but taking out the two most controversial aspects: random occupations and feeding people, removes the replay-ability and the tension of the original.

I read a review that said there was an "embarrassment of riches", and that's a good way to put it.  Except for once, I've never seen a person have trouble getting all the resources they need/want.  With three rounds left, many of the players at the table are saying 'What... there's still more?"  "We all have a fully sustainable farm, and now we're going to go through 12 more actions."  "Well, I already have ten wheat, but I guess I'll just get some more then?  I don't know."  

That's not to say there's nothing good here.  The questing mechanic is brilliant, and the strategies with focusing on the quest side or the farm side add a really interesting layer to the Agricola formula.

It's just disappointing how easy it is to get what you want.  With an hour left, the game has lost its challenge, and it's all about throwing more points onto the pile.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I was not expecting to like this.  Although I burned out on it, I loved 'Gric playing it hundreds of times.  When Le Havre came out, it lasted about 40 or 50 before I became tired of what I saw as a single path to victory.  When OeL came out, it didn't even last that long; the huge number of resources and very long conversion paths just didn't interest me.  When I heard of Caverna (and its price tag), I expected more of the same... more bloat, more complexity, not any better gameplay.  I was wrong.

In Caverna, Rosenberg goes back to 'Gric and improves on it.  That may be a bit over-stated.  At the very least he takes what is arguably the best and worst feature of 'Gric (the cards) and moves in a different direction.  There are two primary differences between Caverna and 'Gric.  First the cards are removed and replaced with buildings.  Buildings come into play in a 2-step process.  First, players must lay the ground for the buildings (similar to plowing fields in 'Gric) on a second player board and then, with a second action, players can build a building taken from a shared pool.  The buildings work similarly to Major/Minor Improvements and Occupations.  They give discounts/bonuses/end-game VPs/...  The big difference is there are significantly less of then (~40 - 50) and they are all available to everyone from a shared pool.  I think I prefer it to 'Gric.  I wish there were more buildings (and I'm sure there will be expansions coming), but other than that I like the whole mechanic.

The only other significant change from 'Gric (and this could have been done with a 'Gric expansion) is expeditions.  It is a way for players to get some free stuff and the more times a player takes an expedition action with the same worker, the better the stuff that he can get is.  

My one play was solo so I can't speak for how bad blocking will be.  However, looking over the spaces, I suspect it will be a little less contentious than 'Gric but not much.  

Finally, there are other differences, but while significant to strategy, they are minor mechanic-wise.  There is no longer as much of a need to generalize.  Every vegetable and animal gets you a point, so no need to max sheep and boar.  Get one sheep to avoid the negative (or two so they breed) and then focus on boar (or vice-versa if food isn't an issue).  Harvests occur much more frequently than in 'Gric but I'm not sure if that will have much impact or not (since breeding and field harvest also happen more often).  

Overall, I was impressed.  Resource types are on par with 'Gric.  I like the idea of changing cards to buildings.  Luck of the draw is gone but repeated plays will be needed to see how much replayability there is.  The expeditions provide another spot where player strategy can diverge which may again make action spaces more/less contentious.  If I didn't already have 'Gric, I'd probably choose Caverna over it.  Already having 'Gric, it's hard to justify.  However, I'd be very happy to play someone else's copy.  Initial Rating: 7.5

RATING: 8
This is basically Agricola 2.0, but with dwarfs. It's a significant improvement, which is saying something--Agricola is a fine game in its own right. There are five really notable gameplay differences:

First, the unique improvements and occupations are gone, with no replacement. This improves game balance at the cost of making each game more similar. (The decision to lock the Family Growth spot in place has the same effect.) I think it's a good change overall; it's frustrating to lose an Agricola game because your opponent had sole access to an overpowered card. I'm glad that dynamic is gone.

Second, resources can easily be converted from one to another in this game. That's very different from Agricola, which severely punishes you if you are short 1 food or 1 wood at a critical moment. In Caverna, there are lots of ways to fix that instantaneously. This makes the game a much gentler and less stressful experience.

Adding to that milder vibe is the third change, that the spaces are generally more powerful. Everyone's farm tends to look good at the end of the game. It's possible to accomplish a great deal. It doesn't feel at all like Agricola, where everything is desperately scarce. You feel like you're running a flourishing society. 

The fourth major change--and my favorite thing about this game compared to Agricola--is that you can get victory points however you want. In Agricola, you have to diversify and get a little bit of everything to score well. That's just how the game works. In Caverna, you can choose to specialize. Want to focus on sheep to the exclusion of all else? It's possible. I really love this change. I think Agricola's scoring system is needlessly unintuitive and restrictive. Caverna still retains some elements of it (there are negative scores for not having animal types and for having undeveloped spaces) but generally it's a more flexible and positive system.

The fifth addition is the presence of expeditions. You can arm your dwarfs with ore, and then send them out to collect pretty much whatever kind of resource you want. This is an interesting alternate development path. The expedition spaces are usually hotly contested, but there's a twist--armed dwarfs must go last. I think expeditions are nicely balanced--viable but not dominant--and are definitely a worthwhile addition to the game.

I have two minor dislikes about this game. First, it can take forever to wait for your turn to come around again, and this just gets worse the more players you have. Second, for unclear reasons, the designers decided to make the game support up to seven players. Seven-player Caverna is a ludicrous idea (anything beyond four would just be interminable), and this means the game is needlessly heavy and expensive with far too many components. Still, as long as you stick to four players or less, the overall experience is quite good. If the idea of a less stressful, more light-hearted version of Agricola appeals to you, I recommend this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

The game has a crazy amount of bits and chits. I know many people like those, but I wish there were less of them. It's the only game I have that needs a custom storage solution to be playable without a hassle.

RATING: 8
I note that some comments and ratings on this site are based not on the merits of this game on a stand alone basis, but whether the writer regards the game as a potential purchase given the fact that he already owns Agricola.  This is rather unfair to the game.

This is not the first 'remake' to hit the market if one can call it that.  I think of Wallenstein and Shogun, Ave Caesar and Ausgebremst, Formel De and its versions, Imperial in its two versions, etc., etc.

There's no doubt that this game is an excellent design with enough changes to provide a new experience.  But it isn't cheap to buy so whether or not you regard the number of changes sufficient to have them both in your collection will really depend on your games buying budget.  Best to try it first.

Our first game had four players.  I admit I didn't find myself as frustrated in carrying out my plans as I often did in Agricola.  There always seemed to be enough alternatives available

Also the initial fear that I would not be able to feed my dwarves never materialised.  Most ended up with four dwarves and nobody failed to feed them.

I do have a criticism with the rule book, which might sound strange given that so much thought seems to have gone into it.  I found it very good at teaching the game but a nightmare to refer to during play when one needed to check up quickly on something.

That first game in which we needed to study all of the building options, all of which were new to us (we played with the suggested limited introductory number), took 150 minutes.

RATING: 7
I bought [b]Caverna[/b] because I am a big fan of [i]Agricola[/i], but there always seems something wrong with [i]Agricola[/i], like it could be a better game.  I was really hoping that [i]Caverna[/i] would fix everything that I felt that was wrong with [i]Agricola[/i].

Now that I have played both, I think I understand what was lacking in [i]Agricola[/i].  I love [i]Agricola[/i] because of the huge variety of improvements.  No two games feel exactly alike in [i]Agricola[/i].  But with [i]Caverna[/i], games do seem very similar because the choice of improvements are the same from game to game.  However, the scoring in [i]Agricola[/i] is unforgiving and limited, and forces you to diversify every single game, but [i]Caverna[/i] allows a player to specialize.  

I think that [i]Agricola[/i] and [i]Caverna[/i] are basically the same game.  If you own one, there is really no point in buying the other.  They are both good games.  However, I do like [i]Agricola[/i] a little better than [i]Caverna[/i].

RATING: 7
Played, enjoyed, but even though it plays more cleanly than Agricola, and gives you more interesting options with less screwing around and more flexibility, I don't think it's better.

Firstly, I am extremely suspicious of balance. Mining, looting, and a few of the mining boosters seem extremely strong. I've never seen anyone win who wasn't heavily into mining and got one or more of the three extremely strong rooms (food with donkeys in mines, extra ore for mines, points for ore). Many of the other rooms related to agriculture and husbandry are obviously laughably weak. Also, there seems to be a significant first turn first player advantage.

Secondly, the huge amount of up-front complexity, along with completely open and symmetric information is obviously problematic. With little inherent variability, this will be susceptible to optimal strategies and severe AP. I see the problems he was trying to solve, but this "solution" just introduces a different set of severe issues.

Also, the 7p support is ludicrous and has jacked up the retail for no good reason. Even 5p is probably a little too thin. 6 or 7p would be insane.

So I dunno. We were basically done with Agricola, and Caverna is kind of fun because it gets the system back to the table again with some neat new features. But I expect it to have a short shelf life.

Last thing, while Caverna is pretty playable for Agricola vets, for anyone who hasn't played Agricola, Caverna can be completely overwhelming. Newbies are going to have to plow through several or many games of frustration to catch up.

RATING: 5
It's tough to rate. It is definitely much easier to get into than Agricola if you've played that a lot -  it really does play like an agricola variant. I weep for players learning this one from scratch. The new board split between cave and field is pretty cool. The expeditions are a nice addition - though they massively exacerbate the turn order issue Agricola had with players to your left or right taking start player having a huge influence on your game. That said, there are 48! buildings to replace the cards here, and I'm sure they have interlocking combo possibilities that the nerds will be poring over for years. It is so overwhelming that I essentially ignored them for most of my game, just picking up some extra rooms and end game point scorers. All of the players in my game did - and it still clocked in at a little less than 3 hours for 5 players. At least the game is still very playable without having a deep knowledge of the buildings, unlike games like Ora where they are so essential that you are just lost. In caverna, you can take a couple of games and become slowly more acquainted with the buildings over time, still being able to enjoy yourself and feel you are executing a reasonable strategy for your first batch of games. I think players trying to comprehend all of the buildings on first play is why I am reading about 4-5 hour playtimes. No thank you. 

So, decent first impressions. The game is definitely not as thematically integrated to me as Agricola. But I still enjoyed it. 

One other thing. I would say that, including setup and breakdown and inter-round refills, that about 1/3 of your time is spent on fiddly maintenance stuff. It's annoying.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Many people feel that Caverna has replaced Agricola. Me? I don’t think so. Caverna is a splendid game and one of my favorites, but it’s simply just not as good as Agricola. There are multiple reasons for this (feeding is not as hard and intense, the setup is almost the same each game, the gameboard is too open etc).
Contrary to Agricola, a game in which you need a bit of everything in order to score a lot of points, it’s easier to diversify and follow different strategic paths (due to the various buildings, expeditions, rubies and the fact that almost everything scores 1 point each with no cap limit). I guess that’s one of the (few) advantages Caverna has on Agricola, and it’s the thing I like the best I guess; your farms look different from each other, whereas if you have a competitive farm in Agricola, they tend to be similar, although the way you built your farm might vary.

The expeditions are another thing I love about Caverna! The forged weapons you use to go on expeditions make family growth a lot less desirable. It’s no longer a must (as it is in Agricola) due to the fact that you can get a lot of actions with a few dwarves equipped with strong weapons and due to the fact that each dwarf only gives 1 point (which almost everything does in Caverna, which makes it easier to understand and calculate scores) of about 60-120 or so depending on the number of players. It’s also a good idea that you can block the family growth spot even though you aren’t going to get an extra worker; it adds some extra strategy to the game (and I like that later on in the game another family growth appears). It’s also nice that you now know when the first family growth appears, and in games with more players it’s even available from the start of the game!

One thing I dislike is that if you don’t go with armed dwarves, there is only one crappy action spot where you can build buildings – and the buildings are powerful! What is brilliant about Caverna is the fact that there is a spatial element; you need to carve out the cave in order to build additional rooms. I think that this is a very nice addition. It does seem, however, that some buildings are never ever built whereas others are bought in almost every single game… And that’s a little sad. Some of the yellow buildings are extremely powerful and if you don’t get a couple of them it’s hard to win.

And speaking of these buildings, they are the equivalent of the occupations and improvements in Agricola – but whereas Agricola has 10 cards which appear in every game and a gazillion of cards where just a few are drawn randomly each game, there are only 48 buildings in Caverna, and they all appear in every single game. This means that the variety and replayability is decreased and vastly inferior to Agricola, and that’s a huge bummer. I’m fairly sure that a couple of buildings haven’t been built in any of my 18 playthroughs… Yes, I know that they are situational and sometimes they might work, but I’d rather stick to build those buildings I know are great! And those yellow bonus buildings are so crucial, and with higher player counts they are highly contested. Oh, and I’ve never lost a game in which I’ve built the food chamber, and I’ve played against very experienced Agricola players. That building has given me more than 20 points a lot of times, and I’m pretty sure that I scored 40 points with it once as it’s so much easier to plant and sow in Caverna than in Agricola!

However, Caverna is way too forgiving and loose. It’s simply too open with especially 2 and 3 players (3 players even having 3 more spots than the 4p game?!), so it’s almost impossible to block your opponents – there are always a lot of good actions to choose from. In that sense Caverna feels more like Le Havre than Agricola. Besides the fact that there are too many open spots, there is not at all the same tension about feeding your family because it’s so easy to get food. Yes, there is a feeding phase after almost every round, but it’s so much easier to get food, and get a lot of it, so it doesn’t really hinder you too much. Maybe in the beginning of the game before your engine kicks in, but in the middle and at the end of the game it’s not too hard. And that’s sad, because the tension of the tight gameplay and feeding phase and the fact that some actions are highly contested are all reasons why I love Agricola and why it’s such an awesome game. 
So while Caverna has removed some of the features I like most about Agricola, it has also added some cool stuff (expeditions, spatial elements, streamline the point system). 

I think Caverna is best with 4 players, although 5 and 3 are fine too. As it takes about half an hour per player, I probably won’t try it with 6 or 7, although I would like to try it one day. Caverna is a great game (cf. my rating), but it doesn’t replace Agricola. Not by a long shot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Pretty much a sequel to Agricola. Same basic gameplay of worker placement, revealing actions, family growth, feeding your family, and breeding your animals. I really enjoy some new aspects such as 1) an easier scoring system that makes more sense 2) less points for family members so you are not punished for not getting to 5 actions 3) most actions are to do two things, which makes for more "getting stuff done" 4) the addition if rubies as a wildly flexible resource 5) more harvests which means more breeding/growing going on. Food is still tight, but while getting food you are also doing other things. The thing I like most though, is how you attach a weapon to an action disc and then the most powerful discs go last, creating a tension of hoping a juicy space stays open long enough. However, I do miss the cards and the fun combos, and prefer that over the public first-come one shot buildings that replace them. The rules for The new animals are also a bit fiddly, but the dogs watching the sheep make up for it. This makes a strong case for me to like it more than Agricola, but I'm not sure yet.

RATING: 6
I like it better than Agricola. In fact, this is my 2nd favorite Rosenberg game behind Ora et Labora.

I didn't care for Agricola. Too harsh, with the first player spot too important, and I never liked the cards. I found the game frustrating and did not like the rush at the end to homogenize and get a little of every resource.

Caverna basically addresses all my concerns. So why am I not more enthusiastic? Because if I'm going to play a game of this length, with this many pieces and of this complexity, I'd rather play Ora et Labora. In Ora, the placement of buildings is more interesting, the richer playground of resource conversion provides a better "sandbox," and I actually find Ora et Labora more thematic. I feel like I built and accomplished something in Ora. In Caverna, I feel like I simply followed a path that was available, if that makes sense.

Nevertheless, more plays may change my mind, and I'd happily play this again.

Update: played this with six players... ugh. Now, my group might be fairly slow playing, but four hours is not the way I want to invest my time in this game. I'm thinking four players max if I play this one again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Caverna certainly retains most of the same aspects of Agricola, but it adds a bit more to it by allowing you to conduct adventures with your workers to gain additional resources. This ultimately makes the game a little easier in terms of feeding your family since these extra small actions can help you get something without taking another action. It's also easier to convert just about any resource into food without needing an oven or extra action to do so as well. However, the game also opens the decision space giving you buildings to add to your cave instead of a hand of cards, which are available to all players from the start. Caverna is a solid game providing a slightly different spin than Agricola, but although while the game feels easier in terms of keeping your head above water each round, it also removes the turn 1 focus that the cards from Agricola give you making starting harder in my opinion. I will typically be happy to play this one, but don't ever see a need to own it given that I have Agricola.

RATING: 8
While the art and theme are beautiful, it doesn't quite capture the feel of the Harvest Moon series of videogames which I really hoped it would - as written. Saying that, however, the thematic implementation surpasses that of Agricola, and the sense of building a personally-tailored cave system/working farm is much higher.

Plenty of action options make it less aggressive than its predecessor. I would have perhaps liked to see less resources granted by secondary resource cards, but given the variety in those offered by player count, perhaps a few substitions will easily rectify this.

I believe it should always be played with an expanding, but randomly chosen, selection of room tiles, to increase gameplay variety and encourage very different strategies.

I would like to have seen some tiles that focused in the waterpools/river on the board, perhaps variable bad harvest events, some tiles that can *only* be built via expeditions/quests, starting board feature layout, and think it is a damned shame that mushroom farming in the caves themselves wasn't explored. Dogs too could have been given a slightly stronger role. But... always a chance some of these things might (hopefully) crop up in expansion plans! A substantial list, but all relatively minor, when you have a game as solid as it is.

RATING: 8
This is a really great game, but (for now) I have to give the edge to Agricola. The tightness of that game forces you to pay more attention to your opponents. Also, instead of the 50+ rooms available at the start of Caverna, Agricola gives each player 14 unique cards. The cards and rooms act very similarly giving you bonuses or upgrades for actions, and end game points for meeting requirements. Since the same ones are always available in Caverna, you can go for the same path each time. Whereas the limit in Agricola forces you to evaluate a totally different set of cards and try to figure out new combos and explore different paths to victory each time. I enjoy that puzzle a bit more.

Okay, I've got a few more plays down.  I'm feeling weirdly inspired to write a review.  

Notes towards a review:
This game is not Agricola and it's not like the other worker placement games, where the design space has been rigorously tightened to produce tension.  No, that design path perhaps saw its apotheosis in Vanautu. Caverna is nothing like that.

No, despite the player boards Caverna is an overt attempt to bring us back together.  It is a wonderful combination of pure and mixed strategies that offers the players opportunities for truly sadistic play. 

Of course, none of this will be apparent to the casual player where the game  will seem endless forgiving and open to nursing their poor position.  Such players will always loose.

RATING: 9
This is a great game for anyone who wants to build their own village and farm. You can choose a multitude of strategies to gain your points. The first game you will be lost for what to do, but after that you will know how to score points. You don't have to farm at all to feed your dwarves or score points, or you can devote almost exclusively to farming if that's what you want to do. Every game you can try a different strategy, or you can refine a strategy. It can take a long time both to set up and put away. The box says 30 minutes per player although it can take longer if someone suffers from analysis paralysis. Overall, a fantastic game. I would recommend this to anyone willing to devote at least two hours to a board game.

RATING: 9
After playing Caverna a few times, it's clear that it is a much "tighter" version of Agricola. The granularity has been reduced (meaning that you need fewer actions to achieve the same end). The early game is much less brutal than it is in Agricola, and I feel like the replacement of cards with buildings makes for a more balanced game. The mining is a nice addition to the agricultural aspect of Agricola. The only real complaint is that some aspects feel a bit more "gamey" than one might expect (especially rubies' role as wilds), but that's to be expected to some degree from a euro. All in all, a great game with a lot of ways to victory and much less of a point salad than you'd think.

RATING: 8
Update: Finally busted out my own copy and played again, after reading the rules for myself and getting everything straight. And ok, yeah, it's better than I first gave it credit for. But this is one of those rare games where you really should just start with the base game. Unlike something like Through The Ages, the full game in Caverna doesn't add any significant new concepts, it just adds more tiles that can be purchased. And looking at 20+ possible tiles that can be purchased, all from the get-go with no progression (like Le Havre) is simply overwhelming. I played my first game with the advanced version, the second game with the introductory version, and have no desire yet to go back to full version. Maybe after a few more plays.
---
1 play so far. Probably too early to rate, but I did it anyway. Played the full version without pre-reading what all the things you can build are. Wow, that's a wall of options. Unlike Agricola, where occupations an minor improvements are randomly selected each game, or something like Le Havre where a large selection of buildings are gradually introduced, in Caverna you have [b]fifty[/b] options of buildings to build, right form the get-go. Thud. That's the sound of most people's jaw hitting the floor. I was a bit surprised by how samey it was to Agricola, despite already hearing that from others. It's really Agricola 2, for better or worse. Although it's not strictly stuff added (e.g. adventuring, digging out your house before building in it), it's also stuff subtracted (room material type upgrades, building fences--in fact the layout of farms/fields matters now almost not at all, which is a bit odd, given that you still have to do it). Withholding final judgment until I do my homework and spend the time to learn all the buildings.

RATING: 4
Much more Agricola V2 than Le Havre or Ora & Labora ever can be. I regret to say that the game is wasted on me, as I'm not much fond of the parent game, and it can still all bog down in a remorseless fight for food and blocking actions before others can. In addition Caverna does away with the puzzle aspect of the cards which tests your ability to 'reprogram' the action cards while the game is in progress; instead simply offering you a bunch of action buildings and 'looting' resulting in free reign to [i]augment[/i] the core of the game. 'Agricola' is now much more a regular economic snowball game. I suppose that pleases many who pleaded with Uwe to create a game where you could actually do something with all your actions... but at the same time it also drives home rather painfully—for me, that is—that Caverna is the game some, and perhaps many, people actually wanted in the first place. I think that a little sad: we already have tons and tons of these engine games, and one of the most defining aspects of Agricola is also the first to be chucked out the window.

In any case, based on my fight with the system: If you didn't like the food fight and action blocking in Agricola then Caverna is also not for you. That much stays the same. If you didn't mind them, then you need to look elsewhere to find out whether Caverna is for you or not. Oh, and everyone can question the wisdom of the publisher to needlessly hike the price of the game to include material for 6 and 7 players when it is fully obvious that noone in their right mind would ever want to play the game at that number. But I suppose there are morons who insist that having a group of 7 is better than two groups of 4 and 3 and subsequently put Caverna out.

RATING: 10
Wspaniała gra, która pochłania na wiele godzin. Mnogość taktyk i możliwości sprawia, że gra absolutnie się nie nudzi. Jedna z niewielu gier zaawansowanych, w którą można grać nawet do 7 osób (co oczywiście wydłuża czas rozgrywki i wymaga duuużego stołu). Hodowanie zwierząt, ulepszanie jaskini, rozbudowa rodziny, uprawa roślin i szeroko pojęty rozwój gospodarstwa sprawia ogromną satysfakcję, nawet jeśli ostatecznie przegramy grę. Wtedy widać jak na dłoni, co możemy w przyszłych rozgrywkach zrobić lepiej. Praktycznie losowość jest tu zerowa i wszystko zależy od naszych decyzji i poczynań. Elementy są solidnie wykonane, ma się wrażenie, że jest ich nieskończona ilość! Jedyna wada to długi set-up, koniecznie trzeba zakupić/zrobić wypraskę i posegregować elementy (na szczęście szybko to zrobiłam), żeby jak najszybciej móc rozkładać tą wspaniałą grę. Jedna z moich ulubionych gier zdecydowanie, obowiązkowa pozycja dla fanów eurosucharów i śrubowania coraz lepszych wyników.

RATING: 9
[b]CoolStuffInc - Christmas 2014[/b]
Given the choice between this and Agricola, I'd probably choose this.  The game play is very similar to Agricola's but has one awesome difference: you can go on dwarf quests/adventures which gives you extra opportunities to build and get things.  There's only one way to get pumpkins and cows: exploring!  So much fun.  The only downside is that it is a bugger to set up, so many pieces.  But worth it!

[b]Expansions owned:[/b]
- [geekurl=/boardgameexpansion/166187/caverna-mini-expansion][i]Mini Expansion[/i][/geekurl]
- [geekurl=/boardgameexpansion/183531/caverna-water-expansion][i]Water Expansion[/i][/geekurl] - some fun new tiles that are built on the river, lake, or adjacent to the lake.  Fairy lake is fun - gives big points and an extra turn (but if spent is not worth any points).  Also adds bonuses to pumpkin harvests, etc... Very whimsical.

RATING: 1
Man, I loathe this game. It is right up there with Monopoly.

Why do people dislike this thing but rate it N/A? Look at the comments. Gee, post a rating to unskew the ratings.

I made the mistake of buying this and then playing another copy and realizing the mistake.  

This is from a reviewer about another game but it sums up this yet again rehash from this phone it in designer, "....epitome of inelegant, kitchen-sink designs that leave me feeling cold after hours of slogging through what feels like a meaningless point salad of mismatched mechanisms.

The worst is getting sucker in by reviews and the hype.

One of the most expensive regret buys I have ever made. Despite the publisher's price fixing in the U.S., artificial pricing, this thing is a nightmare to unload and try to recoup your cost. 

It is a niche buy. Those that wanted it, payed up, those that didn't, don't want it, what is left are those that expect to get a mint unplayed copy for $45, shipped, I kid you not. Back out after back out when they discovered the $18.00 shipping on this cinder block.

This behavior of publishing a game that "is not for everybody ", Mayfair words, posted here, in terms of that $98.99 manufactured price, not only causes it to be hard to sell, but also skews BGG's rating.  

It is like that Magic card collecting, Dice Masters. These people willing to spent a fortune on it, will vote their life style high up, not so much say for a game like Airlines Europe which who get a mixture of buyers aka a better sample of buyers, and a more balanced rating. 

Running a poll on 100K Lamborghini buyers on how they like their car is a moot point. They were in love with the designer, the car before going in, before the purchase.

Interestingly, same group that just loves this game are the ones that wanted an expansion to it 1 month after owning. What the hell is that all about? You read,"well if it had this or that", "I sure wished it had", to add some variation, yet 10 rating from these people. Read some of the glow job comments, I have never seen a game so loved by a group and yet that same group want so many changes to. A game that cost 2 cents less then $100.00 should have longer legs, not need expansions. 

Look at the N/A comments rating, amazing so many dislike this thing but fail to give it a rating so it would reflect the real rating. What is wrong with these people? 

Thoughts and just bad vibes after playing this, factoring the money for it:

I'm done watching Dice Tower reviews they are just too skewed to personal "like" of the reviewer, I'm done with this designer. There is a feeling of cranking out the reviews, there is a strong feeling on a one game played review, to keep up with the status quote. A problem I guess when this is now part of your income? I prefer more of a hobbies review.

I don't have the luxury of getting free games to review nor do we have those wax poetic FLGS to try before you buy. Our shops are more of the Comic Book Guy. So, I need a better review, with some real insight in the game. 

I really wonder how often this thing hits the table. To me it's a groaner, "do we have to play that". 

An amazingly dull, flat, repetitive game. There is no direction in this game. This is just a church groups activity, it is no game.

The real deal is you don't seem to be playing against other players, only yourself and trying to beat your easily obtained score by 1-2 points. Seems to be totally missed by any review of this thing. The lack of any real randomization just added to this besting your last score. 

As for Dice a Towers over blown love for this thing, why is it Egyptian themes, Mediterranean trading themes get no love, only because you have played more games and thrown them away then most people will ever own; you never grow tired of just another rethemed, get this or that, no real reason, now trade those in, that is all it is to it resource management; and yet again with this guy, this so called game; a piss poor, erroneous in real play, player count on the box; or you never tire of yet another small variant of Yahtzee game with another theme slapped on? 

I can't wait for the fixed priced $50, 2 player game only Caverna, if history repeats itself, created by pulling out some boring mechanism out of this penny a point salad from McDonald's $1.00 menu. The worst Euro cube pusher has nothing over Caverna's mindless, directionless game play. 

Totally amazing Dice Tower's loathing of Feld's games but the way over the top of this ilk of games especially from at his designer. It is like buying the same puzzle with a different theme for $100.

To Dice Tower, I'd rather play Shadow Era over Caverna. I would like to say I would like my $100 plus back, but I can't, sold for $65 dollars plus 2 $15 mini expansions, one for Caverna, one for another game  just go get rid of it. 

Yes, quite a lost. I figured I got $35.00 for it. You best know you want this thing given Mayfair Games business model of price flooring and slapping on any MSRP price they think they are entitled to.

I finally realize with this game Dice Tower reviews are basically infomercial and are nothing more.

RATING: 3
Decided to give it a run and see what the fuss is about.  A bit of a spoiled for choice type game, in my opinion.  It lacks the feeling of 'scarcity' that I know and love from Agricola.  

Despite having harvest near every round, I never felt the need to more towards harvest, or take specific actions to meet it.  This is because of the presence of so many easy 'trade for foods' and the wild card that is rubies relieving all tension of 'having enough' after things like oven's were effectively removed.  Yeah it isn't fun to give up resources, but the don't feel hard won.

Other than that , there is an unorthodox amount of tiles for this game, which makes it exceptionally fiddly.  Set up and tear down are absolute bears, which could be rectified with some custom story solutions (which would be a lot of work in this case).  

Overall, I do not feel this lives up to the hype at all.  I think the 'toy factor' due to the excess chroming (which eats my table) really detracts from this.  Not an experience I look to repeat.

Sign me up for another round of Agricola, I feel it is infinitely more enjoyable.  Firmly in my rather not play again pile.

RATING: 9
My only purchase from UK Games Expo and the highest BGG ranked game I own so my hopes were high. Initial thoughts from one 2 player play through. 
1 I set the game up to try a few rounds to get the feel of it and some understanding of the rules so that the game would flow better when we introduced the kids to it (they hate stop/start rule checking - but don't we all!}. Started with beginner layout for buildings. In the end we completed the game and the time flew. This was good as we got 'into' it.
2 KIV there are LOADS of bits - set up and take down are events in themselves.
3 Lisa was disappointed that the round cards are limited in number and the options available will not vary unless the number of players varies. We will have to see if this is problematic for us and if replayability is hampered by always having the same cards. 
4 I was afraid that it may have been too heavy a game for us however play was relatively intuitive. We have not played Agricola but after watching a couple of videos and the rule book we cracked on and I am confident we got most things right.
5 Our scores were pants 43 v 45

Judgement is reserved on the game - it will depend on subsequent plays and whether the enjoyment remains and indeed increases. It is certainly good but I really want it to be great not least due to the price point. Component quality is fantastic although I would have liked dwarf-like characters I could get emotionally attached to and that could hold the expedition shields rather than the round counters supplied. 

Update : further plays have revealed this to be a grower. Even in 2 player games trying to increase your own personal score (even if you lose) is rewarding. The variety comes with playing different player numbers which opens up different options. We have played with 2,3 and 4 so far and each feels different. We feel that there are still lots of strategies to try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Caverna is a masterpiece because it excels in so many aspects. No matter what kind of game you like to play, you will find something enjoyable about it.

Casual players: look at the gorgeous artwork, the vibrant colors and all the cute animeeples! You don't need to play to maximize points; just build the farm to your heart's content, bread lots of animals and mine the caves to get shiny gems. You will build a unique homestead every time, and it will always looks good. You will feel like an accomplished entrepreneur who turned an empty field and primitive cave into a cozy home and thriving farm/mine.

Competitive players: the number of choices to make is mind-boggling. You can focus on farming, animal husbandry, gem mining, questing or any combinations of them. Your opponents may try to block you, but you can always take another path. At the same time, there is a large collection of rooms, each of which provides a unique special ability. Building the right rooms at the right time can give you a significant boost, but which one is right is never obvious until the very end. The large variety and endless choices mean that you will not find a dominant strategy or a few set paths. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Minecraft: The Board Game. After one play, I can say that I like it better than Agricola, but that may just be because I've played Agricola so much that's it's getting stale. I like how this one adds a couple of interesting side games (forging weapons and expeditions as well as the "cavern" side of the player board to develop). The scoring is improved in that there are fewer negatives, so one doesn't have to focus so much on "a little of everything", (although that still seems to be a viable way to win). 

Another excellent Uwe Rosenberg game with wonderful components (although severely overproduced) and excellent scalability for different numbers of players. Why did they not include a player aid that covers end-game scoring?!

I don't think it's worth owning, given that I've already got Agricola with the Farmer's of the Moor expansion. This is very close to the same game as those two, and I feel that it would get stale after a few more plays just as Agricola did. 

Update: Now, 3 plays in (all 2-player), and I think it's as fun as Agricola. I traded for it, and I still own Agricola plus FotM. I'd like to play it with more players, but I fear that it would drag too long at the upper end with inexperienced players.

RATING: 8
If you've played Agricola, you've essentially played this game. The experience, the feel and the sorts of decisions you'll be making are more or less identical to Agricola.

But is it an improvement on Agricola? Not really, since my favorite feature of Agricola was the variation provided by the Occupation and Minor Improvement cards.

In Caverna, there is no such variation, but it's still a very playable and enjoyable game.

I've docked an additional half-point off my rating because I feel this game is really much too derivative. (And I really wonder if the essence of the new mechanics couldn't have been implemented simply as an Agricola expansion/variant.)

RATING: 10
In the first 10 days of owning this game, we played it 15 times.  We literally didn't take another game off the shelf.  

11/16/17 Update:  As we played it probably more than 50-100 times (sorry I didn't log them all!), I've realized a couple things:

1)  It becomes exceedingly obvious that it is very difficult to win with only 3 dwarves (opponent has to make mistakes) and it is impossible with 2.  Thus, there is always a race for people when I wish other strategies would be more viable.  My wife and I played with a house rule of starting with 3 dwarves and not being able to add another through the game for our last 10 games of this, and it opens up entirely different strategies.
2)  There are just some tiles in the game that are worthless and no one ever buys (except maybe a couple that new people buy that don't realize its a wasted value).  It makes me wish they'd rebalance those tiles to help even their value with the others.

It's still a 10 in my book as I will always be willing (and want to play) a game of this, be it 2 player or 6 player.  It's easily my favorite game I own of our collection of ~100, just still has its flaws.

RATING: N/A
Russell
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow, Agricola on steroids!  I really like it.  If you like Le Havre over Agricola, you will also prefer Caverna over Agricola.  Imagine Agricola without minor improvements or occupations, but instead it has 60 major improvements (instead of 10).  While that is still a lot of fun, I prefer how Agricola provides more variability with fewer cards...making each game different.  In Agricola, I only have to worry about the 10 major improvements plus my 14 cards.  Scanning over 60 potential purchases in Caverna is quite daunting.  I suppose an upside for some will be that everyone has access to the same cards. So for those who are averse to bad card draws or bad drafting in Agricola, this will be a positive to Caverna.  I do worry though once I memorize the buildings (equivalent to cards in Agricola), game will become static and played out.  Caverna will never reach 10 status for me...but could drop to 8 after many repeated plays if it gets played out quickly.  Some of the buildings are clearly better than others...just like with the cards in Agricola.  The difference is that in Caverna, will it always be the same buildings bought?

Ok, after 4 plays, there is no denying this is an awesome game...bumping to 10.  Is it as good as Agricola...maybe it misses by a hair....but it isn't far behind..and deserves a 10 too.

After 11 plays in, dropping this back down to a 9.  The static furnishings presents a "race" to certain buildings.  While still very good and interesting, I prefer the cards of Agricola to develop a strategy around.
