RATING: 6
Players manage a family of two dwarves, living in a cave in a mountain.  Players take actions to expand their living space, clear land, establish pastures for animals, sow fields with crops, mine ore and rubies out of the mountain, and so on.  The game is a worker placement game, placing dwarves to take actions, that expand your holdings and get an economy up and running.  The family can grow out to five dwarves, which must be fed regularly with food resources during the game.  Almost everything gathered is worth victory points at the end of the game (12 turns).

This is a reboot of the insanely popular Agricola from six or seven years ago.  A lot of the fiddly stuff from Agricola has been pruned out (the cooking, the cards, the wood/fences, the weird animal victory point ratios), and the game is better for it.  Expanding your holdings is faster now.  Dwarves can be armed with a weapon for the remainder of the game, allowing them to take the "expedition" action - a new feature - that rewards them with loot - basically free stuff that gets better and better as the weapons "level up".  Finally, there are mines and a new resource, rubies, than can be traded instantly for almost any other resource in the game, making powerful combo plays possible.

All in all, a glorious reimagining of Agricola, that now plays up to seven players.  A monster of a box, packed full of wood and cardboard.  Some concerns about down time as the Expeditions and associated loot selection can really bog the game down.  The game doesn't feel as tight as Agricola, with players constantly head-butting for the same contested actions.  Here, there appears to be more space to explore your strategy, and hence the game is slightly more forgiving.  Some will like that, others will miss the Agricola anguish.

After 8 plays, sold it on.  Agricola is a much better, tighter experience, and the cards set it above Caverna for me.

Being able to do so effectively is hard but highly satisfying.

RATING: 10

At the end of a game I would walk around and see what the other players had created and its almost like each player had their own art project very unique and different from any other player or any other game before. Many Agricola veterans may likely find Caverna too light, but it is more appealing for general gamers and with less stress and I feel it is more fun.  
I guess the only thing that keeps this from hitting the table more often is the long set-up and take-down/pack-up time and the relative large amount of table space required to lay out the game.

RATING: 9
Caverna is so reminiscent of Agricola that it is impossible to measure it as a game without reference to its Mothership. In many respects Caverna irons out the flaws of Agricola, which were twofold: (1) your farm rarely really develops into a farm until right near the end when a rush for fencing or ploughing usually ends the game; and (2) the benefit of family members is too strong: not only do they give you more actions but they count as 3 points each too. In Caverna family members are only worth a point, and the food requirements and food benefits are re-ordered to encourage a much earlier farm (or cave) development. In many respects it is like the All Creatures Big & Small 2 player game where it is so much easier to have families of animals grazing away early in the the game. 
Ultimately these changes, as well as the interesting dwarf hunting and mining options, make it feel different to Agricola and so there really is room for both. Perhaps the greatest overall difference is that the lack of cards in Caverna make the game a lot more balanced, in that you can't win simply by having a powerful card-combo. Instead all buildings are available to all people. I think it will be hard for this game to displace Agricola. I am not sure why, as on paper it could be seen a better game due to the differences outlined. Maybe it is simply familiarity, in that you tend to love what you know more. Or maybe it is that the variability in the cards and the tough early feeding regime of Agricola are the things that distinguish it so positively.

RATING: 9.44
If you liked Agricola you will like this one also.
From what I can see those who have some problems with Agricola (difficulty in managing choices and winning) tend to prefer this one, which is more forgiving and relaxed.

In Agricola you had your farmers, your little wooden house, which could be upgraded to bricks and then stone for extra points, and your fields, where most of the action happened.
Here you have more of the same, but with dwarves instead of farmers, plus a mountain to excavate your custom dwellers' rooms into, to unlock bonuses and powers.
And you can build weapons for your little dwarves and send them to adventures for some cool loot.

Full of stuff, tons of pieces around the table.

Totally worth it if you dig Uwe Rosemberg stuff.

RATING: 7.5
I wouldn't play it with more than 3 because it drags too long.  2 is my favorite and 3 is okay.

Fourth Take:

The game grew stale for me because of the lack of variable set-up.  In solution to this I found this very good variant that I believe has much improved the base game.

Random Selection + Goals Variant (Variable Setup) 
https://boardgamegeek.com/article/19258251#19258251

With this variable set-up, the game is always fresh and a different strategy is mandatory every game.  Until the expansion arrives, I will be playing Caverna this way from now on.  With this variant, I give the game a 9.

Third Take:

This game is just straight up sexier than Agricola.  I hesitate to use the word "sexy" to describe a board game.  I never thought I would.  But when I think of these two games and compare them this is the word that comes to mind - 
"Sexy."  Caverna is just that.  When you look at the player boards and see the cleverly devised cave system next to the field system; When you see how each player can specialize and truly take a different path to victory than the others - developing their respective cave system with ruby mines, ore mines and furnishing tiles or rather, focusing on the fields outside and animal husbandry - you begin to see the superiority of this game.  It is SEXIER.  Agricola is good - I own it and I will still play it.  Honestly, I like the fences in Agricola better for some reason - even though they are more fiddly.  The cards in Agricola - the way I see it is that their are cards in Caverna as well.  The only difference is every player has access to them and they are in the middle.  The game is still different every time because a) to win the game and get a higher score you strategy needs to be tweaked against experienced players b) your strategy needs to be able to change on the go as other players disturb your original intentions c) you are prevented from doing the same thing every time by the 1) the need to score higher and 2) the player interaction.  So GET OUT OF HERE all of you who feel that this game is samey!  You just have not given it a chance because you are in the "Cult of the Old."  Move on to a better game and embrace the "sexy" of Caverna!

Second Take:

The game is not lighter than Agricola.  It just has a different approach.  I see it as the idea that one graduates from Agricola to Caverna.  Unless you have mastered the occupations and improvements of Agricola, you won't fully appreciate Caverna.  I say this because Caverna is like Agricola in a sense.. except Agricola gives you 14 cards while Caverna lays all the cards out on the table.  Your strategies have to constantly change in Caverna due to the pool of tiles instead of private cards.  To get a higher score in Caverna your strategies need to constantly be tweaked and changed as well.  Don't believe those who say that this game doesn't have replay-ability.  I am hoping for an expansion that will include a variable set-up though.

First Take:

I love caverna.. it is a great game.  The only drawback is the lack of variable set-up.  The game doesn't change and thus the replayability goes down.  Although the game provides plenty of different strategies that will keep one modifying for years if wanted.  However, Agricola seems to have the upper hand in this category because of the cards.  This game - Caverna - is lighter and less deep than Agricola in this regard.  If your looking for an easier game than Agricola in this regard, than pick up Caverna.  I am going to keep this game for now.. but I am thinking about trading it for Agricola with the Farmers of the Moor.  If you are interested please let me know.

RATING: 7
A good successor to Agricola, which to me always felt a bit too stressful. In Agricola you must do a bit of everything, in Caverna there are so many options to choose from but not many of them punish you for ignoring them. There are a ton of specialized rooms to pick from to create a niche strategy each game, but it can be a bit overwhelming. Still a good game, but not one I want to pick off the shelf and play very often. Someone explain why this wasn't made for 4 players rather than up to 7? Madness.

90-120m, best with 2-4 players. 5+ plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After playing Ora et Labora and hearing about Caverna I kept wondering if Le Havre : Ora et Labora :: Agricola : Caverna.  For me, it's true.  Caverna would completely replace Agricola in my collection if I owned it.  The biggest thing for me is that there is just a lot of good options to take, even if you get blocked.  The expedition is perhaps the most interesting addition to the game.  Getting different resources from a single action space is not that interesting, but how you arm your dwarves, how they level and the fact that lower-level dwarves have to be placed before higher level ones, is really interesting.  It's also great that it's a lot easier to feed your family and the scoring differences mean you don't have to diversify if you don't want to.  

It's not all sunshine and lollipops though... I still don't like that people can have uneven number of workers.  They've partially addressed this in that it's easier to build rooms for extra people and there are more action spaces that will grow your family.  I like that there is a lot of buildings available, but I don't like how they're all available right from the get-go... it reminds me that I dislike a lot of front-loaded decisions like a set of 10 cards in dominion.  Even worse is the fact that there is no player-aid for people sitting further away from the buildings.  After the lovely resource wheel of OeL, it's tiresome to go back to filling all of the action spaces each round.  Speaking of resources, I find it weird that there are so many bloody components, but we almost ran out of stone and wood multiple times throughout the game and we only played a 4-player game.  Granted, we didn't use the multipliers but I think you would definitely need to when playing with more than 4.  This game is a beast to set up.  So, while I think Caverna is a solid title and an amzing improvement over Agricola, I think there will always be other Uwe games I prefer.

RATING: 8.8
I only played Agricola once so I can't really compare the two. What I can say is that I REALLY enjoy this game. The components are amazing. You can accomplish a lot during the game so I'm left feeling satisfied at how much I developed my farm and cave, even if I don't pull off the win. It seems like there is always a good move to take, even if the ideal move was taken by another player before you. Also, I think there are a lot of strategies to victory. The rule book is a bit lengthy, though. And it can take a while to set up the game and put it away because of how many unique pieces there are. To solve this last problem, I found some plans on BGG to make my own trays out of foam board, which work wonders! Another aspect that is a bit overwhelming is the number of furnishings available. I think this is a game that I will enjoy more and more as I become more familiar with the furnishings and don't have to spend so much time reading them over. The number of furnishings was probably the most difficult aspect of introducing the game to new players. I encouraged people to ignore them during the intro to the game and look over them when it wasn't their turn, which seemed to help. As with any game with a lot of choices, AP-prone people can freeze when trying to decide what to do.

RATING: 7
Caverna is not as good of a game as Agricola, but it is more fun. Building out your cave with unique rooms (and abilities), and weaponizing your dwarves to reap the spoils of adventure are great additions to the Agricola mold. However, the tense and unforgiving scarcity of Agricola is completely lost, and the absence of the occupations/improvements is noticeable. Caverna is a more friendly, more 'fantastic' version of Agricola, but I prefer the ruthlessness of the simple farm.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game is the solution to a problem I don’t have. The oft maligned cards of Agricola are removed here and a tableau of rooms are on offer for all players. Mostly, there’s just one copy of each though. The rooms confer in-game benefits or endgame VP bonuses and serve like the cards of ‘Gric but without the griping of who got dealt what.

Another problem that doesn’t need solving is addressed via the expedition mechanism whereby you can equip your workers to go hunting for everything and anything. You can get pretty much whatever you want by spending actions to train up your dwarves then send them out to collect whatever you want from a list of allowed items. 

The whole affair is fine and I’ll play on occasion, but it lacks the oomph of Agricola for me. Giving players a very tight worker placement space along with 14 cards and asking them to figure out what to do excites me much more than getting my dwarves up to a level where they can go shopping for whatever they need. I get that there’s tension in having players race to get certain rooms first – in fact, that IS the tension here – but I prefer deciding which cards to play and which not to.

RATING: 7

When compared to Agricola, Caverna seems a little more open and less constrained, and therefore lacks some of the tension of its older sib.  I also feel mixed about the open nature of the buildings. RATING: 6
This is less punishing than agricola as resources are more plentiful and each action space allows you to do up to two different things. I disliked this less than I thought I would. In fact I think I want to play again!

My experience with this game is somewhat soured by the fact our rules explainer, with three turns to go, dropped into conversation that you lose points for not filling your cave/farm and for not having all the animals, you know just like in agricola. Because everyone who plays games is familiar with agricola right? ... Well actually NO. Three of the five players at the table were not, and so this came as a bit of a shock and a mad scramble to fill said cave/farm to minimise the damage at the end of the game

The expeditions feels a bit like a win more mechanic as it allows you to break some of the fundamental rules such as both clearing a field, ploughing it, acquiring the seed and planting it in a single action whereas it would be two or three actions with out the expedition... expeditions are so powerful I am not convinced we were playing them correctly at all.

RATING: 6

Rating history: 10|10|10|8|8|8|8

Played 7 times.
2p - 2
3p - 1
4p - 2
5p - 2


MAY 2014: After playing a second time, I kept my rating at a 9, but now - a few days later - I'm upping it to a 10. I held it at 9 because I like Agricola better than Caverna, but that doesn't mean Caverna doesn't deserve a 10. After 20-30 plays, Caverna could drop to a 9 again (lack of variability with the buildings), but right now all the buildings are new to me and I'm having a blast.

May 24, 2014: It only took 4 plays for this game to feel both "same-y" and loose (lacking competitive tension). I thought this game would have at least 20 plays in it, but after 4 plays, I'm downgrading my rating from a 10 to an 8. I expect this will be an 8 for a while though. Not a very good two player game.

My initial impression of Caverna was that it was more fun than Agricola. On my fourth playing of Caverna, the fun is largely gone. Quests are still fun, and I like the visual presentation, but Agricola is a better and more variable strategy game.

Caverna looks fantastic, plays smoothly, and offers tons of options. These tons of options wowed me at first, but on my 4th playing, just feel loose. Agricola is excruciating at times, has a fairly boring "food engine" start-up, but then hums as the Occupation and Minor Improvement cards allow TONS of replayability.

June 2014: Another play, another 8 rating. I approached this game determined to appreciate the sandbox feel that it provides (something that Agricola does not do), and even with this new approach, it's not that interesting to me as a sandbox game. I want to give this game another 3 plays with RADICALLY different sandbox approaches just to see what happens when you stretch some of the boundaries.

AUG 2014: Played again, trying as much as possible to forget my Agricola training, and just play it sandbox style. Went mine strategy, Ore Storage for 15 VPs, rubies bought me necessities, and I won the game. And yet this still is only an 8 for me. Every game feels the same. It's a fun game, and I'll play it, but Agricola is still more fun for the starting hand of cards. No matter how I approach/play Caverna, I can't get more enjoyment out of it than an 8 rating.

7th play: 7. 4p - Milking Stool/yellow furnish. Starting to fade for me. The components are awesome, but every playing feels sort of the same to me now. If Agricola didn't exist, I'd have this at an 8 or 9, but Agricola does exist, and I'd rather play that. 08/14

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pros
-Same as Agricola but somewhat streamlined. Made easier to feed and get points appeal to many.
-New options such as exploring are available and add new winning strategies
-Geometry of farm/cave becomes more important
-Dual housing rules for most animals adds to flexibility
-Lots of game boards allows game to scale fairly well up to 7 players (but you may not want to do that due to downtime)
-Lots of meeple/chit in box (but somewhat high price)
-Can be played solo with very few rule changes (but the order buildings come up in are predetermined, so replayability is somewhat limited unless you choose to change that and just randomize them - not sure on balance).

Cons
-Took away cards from Agricola
-All buildings are available right away, takes away from inherent variability of game
-Feeding (some) and growth phases are predetermined
-Better than a lot of euro games, but still indirect competition

Note
-Rated very slightly higher than Agricola primarily due to own preference and experiences. I prefer the greater variety one can take to win, and I feel there is a greater quantity of options on any given turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It really is a lot like Agricola.

However:
* The scoring is much simpler and more streamlined
* The special buildings are open and available to be purchased by anyone
* The game isn't as constricting - there are alternative spots for actions, you can convert resources to food automatically, rubies are practically a wild resource, there are "mimic" spaces, and actions are very powerful (eg family growth is available from the start, plough-and-sow is available in the first round).
* Tile placements are much more free, but the spatial element is much more important.

I actually like the game better than Agricola because it's easier to comprehend.

RATING: 8
I’m not sure I like this over Agricola. More options is not necessarily a better experience. I like the tense decisions in Agricola based on scarcity of resources, which doesn’t seem to be the case here, as there are more ways to solve your problems. Still a good game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Caverna is a solid game - its close resemblance to its older brother Agricola already sees to that. When explaining the rules to someone who already knows Agricola I would guess that the time needed is reduced by about 50%.

So, what's different? I'd say there are two major changes. There are, of course, several others in the fine print as well, but these two stand out as game-defining, and they are the reason that Caverna is not just Agricola 2 with minor tweaks although it is still obviously in the same category of games.

The first change is primarily mechanical. The deck of cards with individual player abilities has been replaced by a pool of buildings akin to the major improvements in Agricola. This certainly removes some of the volatility from the cards which is nice while the obvious drawback is reduced variability from game to game. Whether that is a problem for replayability in the long term remains to be seen. It also means that there is a bit more frontloading happening when you're new to the game and need to get a grasp on all of the available buildings. All in all, I'm not yet sure whether this change results in a net positive or a net negative.

The other major change is mostly one of how the game feels. It is, overall, at lot less tight than Agricola. There are several changes that contribute to this, starting from more ways to choose certain actions, including easier procurement of food, and ending in a streamlined scoring system that allows quite a bit more specialization than Agricola did. I happen to like the additional pressure so this is not for the better.

Even though there are a few nice tweaks in Caverna I still prefer its older sibling. Obviously, that does not at all mean that Caverna is a bad game and, depending on where you stand regarding turn angst, it might even be the better one.

Great game with lots to do, though it's a little rough to only have one copy of most furnishing tiles.

RATING: 9
I was a little concerned that I was not going to enjoy this as much as Agricola. It read like it was an Agricola reimplementation (which it is) but far more bloated with a bunch of silly little rules plaguing the system. To my surprise, it flows just as well as Agricola, which is an amazing feat of design considering how much more to look at on the table in Caverna. Overall, I might even like this one slightly better than Agric as it doesn't rely on the random Occupation/Improvement cards going out.  (Though I'm always up for the challenge of luck in Agric.) Everyone has a chance to buy the special tiles that are available in the game. It also looks very impressive. There is not as much pressure to scrounge for food every round, which sometimes feels like the primary aim of my turns in Agricola if my engine isn't running efficiently. As such, you have room to stretch and try things out and make things work. Not much I can say here in the realm of badness. I wish so much that this was not made for 7 players. It must have bumped up the price of this game by about $20 and it is completely unnecessary for a base game.  But overall the quality of the game is excellent.

RATING: 5
Received from my Mum for my birthday 2014.

Rating based on one play. Played my first game of Caverna yesterday at London on Board. Lost 77-83. It made our brains hurt. Yes, it feels like turbo-injected Gric, but I fear that's only half because it's different and half because it's just more complicated. Not freewheeling so much as trackless. I would balk at teaching this game to anyone who wasn't already familiar with Agricola.

Here's why I prefer [thing=31260][/thing], in approximate order of importance:

1. Less intimidating for new players. OK, Agricola can still be pretty intimidating, but Caverna starts with 48 buildings face-up (or I think it's 25 in beginner mode) and you feel just clueless about what they all do and how they might interact. In Agricola, you're equally clueless about the cards, but there are a lot fewer of them - and fewer still if you play the family game.

2. I can hook in gamer-sceptics better with "we're farmers trying to survive..." than "we're dwarves..."

3. The cards you get at the start of Agricola are different every time, which has kept the replayability unnaturally high for me. I haven't played Caverna enough yet to get bored of the buildings, but they're the same every time so, well, I might...

4. Agricola is more portable.

Looking at those reasons I realise my opinion is largely shaped by the way I usually play games: often introducing new players and trying to hook them in, rather than intensely exploring games with expert players. People seem to like Caverna because there's less struggle to feed your family, so you can focus on different strategies - but for me the struggle for food in Agricola drives the game. Also, you get more bling in Caverna, but I think it's *too much* of a good thing. Agricola with animeeples is plenty pretty enough.

I wanted to give it more chances, but every time I had a chance to bring it to the table the thought of ALL THOSE BUILDINGS put me off. Sold in December 2014.

RATING: 8
This was a purchase based on how much I like Agricola and Le Havre, and people saying that it improves on many of the things that make Agricola a hard game to get to the table sometimes.

Now that I've played this a few times, I think I have a solid opinion on it.  I don't think it replaces Agricola really, as they are two different games.  The puzzle of getting your cards in hand in Agricola and figuring out your path isn't really present here.  That's not good or bad, it's just soooooo different I think.  I totally get the comparisons, but that's a pretty major part of Agricola, so despite so many similarities, they feel like different games to me.

My wife likes this a lot more, probably because of the lack of stress induced by feeding being so much easier in this one.

I'm pretty much in on anything from Uwe, and this is not different.

RATING: 9
The threat of starvation in Agricola is a source of stress for us, and the game ends right when we finally get the puzzle solved so we never really get to see our engine running smoothly, which robs us of a little satisfaction.  I'd like to try Caverna before buying to see if the lack of card variety is a dealbreaker despite the removal of the starvation threat.  Rahdo thinks Glass Road is better than this, and it plays faster, so we're going to give that a shot first.

UPDATE AFTER FIRST PLAY: OMG, so much more relaxing and enjoyable than Agricola.  It's so much more streamlined and straightforward.  I feel like I could actually teach this to people and that they'd enjoy playing.  This game is just plain fun.  Thank you Uwe Rosenberg.

UPDATE AFTER 2nd PLAY: I successfully taught this to 6 new gamers, ages from 11 to 45.  Everybody had fun.  This would not have happened with Agricola (on their first play).

UPDATE AFTER 5th PLAY: I can see the validity of the criticisms regarding the sameness of every game.  I'm not to the point where it bugs me yet, but I can already appreciate what Agricola did with the cards and variability between games more as time goes on.

RATING: 8.9
[ES] En esencia, Caverna es agrícola 2.0. Como su hermano mayor, Caverna es un juego de colocación de trabajadores y gestión de recurso. La mayor diferencia es que Caverna ha transformado las adquisiciones y oficios en habitaciones disponibles para todos los jugadores. Es una excelente solucionando para lo que para mi es uno de los  fallos mas grande de Agrícola: lo cuesta arriba que puede ser una partida si te tocan malas cartas. Caverna no enfatiza tanto el alimentar a la familia, dando así mas margen para las demás acciones.

[EN] In essence, Caverna is Agricola 2.0. Like its older brother, Caverna is a worker placement and resource management game. The biggest difference between the two are the rooms, caverna's version of improvements and occupations. Unlike Agricola, In caverna, all the rooms are available to all players, solving one of caverna's biggest flaws: Fighting an uphill battle only based on luck of the draw. Caverna also eased on the feeding your family pressure, allowing players to explore other actions and strategies.

RATING: 7.5
I'm not sure I see a reason to prefer this to Agricola. It's smoother in a lot of ways, to be sure, but between the slew of special buildings available at the outset (Caverna) and the cards (Agricola) I think I prefer the cards. They throttle information and choice, so as to prevent overload, and they also give a reason to pursue different approaches each game. Caverna already feels too samey. The flexibility afforded by rubies and expeditions are nice, but that might make them too dominant.
It's a great game, to be sure--but not as great as its forebear.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I'm not a big fan of Uwe Rosenberg's games (like Agricola and Le Havre).  There are too many options each turn, and you need to gather resources to do them, so you end up working on these long chains of actions which will take more than a turn... and then someone takes the action that you wanted, thus blocking it, so you have to recalculate a different path of actions (and even come up with a new strategy).  This can easily trigger Analysis Paralysis, and the game can just drag.

Caverna is a lot like Agricola, with family members as workers, various categories of resources, and ways to score points, but it seems a little less congested.  It's nice that everybody has access to build any of the buildings, rather than having random cards dealt out at the beginning.  But with all of those buildings, it can be overwhelming if you don't know what they all do (or if you forget to build one when it would really be helpful).

You don't have to have all 5 (or 6!) family members to win.  But beyond that, there's no magic theme -- you have to grind out a strategy, turn-by-turn, action-by-action, since your best-laid plans can be thwarted pretty easily.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Euro Game- Worker Placement

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
I just can't play this anymore. Every time I play this game I wish I was playing Agricola instead. Agricola's worker placement mechanism is dated at this point but the game has lasting playability due to the tightness of the game, the depth of the play and the variable starting situations. Caverna omits all of the above except for the dated worker placement mechanism.

Pros:

-It has a neat theme and the player boards look really nice as do the animeeples. The components are top-notch even though I've heard that an entire glen of elves were displaced due to the manufacturing of this game. That's dwarves for you.

-You can play 8 players right out of the box. Though that is a con for me as it drives the price up and I will never be playing this game with 8 players. But for some I'm sure it's a pro.

-There's plenty of room tiles to choose from so there are plenty of decisions in the game. Also it's really neat to build your own little cave and farm.

Cons:

-Setting up this game is annoying. There are so many single tiles that have to be placed in their correct spot on the board. And Caverna takes up a lot of table space. I don't know how you would even play an eight player game. People would need to sit so far away from the tiles that reading the small print might be impossible.

-Rubies. Everything can be solved with rubies! Hey what's that, did another player place their disk on the ruby space already? That's okay, just use the other one.

-There is no stress, there is no tension, there is no competition. It's people sitting around building a farm in a leisurely pace according to some rules. It's almost as much as a toy as it is a game.

-Every game starts the same so you can form a good working strategy and there's a good chance you'll be able to stick with it for the whole game at which point you are just following a predetermined set of moves. Sounds fun. Make sure to check the forums to follow another person's strategy so you don't even have to figure one out on your own.

Caverna is Agricola for babies.

5/10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Great game. Every game I find new ways to improve my strategies.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
I am not yet sold on this re-iteration of Agricola. While I do think that taking some of the great communal elements of its predecessors Le Havre, Farmers of the Moor, and All Creatures Big and Small, the tension might be worse off from it. While I was initially incredibly excited about the communal buildings (rather than secret and personal occupations and minor improvements), these buildings do not become as tense and fought over as in A:ACBaS. Rather, compared to both its farming younger siblings, the tension feels less present. I believe that this might be due to the game presenting more options and paths to accomplish the goals you set out to do. Furthermore, this then, unfortunately for me, becomes one of those games which you can set out with a strategy from the get-go which the tactical flow of the game has a lesser impact than selecting an appropriate point-generating machine (with the winner best implementing their point machine).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Probably the best of Agricola's siblings, at least so far. The biggest difference is probably that there are no cards... every one of the several dozen buildings is always available, so you're not constrained by the luck of the draw. The volume of options available seems overwhelming at first, but it may be a worthwhile tradeoff to avoid the arbitrariness of the card draw. In fact there is no chance here, with the minor exception of action cards coming out in different rounds, as in Agricola. The other big difference is expeditions. You can arm your workers and send them off to hunt for whatever stuff you decide, limited only by how well armed they are. The better weapons they have, the longer the list of spoils for you to choose from. This is a fun addition, and the rewards for it are well worth the effort.

I'm not sure this is a great game--to say that is derivative would be a massive understatement--yet I can't deny its quality. Will be playing again.

RATING: 8
Will often be compared to it's sister game Agricola.  The general mechanics of the two games are quite similar.  If you like Agricola you will like this one more than likely.

The biggest difference between the two are that all the special rooms are available to be bough by any player.  This makes the game completely wide open with no secret information.  Players will be forced to recognize and react to what opponents are doing.  Personally I like the card driven occupation/improvements more, however the majority of my game group likes this way more. Regardless, still a very fun game.

[b]SCENE:  A CAVE MOUTH AT DAWN.[/b]

An armored dwarf, Siegren, returns from an "expedition" with a sheep and pumpkin in tow.

[b]Glorthilda, dwarf-wife:[/b]  What a wonderful haul!  Wherever did you find such delightful things?

[b]Siegren, dwarf-husband:[/b]  [waving hand at indistinct horizon] Oh, you know, "out there."

[b]CUT TO:[/b]

[I]Agricola[/I] player board with a shattered farm, half-timbered house roiling with flame, fences stomped flat by the ghosts of stampeding livestock.  A disemboweled farmer lies in the mud, grunting and struggling to keep a pig away from his glistening organs.

[microbadge=29582]

[i]Caverna[/i] absolutely requires some kind of robust organizer just to make it baseline playable.  Setup and takedown are godawful chores even with the game properly sorted and bagged...  Luckily, such a thing exists, but this is the first game I've encountered where it's an absolute necessity.

UPDATE:  Up-rezzed to the Broken Token organizer, and man, is that thing ever [i]slick[/i].  Went together easy, a place for every thing, and it looks great!

RATING: 10
Nagyszerű játék, ami erőteljesen hasonlít az Agricolára. Persze így természetes is hogy ezt is jó játéknak tartom. Én az alábbi különbségeket láttam az Agricolához képest. 

Itt nem embereink, hanem törpéink vannak, akik a föld alatt a bányákban dolgoznak, a hegy melletti erdőben pedig állatokat tartanak, illetve ezen terülteteket szánthatják és vethetik be.

Nincsenek kis és nagy fejlesztések, hanem azok a különféle szobafajtákba lettek beépítve. Ezeket a hegy alatt lévő szobák helyére rakhatjuk le. Könnyebb fához jutni és kőhöz. Nincs benne agyag és nád, amelyek helyett vasércet és rubint bányászhatunk. Szerintem sokkal könnyebb etetni, mivel alapból átválthatók az állatok, a búzák, a zöldségek, a győzelmi pontok és a rubinok is kajára. Sűrűbben etetünk, viszont aratás és szaporodás is sűrűbb. 

Ugyanúgy mínusz pontok járnak a felhasználhatatlan területekért, mint az Agricolában, itt viszont a táblát könnyű lefedni, mert egyszerre két területet foglalunk el mind a bányában, mind kint az erdőben. Állatot is könnyebb tartani: szamarak lehetnek a bányákban, vaddisznók az erdőkben, birkák a mezőkön (kutyák őrzik őket). Marhákat csak karámba lehet tartani, de rubinért és kajáért bármikor vehető marha. 
A törpéinket harcos törpére is felfejleszthetjük a bányászott vasérc segítségével, ez azért jó, mert amikor megfelelő küldetésre küldjük, akkor a harcos törpök plusz akciókat hajthatnak végre. Változás, hogy két ételért olyan akciót is végrehajthatunk, amit más már elfoglalt. Ráadásul itt több olyan hely is van, ahol egyszerre két akciót is végrehajthatunk.

Ezek miatt könnyebbnek érzem mint az Agricolát. Hátránya, hogy drága (nem is kapható itthon, kint 65-75 euros játék), de tele van pakolva alkatrésszel. Sokkal vastagabb mint az Agricola doboza, de az alkatrészek teljesen kitöltik a dobozt! A set up része emiatt elég hosszú. Kicsit nyelvfüggő csak, könnyen játszható nyelvtudás nélkül is, mert csak a szobákon van szöveg, ami ki van rakva középre. 

Egyelőre 9-est adok neki, meglátom több játék után hogy fel megy-e az Agricola 10-es szintjére.

----

Játszottam az expert verzióval. Itt jóval több épület jön fel, de azért az újrajátszhatósága meg sem közelíti az Agricoláét.

RATING: 9
Like Agricola except the swim course isn't so badly infested with stinging jellyfish and deadly eddies. You can actually compete with other players to win the race rather than spend all your energy to stay afloat.

Caverna takes all the smooth mechanics of it's older sibling and trimmed the gruelling elements. Right length, interweaving mechanics, opportunity to interact with opponents, many ways to win and most of all, fun.

Regardless of the Agricola-Caverna debate which is a better game, the designer has done brilliantly well to craft 2 games via the same core mechanics that captures so different audiences.

RATING: 9
Agricola version 2. Gone is the random deal of cards of variable utility, gone is the ghastly 'must have a vegetable' moment, gone are the near identical farms at game end. Instead the Occupation and Upgrade cards are replaced by a large set of rooms that offer in-game abilities and end-game bonuses. Not everything is wonderful in this brave new world, however. The Guest Room and Office Room are insanely powerful and should probably be banned by sensible groups. There's a new Exploration feature that allows for weapon building and quests for plunder, but it's not essential to take part. This is very much the same game, but overall balance and opportunity for divergent strategies are massively improved. Indeed there's so much diversity that old time Agricola players can be caught out. It's essential to pick a strategy and run with it, doing 'a bit of everything' is a short route to losing.
Just a comment about production values: this is a lavishly produced game, but at £75 with online discount, I'm starting to get concerned that the price of Eurogames is moving beyond what can be justified even for game enthusiasts. The provision for 6/7 players is just silly and has inflated the component count for little gameplay return. Please, publishers, let's keep it real.

RATING: 8
I've only played this once (at time of writing) but I feel qualified to rate it a 10 based on that one game because I know it's father, Agricola, so well, and I feel like I can tell how it will play with future plays. And that is; well. Caverna has a plethora of interesting options to take, a variety of paths-to-victory (which base-set-only Agricola is kinda missing), and the same gorgeous components, tense gameplay, and tight strategy.

My only complaint is that the random harvests are exceptionally annoying. Since you more or less always have to plan for the worst possible outcome anyway, it's basically just occasionally, you randomly get some free resources. I know a lot of people dislike the overload of up-front building options in the full version, and while I can see that may be a problem for some, it isn't for me, I actually really like that.

I hope that expansions are forthcoming and that they give it more replay value, especially since there isn't such an obivous expansion route as Agricola.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Colocación de trabajadores ambientado en un clan de enanos que decide dedicarse a mejorar sus cuevas y dominios exteriores. Inevitable compararlo con Agricola. 

La premisa del juego es similar a su primo mayor: gestionas una familia de enanos que tiene acceso tanto a su caverna para obtener recursos de la minería así como a sus dominios exteriores de alrededor. Es el mismo sistema de juego que Agricola: un jugador elige una acción, la ejecuta y el siguiente elige cualquier otra y la ejecuta, revelándose más acciones conforme el juego avanza.

Uwe Rosemberg ha diseñado un gran juego tras años de feedback de los jugadores de Agricola. Añade nuevas mecánicas que parecen hacer el juego menos exigente con la alimentación de la familia y expanden las formas de ganar de su predecesor. Estas mecánicas son:

- La ausencia de cartas. La viarabilidad y los poderes especiales de oficios y adquisiciones menores se ven sustituidas por las habitaciones, que están al alcance de todos.

- Aventuras. Una mecánica que emplea la acción de forjar gracias a un nuevo recurso llamado mineral. Permite obtener mejores recursos conforme mejore la calidad del armamento.

- Reduce la importancia de la familia. Ahora es más sencillo alimentarla (no hace falta convertir el trigo en pan o cocinar los animales) y sus miembros otorgan menos PV´s que en Agricola (1 PV en vez de 3 PV´s).

- Al igual que en Ora et Labora, la situación de las habitaciones en el tablero personal, cobra su importancia.

- Hay un recurso que puede usarse como comodín (rubíes).

Si buscas un final más abierto, un juego más permisivo con los errores y con un sentido más claro de "construir algo", decántate por Caverna. Si buscas un juego más esstricto que recomepnse al más habilidoso, Agricola.

PROS: Menos punitivo. De entrada, menos exigente para novatos.

CONS: Partidas más largas. Mesa extremadamente grande para jugarlo a gusto. Montarlo (y desmontarlo) necesita más tiempo del que piensas.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Caverna is essentially the sequel to Agricola with a giant tile pool instead of a hand of cards. I'm less inclined to like it as much as the 'Gric. The player cards in Agricola were a plus for me rather than a detriment; and the splay of room tiles in Caverna is a ton of open information to soak in compared to the cards you're handed in 'Gric.

Still, Caverna does a lot of things that make me go "I wish Agricola thought of this." The collect resource actions that add more resources to their offer if they're empty is smart, knowing when the "get new worker" option is coming into play is nice, and I guess overall the game feels more streamlined.

There are differences enough between the two, at least in strategy (Adventure actions in Caverna alone make a huge change). Still, Agricola (especially with its expansion) wins my heart.

RATING: 8
In this game every player takes control of one tribe of dwarfs and compete against other tribes for being the most successful one, by improving its land, which is divided in 2 areas: cave and forest. The cave has to be opened by digging tunnels and setting up rooms or other different locations and the forest is destined for farming crops and animals. Each player has an action for every dwarf, starting with 3 and increasing the number up to 6.
Positive:
1. Very thematic
2. Many strategies can be followed
3. It is fun to play
4. Better than Agricola. Forget about Agricola, please
Negative:
1. It is not an easy game to explain
2. Not suitable for every type of player

RATING: 8
8.2 / 1-7 Best 4 / 30-120m / Overall 6 / Strategy 5 / 3.79
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
"Cave Madness" is what I dubbed this before playing and to me it sticks after my one game. Right off the hop, the biggest thing you notice and biggest difference from its father is the crazy myriad of options, really there are too many and I think Uwe may have slipped a little here as I don't see why he would not have scaled the amount of green buildings to the number of players. Having ~20 of them out in a 2 player game seems silly, to give you some idea there are already so many options in the game not only did I not buy a green building my first game I did not even look at them. 10 would be plenty and would cut down AP at least for first 10 games, it would also be interesting to see which come out game to game too, easy tweak at any rate, 10 for 2player, 12-14 for 3player.... (or maybe this is actually suggested in rules? edit apparently family version has less but not that many less and still not scaled to player #)

Anyway Gric (with drafting of course) is in my top 5 so this had a lot to live up too for me, and its unquestionable Gric 2.0 and Uwe made no attempts to conceal it as it is very very much the same game "mechanically". There are a good number of differences and reasons why you would also consider owning both however, and these are what really make the game interesting as well. Also on this topic, I should probably address the whole lack of tension and related comments as I think they have been somewhat overstated and maybe even incorrectly stated in some instances. Now probably THE biggest difference here from Gric is FLEXIBILITY as in Gric, everything is sooooo tight and this really ramps up angst and tension. Here, yes things are not as tight, feeding is more flexible (still not "easy"), there are potentially (edit see update) more strategic paths to take and flexibility in those paths, and more things up for grabs in the tactical grab game as well HOWEVER, you still want to do way more than you will ever be able to so while the "tension"' is somewhat lower, turn angst is still ever present and not "gone" and creates its own tension so don't be fooled. 
  
I think the biggest strength here is the vast amount of game to explore, I'm thinking a good 10 to 15 games just testing out the many strategic paths here, then many many more trying the hone those paths through all the flexibility you have within each one.  This alone I think will push me to fork over the $70 and force a spot on my overcrowded shelf for it. As I said Gric is a top 5 for me largely due to the incredible tightness, tension and impending doom you must fight to escape creating a real gaming challenge each play and leaving you with a true satisfaction when and if you've built a good farm. So while this mostly falls short of Gric in those departments, Uwe gave some of us the one thing we wondered if Gric was sort of missing, true strategic flexibility as this is much much more open in this department. 

I do wish they would have left this for 5 players; I am very unlikely to play with more, if ever, like many I imagine, so having less “stuff” for less money would have been better and leave the 6 and 7 player craziness for an expansion.  One other thing, while having the buildings face up is nice as you get to work towards them, there is the AP factor as mentioned but worse is the PIC (player induced chaos) factor.  You might have your eye on two of the yellow end game buildings, only to see them get snatched away 2 turns before you were going to build them, and they are big points and for this reason I would have liked to see a few more yellow and a few less green buildings, something we can tweak when the expansion comes out. 
 
Summary – Nice variant, feeding is a little easier due to flexibility, a little more flexible strategically, buildings are open to anyone to build so a screwage/chaos factor vs cards and here no cards at all.  

[u]UPDATE[/u]: 2nd play again with 2 players. Maybe not quite as flexible and open as I first thought, but a LOT more than Gric. The thing is, you just don’t have enough time, there are SO many harvests and you need food which is animals and veggies, and you need somewhere to PUT those things, and you want mines, and you want/need this_that_and_the_other_thing so damn, its tough, and very similar to the Father here, so much so, that my rating here reflects a whole point docked for being too much the same. 

2 players probably best for me due to the PIC I mentioned above, which is the biggest negative in the game to me (set-up/tear down nasty too). Think of the green buildings like minor improvements as it seems this is essentially what they are, trouble is now they are open to everyone; playing with 5 players but want that ruby building? Good luck, so do 2 other people. Same with the yellow buildings but worse as these are higher points. Now there is a fair amount to go around, and it could be argued that maybe you don't even need to build many buildings, or any, to win (likely jury out still on this). For me though at any rate, you have less control here than Gric on these important factors, and this is the other reason this is a whole rating point lower for me.  

=======================

Donkeeples and Dogeeples... 

Less theme, less tension and less cards. hmmmmm looks like steps in the wrong direction for me. 

Don't think there is room for this on my shelf, for people that found Gric stressful in a bad way, this might be for you. Need to play, if I had more room I might consider

RATING: 7.5
This is definitely spiritual successor to Agricola, but the changes it makes for me are definitely improvements. The two biggest things are that the special powers have moved from cards which are randomly distributed to a common offer of tokens, and that the scoring opportunities are loosened, so there's now more than one strategy. The common offer of buildings eliminates the possibility of someone just getting better cards. The scoring is changed in a couple ways, first of all things went from thresholds to 1-1 point opportunities, e.g. instead of vegetables only ever scoring you 4 points no matter how many you have you get 1 point per leftover vegetable. Also, a fair amount of the building tiles (that replaced the cards) are bonus scoring tiles, so you can cater your scoring opportunities to whatever you're doing. And crucially family members are only worth one point each at the end of the game, so they aren't as necessary to max out. It's also much easier to feed your people but I still feel like food doesn't do enough and so you feel punished for overproducing, though to a bit lesser of an extent than in Agricola. But really I just find myself enjoying the game when I play it, I appreciate the variety of things to do and the variety of ways to get things, and I think that the way I'm naturally inclined to play this game (just do what seems fun and make a cool farm) is much easier to do here than in Agricola. I think the fact that the theme comes through so well makes it hard for me to play these like efficiency strategy games and in this game you can do that and still do well.

Since there's no variable setup it is possible to just do the same thing every game, but I feel like that's sort of missing the point. People in my group purposely choose to do different things every game for fun, so that kind of self imposed variety works for us. But it might not work for everyone, and I can see the game getting stale fast if you let it (though I'd be inclined to argue that if that happens it's really on you).

UPDATE: I'm a little less excited by this game. I still think most of what I said above is true, but I'm just less interested in a game like this, where it's more about the narrative and the journey than it is about the mechanics. Granted, there are a couple of games like this that I enjoy, but the point is my favorites in this category give me what I consider more interesting narrative experiences. Also, I'm less enamored by worker placement unless it adds a unique twist and this is pretty standard worker placement. Still happy to play now and then, though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

But I think that the game play in Caverna is slightly better. In the end, I'm going to rate them the same and just say that I'm happy to play either any time.

RATING: 8
Comparing to Agricola:

Pros:

1) The feel and look of all the new pieces compared to just placing a card or fence.
2) No more luck of the draw as to who gets the best starting hand.
3) It's not as hard to feed, HOWEVER in order to get a high score there is still just as much importance in feeding efficiently.
4) The victory points system has been cleaned up, you still need to get one of each animal type, and have no free spaces, but otherwise you can specialize in whatever you want with no maximum points (you can have 16 animals in a double pasture with 2 stables now).
5) It is still an incredibly skillful game, despite it's differences to Agricola.

-----------

Cons:

1) Other than the order in which the round cards come out, the game has no other random setup (there is competition for buildings (each building is unique) on top of the normal worker placement denial to affect strategy though), also on top of who is going animals/crops you also need to pay attention to who is going expeditions so your strategy is not conflicting too much with others. But the game still requires human interaction to create a unique game each time rather than board setup.
2) Players do not start the game with their own unique circumstances (does not have to be cards) to give you an incentive to try a certain strategy.

Minor cons:

3) I'm not convinced on the random harvests, if you get all the normal harvests early on, growing your family can be a nightmare, and the alternative harvest markers can really mess with your strategy. (says the person complaining about no random setup)
4) I'm also not convinced on the expeditions, they don't feel quite right, but this is very very minor and I have no idea what I'd change about them.

-----------

In conclusion: The game is awesome, I just fear that once you've figured it out (especially in 2player) it will loose it's re-playability. From then on it will still be a lot of fun, but only playing 3-5 player with equally skilled players (the competition for buildings with enough players should make the game different every time). I have no interest in playing this 6-7player unless everyone is both a FAST player and knows all the buildings very well.

Saying all that, I have no doubt that I will get my money's worth out of this game, but it does not replace Agricola because of the first two cons (if they were fixed in an expansion, I would never play Agricola again). It is however better than most other euro's out there.

Update after 30 plays: 

Still a great game, but no longer a game that I want to play too often. Have gone back to Agricola which is still interesting every time even after 50+ plays. Still a keeper though! It needs an expansion which would need to include:
1) more buildings
2) variable setup (which would need well balanced rules, not just draw x number of random buildings of each colour)
3) unique player starting positions
4) something else to add another dimension to the game

RATING: 8.5
Plays easy with Agricola veterans. Tiles are varied and change the game mechanics. Learn the cost and function of the tiles for maximum effectiveness. RATING: 8.5
My kids have spoken... way better than Agricola!  They like that there are more options and the quests or "expeditions".

Hands down out of the box base game, better than Agricola base game. I can't compare it to the expansions.  However, I see that Caverna is ripe for expansions in the cavern room tiles.

First off, very similar to Agricola in gameplay.  Instead of many decks of cards to start your pre-game draft of Agricola, there are many cavern room tiles for bonuses that you can draft throughout the game.  The only tiles with multiple copies is the basic dwelling tiles, everything else is limited to only one kind which can supplement your strategy.

Second, way better components than Agricola and more of them.  Instead of simple wooden round tokens, you have nicely shaped tokens that are thematic to what they represent.  New are donkeys, dogs, ore, rubies, weapons, and coins.  

Third, it is not as punishing as Agricola, but allows players to choose more varied strategic paths to victory.  Agricola feels more limited and forces you down the same path as everyone else, whereas Caverna feels more freeing in that respect.

Fourth, expeditions are great.  Sometimes they feel like "cheating" because they help you work around other people taking your action or resource you need.  However, it's very well designed, so as to not be too overpowering and does cost you either in the amount you can get from loot or changing the order of your workers.

Awesome Game!!!

Even though this is a 3 hour game right now for my kids and I, they still enjoy it better than 3 hours of Sid Meier's of Civilization.

RATING: 5
2014-01-28

Initial rating: 8.5 (January 2014)

As expected, Caverna is a great GREAT game. It is very reminiscent of Agricola (of course) but when the game was explained to me, I was surprised how different it was. I was expecting a very similar game but Caverna has a lot of new things, enough add-ons that, for me, the game feels very different than Agricola and this is very positive in my book! It now comes with Dogs!! Love the dogs and I´m also happy that they don’t provide any food :D. I couldn´t eat my dog to avoid starvation anyway. I like the new scoring system, instead of having limits you always will win a VP if you have more vegetables, cows, donkeys, etc.

The game components are awesome, except for the disappointing start player token. It is out of place with such high quality components. The game box is big and is filled with bits. I also dig the new fantasy theme. Agricola`s was very boring.

On the bad side, I feel Caverna is a little less tense than Agricola, it has an imitation action space that (practically) guarantees that you will have the needed action available on your turn. Also, I’m not sure I would want to play with 6 or 7 players. Even with 5 it can be a long game. I’m open to try this exactly once to see how the games flows, with 7 players the game can be very tigh (I guess) but I feel the downtime would be excessive. Also I think Caverna has a lot less replayability than Agricola, The cards were replaced by tiles and over time the game can get samey as there are few tiles (compared with the amount of cards in Agricola). The expedition actions are very powerful, a player can feed his family with this kind of actions alone. For some reason, I don’t like this part of the game as much as the whole. I think if a player skip totally the expeditions, he will lose the game, making them a dominant strategy. 

In the night board there are some features that are hard to see.

Bottom line, Caverna is a great game. I think it is a bit better than Agricola, but it doesn’t replace the game for me, so I think I will have both of them in my collection :D. For now I’m only worried about the replayability factor in Caverna and I won’t say no to a game. Great game, I totally recommend it.

I enjoy Caverna but now I don’t think I will buy it.

RATING: 9
Elegant design. Not as complex as it seems. Not too hard to teach and once you understand the game a rich 2 player round can be played in less than a hour. Expeditions and mining add a fun adventuring component to the game that provide a level of theme and variety that was absent from Agricola (and why I was turned off by Agricola originally - running a farm just did not excite me). In Caverna, the task of managing your farm, cave, and workers with lots and lots of available choices for placing your workers and buying upgrades can be a real brain burner - but a fun one with lots to chew on.  Although not a problem for me, some folks have a valid criticism that by providing all tile upgrade options from the very start, players begin the game with an overwhelming amount of options rather than being able to chart a unique path based on strategy cards (i.e., Agricola). While true, it is simply up to the player to be creative and explore different paths to victor rather than the game nudging you in a certain direction. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I'm unsure whether I can rate Caverna fairly at this point.  I am erring on the low side; it's somewhere between an 8 and a 10.

There is more of a feeling of abundance in Caverna than in Agricola, a move toward the "resource playground" style of Le Havre and Ora et Labora.  Animals can convert to food without a cooking implement, expeditions provide infusions of resources, rubies serve as wild cards, to name a few examples.  Though this is still a game where the harvest matters in a way more stressful than in Le Havre or Ora; the food requirements will still take some effort to meet and can't be blown off with a well-timed loan.

I like the expeditions and the reduction in importance on family growth.  I wonder how much replayability there will be with the fixed set of furnishings.  Probably I will have an opinion on that after a couple more plays.

Edit 1/2014: Raising rating to 9.

RATING: 8
Caverna is Uwe's spiritual and mechanical successor to Agricola. In Caverna, the players' dwarfs not only build pastures, plant crops and build out their homes, they also hollow out their mountains and mine for ore and rubies as well as go on "expeditions" for additional resources and actions. The mechanics are about 80% the same as Agricola, making it feel like a variant game, but in saying that, Caverna very much stands on its own and many prefer it.

The main difference is that Caverna does away with the hand of minor improvements and occupations and instead puts the special abilities and bonuses on a display of tiles that players can build into their caves, first come first served. This removes the potential imbalance that one player's hand of cards has over another, but at the cost of decreasing variability of strategy from one play to the next. There are still certainly plenty of options - so this isn't a problem - it's strictly a matter of preference and is the key reason why some people like one game over the other. Personally, I love getting my hand of cards in Agricola and using them to shape my strategy for the game. The other big difference is the idea of using ore to arm your dwarfs and sending them on expeditions. An armed dwarf has a token on it that can start out at a value as high as 8 and eventually rise to as high as 14. Some actions allow an armed dwarf to get 1-4 items from a list that gets better as a dwarf's level gets higher. Dwarfs level up after every expedition they undertake. It's an interesting way of applying worker strength and gaining benefits by investing in it.

Caverna was published 5 years after Agricola and, while there are a lot of mechanical similarities, this is where Uwe starts to explore moving from tight interactions and lots of bumping elbows to having players make decisions based on an over-abundance of choices. His later games, Fields of Arle and A Feast for Odin (which I have yet to play), go even further in this direction. While I prefer his tighter games I don't dislike the more open decision space of these later ones. I find it cool that in the history of Uwe's games, there is a clear evolution of core ideas and a sense that Uwe is taking his audience with him on his explorations. He presents clear design choices that players can easily recognize and compare from one game to the next, which in turn enhances all of our understanding of design and contributes to our maturity as a gaming audience. In other words, I see his publications as a dialogue that continues to evolve and keeps me interested in how he will next shape the conversation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
On the one hand, Caverna is another version of Agricola making it easy to pick up for any player of Agricola. Game turns and decisions are similar. It fights for the same spot in terms of table time and complexity.
On the other hand, Caverna provides a different enough playing experience. It's less of an uphill battle of nourishing your family while trying to get something done. There's always something useful to do to scrape together a few VP's. There's more variation in strategies due to the available buildings for game end scoring. End results won't be as punishing as they can be in Agricola.
Is it sufficiently different to warrant owning both? Probably for lovers of Agricola and gamers not worrying about the size of their collection. It's a very good variation on an outstanding game. But I wouldn't mind just playing Agricola instead if Caverna wasn't available.
Finally, a few specific ponderings:
- Totally skipping expeditions seems difficult and doing a bit of them is probably a strategic must unless everybody else is doing them and focus can be shifted to less competed areas. 
- The introduction of a ruby-based economy raises the resource conversion game to a new level, possibly a level too high for my liking as there might be the chance for exploits.
- I don't want to experience the down time with six or seven players, even less the frustration if the player to your left consistently takes first player and six other players act before you get your first turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Feels like the system of Le Havre brought to the world of Agricola with a dwarf theme added for good measure. I really think the Cave Farmers is misleading, as it suggests no element of fantasy. Also, there is no farming done in the cave, but mining. All in all, I enjoyed my play and felt like many of the changes were improvements overall. But, as has been said before by many, there is little to no tension compared to Agricola. That tension probably makes this game more fun to play in person during game night, depending on your group. However, Agricola is so much more exciting. Especially that amazing iPad app. Caverna is one notch below it in my book. I also disliked how the rooms disappeared as the game carried on. Though I've only played once, I can imagine that the best players will be able to find an ideal strategy and exploit it. There is very little random in the game at all.

RATING: 9
After reading the rules, one gets an idea, that it is very similar to Agricola. However after playing it you start to think, that it contains less stress. It doesn't look that impossible to feed ones population. Moreover, there is no feeling that it ends prematurely right after everything starts running smoothly. On the other hand, it is definitely not easy and light. There are plenty of choice and you cannot concentrate one everything. However, if you don't have some mandatory things at the end of the game, you get negative points... To sum up, the players must constantly balance and choose between "a little bit of everything" and "a lot of something single". And then there are other players... You have to choose an action not too late. On the other hand, how can you do that, when you need everything. It is a deep game, which includes a lot of planning, however it doesn't cause too much unneeded tension.

Pagal taisykles, žaidimas panašus į Agricola. Kita vertus, žaidžiant išryškėja, kad streso jame yra mažiau. Žmonių maitinimas nesukelia tiek galvos skausmo. Be to, nėra to jausmo, kad kai tik pradeda viskas veikti, žaidimas ima ir pasibaigia. Kita vertus, jis tikrai nėra paprastas ir lengvas. Pasirinkimo daug, į viską koncentruotis negali. Tačiau jeigu neturi tam tikrų būtinų elementų, žaidimo gale gauni neigiamų taškų... Žodžiu, vyksta nuolatinė kova ir balansavimas tarp "visko po truputį" ir "kažko vieno daug". Ir dar kiti žaidėjai... Reikia spėti pasirinkti veiksmą ne per vėlai. Bet kaip tai padaryti, kai pradžioje iš esmės visko reikia. Žodžiu, gilus žaidimas, reikalaujantis planavimo, bet be bereikalingos perteklinės įtampos.

RATING: 9
I like this game alot. Its basically a slightly more simplified and friendlier Agricola. Visually its very attractive.

That said though, its not a "Agricola Killer" as some make it out it be, although it is very good. I personally prefer Agricola for the drafting of cards at the start and for the increased pressure and planning that Agricola demands of you.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun game which is much better than its predecessor Agricola.  Caverna eliminates Agricola's card drafting phase and instead provides many different buildings that are available to be bought by anyone right from the start of the game.  The harshness of feeding one's family has been tamed and the list of mandatory things one must complete each game to avoid highly detrimental penalties is much less confining.  These differences allow the player more freedom to choose their own path to victory which makes the game less stressful and more enjoyable.

The huge shoe box containing ten pounds of pieces makes set up rather involved and tear down too.  Also it's an expensive game.

This is a game where you'll be lost the first few times you play.  The more time you spend playing it the better you'll understand all the options and possibilities available.  Differences between each players experience level is going to have a large impact on how well one scores in the game.

Fun to play but probably not a buy.  After all, do I really need another long complicated game that does this resource conversion exercise that ideally requires me to spend time playing it and learning it?  I already have Le Havre and Ora et Labora - do I need another?  Besides, I may be getting a little tired of it.  If I'm going to invest multiple plays into a game, I think I'd prefer something else.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Still, 8 points is a lot. I would not hesitate to play Caverna. But Agricola with some of the base cards set is just more fun for me.

RATING: 8.3
I didn't want to play this.  But the company was congenial, and time was on hand.  So I agreed.  And as we played, I felt all desire to ever play Agricola ebb away.

I won't describe the game, others have done so better than I will.  My hesitations around this game include:

size - this is a sprawling game with lots of components.

duration - this is a game that will take longer to play than Agricola.

acceptance - all my usual opponents are burnt out on Agricola.  If I buy it will we play it?

Two weeks later... Ordered

I've now played 6 times.  This is a very enjoyable game.  I like it in the same way I enjoy the late double actions in Agricola.  The game feels rewarding, as you really do feel like you are progressing.  In comparison, Agricola often feels like it is nearly impossible to progress.  So Caverna feels easier.

RATING: 10
:star::star::star::star::star::star::star::star::star::star: (30 plays May 2014)
This is in my opinion "Agricola 2" as the game play is nearly the same. Although it is still as exciting as Agricola, after a number of games, I started seeing my plays tend to stay in the same approach more or less. However, it is still possible to try completely different way of scoring and that makes this game very fun to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A quite pleasant family game. My experience is based on a 2-player game. 

For a 2-player game Caverna has far too many options, not in the sense that it becomes complex, because it isn't. I haven't played Agricola, but would anyway say that Caverna is a quite straight forward easy game. The problem with the amount of options is that it becomes an activity with little interaction, something that probably improves in a 4-player game, even though I doubt it dramatically changes the game.

I went into the game assuming that not feeding your dwarfs is a bad thing, with sever consequences. Not quite true, since my opponent, who have played the game several times, chose to accept several turns of starving dwarfs to gain huge extra points for something else. His tactic proved successful, even though having piled up several minus tokens. I refuse to say the game is flawed, but the theme becomes irrelevant.

Caverna is a nice activity of figuring out and testing a huge number of combinations. Hence I will probably enjoy several more plays. If I'm looking for a gaming experience I will however choose something else.

RATING: 7.9
Fun game, and an interesting variant on Agricola. However, I prefer Agricola still. With Caverna, I have to keep in mind 48 different cavern rooms plus a couple of dozen different actions (many offering multiple choices). That's too many different variables all at once for my taste. In Agricola, if you use the decks, you'll have 14 cards in hand plus 10 major improvements plus a slightly smaller and simpler action board.

Agricola's decks offer more replayability, too, as well as shaking up your strategy each time.

On the other hand, Rosenberg has made the game components a bit more efficient and clear. That, plus Caverna's less punishing mechanisms, make Caverna a good choice for those who find Agricola too dense and too tense.

RATING: 8.5
Caverna is, as many people have called it, Agricola 2.0 and for good reasons. It fixes many issues with Agricola, the most glaring being the food scarcity. Additions to Caverna also made the game more palatable such as the dwarf quests, the better selection of enhancement cards and the rubies/coal aspect. While the game play is nearly Identical to Agricola, there are some minor changes. The game will take a solid 2 hours for 3 people. 20 minutes simply being the setup. 

I knocked it a half point simply for the sheer volume of the game. Im guessing it weighs about 10lbs in the box and there are so many pieces you are bound to drop some or lose some no matter how hard you try. 

Overall I would recommend this game ONLY if you have played Agricola a few times to get the style of playing down, then move onto Caverna.

RATING: 8.5
Caverna: The Cave Farmers is a worker-placement game at heart, with a focus on farming. In the game, you are the bearded leader of a small dwarf family that lives in a little cave in the mountains. You begin the game with a farmer and his spouse, and each member of the farming family represents an action that the player can take each turn. Together, you cultivate the forest in front of your cave and dig deeper into the mountain. You furnish the caves as dwellings for your offspring as well as working spaces for small enterprises.

RATING: 6.8
Uwe "Frankenstein" Rosenberg's monster is, just as I expected, a perfectly well-developed, complex game with even more colorful ways, many strategies to win... and too little to make me want to explore it more.
First of all, this is Agricola meets the buildings (available for everyone from the beginning) from Agricola: All Creatures Big and Small with the addition that this time you can upgrade... wait for it... your workers, to 14 different levels - the more the levels, the more different kinds of actions you can choose from to take with them using certain action spaces. And there are rubies, rubies that would be called 'gold' in other games as they are just kind of wild resources and can be used also more than a dozen ways (mostly overlapping with those of a level 14 worker).
This is what the game offers and if I felt I didn't get enough novelty in Ora et Labora, I really feel it now. for a game that takes one hour to explain and 2 to 4 hours to play I need more novelty or more fun to feel that the time and effort spent was worth it. I can play Agricola: All Creatures Big and Small 5 or more times during one play of Caverna and I would rather do that. Still, of course it's a game perfectly suited for very high BGG rankings; I just feel I don't like most of the changes much.
+: the game is colorful
-: the theme makes less sense here
-: rubies (1 to x extra actions to be chosen of about 20) and dwarves (1 to 4 actions to be chosen of about the same) make downtime and eventually the game itself longer
-: strategic paths seem to be a lot more scripted, forced (yes, I know, it's just an opinion after one play)
-: as food is less scarce because there are millions of ways to make food, tension is also lower
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This is really a remake of Agricola+FotM but streamlined and more balanced.  I like that there are no role cards (many of which were broken in Agricola anyway) and that it's friendlier to a diversity of strategy (you can win without family growth being a main focus).  It's still somewhat long and involved, so it can be a bit frustrating with the improved balance sometimes making for fluky fringe wins.  Always having the same buildings available for building is sort of a downer, and having to read/know all those buildings is rather annoying (which is a problem shared by Le Havre and Ora Labora).  Overall a great game which I would say is only slightly more playable than Agricola.

RATING: 8
This version of Agricola is a bit more open allowing for more specialization and probably even pushes you that direction.  Food doesn't feel quite as tight despite having to feed quite often in the game and the 7 unknown harvest/feeding chits can be rather frustrating when they come up in the wrong order for you...;-)  

Instead of cards being used to provide variation, the rooms provide unique actions and abilities of which most there are only one of each except for the base dwelling.  Adventuring is an interesting addition to the game as well which allows a person to go off and pillage stuff which is often better as you improve your weaponry.  Frankly, this is the part of the game that really gets me interested in playing as it is really unique from the other "Agricolas".  

It does not feel nearly as vicious as the original, but I find it quite interesting and enjoyable.  I don't believe I would still ever play with more than 5...and possibly not more than 4 just to avoid downtime.  

Four player makes for a much tighter game than three player thus far in my brief experience.  Imitation was used far more often in the four player game.

Kas ma ehitan siia rubiini kaevanduse, laoruumi või hoopis magamistoa? Põlluharimine, loomakasvatus või seiklused? Eeslid,lambad,koerad või sead? Ja nüüd on mul järsku jälle suur tuhin Cavernat mängida.
