RATING: 9
A large but tight box of beautiful components. Scaling it up to 7 players was unnecessary as the game would take too long to finish and number of components becomes larger. A first time player will be overwhelmed by the number of options. Grasping the plethora of actions and rooms that extend your cave requires a few completed games. The nice thing is that you can score points by concentrating on only a few things and do not have to study all the buildings in your first game. The game requires a custom insert to make setup bearable. Despite the negative aspects Caverna delivers fun 2 hours of exploring, farming and raising animals in a sandbox. The game is a solitaire as player interaction is limited to blocking actions from competitors. This does not feel mean in any sense. The most enjoyment in this game comes from building engines and feeling proud of your fields and farm animals. End game scoring has not been made the culmination point.

RATING: 8
Similar feeling as Agricola:
- Quick turns (1 action)
- Similar breeding, feeding, harvesting, begging,…
- Relatively simple rules (essentially the same as Agricola's)

Missing compared to Agricola:

- Variety brought about by the cards. They let you be somewhat creative, let you be "special" in a way that you have never experienced or seen before, in a way that may not be available in many of your future games.

Added compared to Agricola:

- The ability to specialize, thanks to buyable end-of-game bonuses (specialize in ore, etc.). Now, this makes the game also closer to many other euro games (like Terra Mystica, maybe), so this is also a negative. There is always the risk that you specialization is not as good as some other one, but you need to play the game many times before discovering it (unless it is well balanced, and execution is what is rewarded, more than choice).

- The ability to be able to keep building something, thanks to more actions being available, doing a wider variety of things: Expeditions, converting woods to meadows, digging tunnels, getting dogs for guarding the sheep, etc.

* Overall: a nice game, that I would be happy to own, but that I don't feel the need to, because I have Agricola. Also, I really like the "simplicity" of Agricola: having to have a bit of everything makes part of the strategy "obvious", which leaves less chance to luck, compared to a game where some basic strategies must be taken, despite the fact that they can be quite invisible to new players (like taking many improvements in Caverna, or whatever in Puerto Rico).

RATING: 7
Agricola in the Stone Age This stand-alone edition of Agricola takes place in prehistoric times. You chop out caves and furnish them, while you chop down trees and keep animals. In the meantime you collect all kinds of raw materials and gems to be able to fund your actions. Choose each turn an action with your cavemen and develop your caves into luxury living space and make your garden self-sufficient. Collect rubies to perform special actions and keep donkeys in the caves Dogs keep your flock under control. Do you have the most valuable cave at the end of the game ?! Agricola players will encounter familiar and new game mechanisms, but especially the experience of Caverna is fundamentally different. Because there are far fewer playing cards in the game, the advanced version of Caverna is also more accessible for beginning players. nice if you are a real rosenberg fan

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love this game.  Amazing worker-placement, and I can get most anyone to play it.  Very thematic, tangible, spacial, everyone likes building out their mine and farm.  I could go on and on but everyone else's reviews already shows that it's amazing.

The downside that most don't mention is that it is huge.  Even if you're playing for 1-2 players, it takes up a full kitchen table basically.  And I mean FULL.  So there is a lot of reaching around, and it takes a while just to get all the pieces ordered well.  At least all the pieces are in different bags but I don't like pulling pieces out of bags.  I'm going to get some plano boxes to make that part a lot better.

The other big problem is that the game is just long.  Longer than what it says.  And if you are trying to teach a game to someone who doesn't catch on quick and thinks about their turn alot--so help you God.  I had a 6 hour game because of one such person.  This game takes 1-2 hrs per person.  I don't think I'll ever get it down to 1 hr per person, but I imagine some people might.  

With that being said, if you're planning on it taking that long and you set aside a Saturday for it, then it's not necessarily bad.  What I'm saying is, it doesn't feel like a game that "outstays its welcome."  Maybe it does just a little bit...but it is quite an epic game, and no part of it feels wasted, like "why do we have to do this stage of the game?"  

In short: don't play more than a 3 player game if one person is a noob or ap.

I guess player interaction could be better.  It feels like you are playing a solitaire game with your personal board.  But there is player interaction with what actions are taken...then other people can't take them.  So I would rate the player interaction as moderately good.

洞穴農夫
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very relaxing iteration of Agricola that is very enjoyable, satisfying and beg to be played more and more.

Its certainly "wider" than Agricola yet a bit "shallower".
Its far less unforgiving and hence allows the players to enjoy the game in a rather fresh and breezy fashion.

Whether or not you win or lose, the game itself would be very satisfying at the end when you look at your accomplishment of a farm.

I really like this game much more than Agricola.

The one point that i think this game could suffer is the fact that all the room tiles are laid bare before all. This would allow the game to be "fair" without some people knowing all the cards and combo by reading and studying them before the game. However, such non-random approach to the game could lead to the game being more of a "multiple solitaire" game and limit replayability. 

I supposed its not a serious issue that some smart variants and house rules cannot fix.

Another thing i really love about the game is that while the game can be a real brain-burner, it doesn't feel like that while you are playing the game. It feels fresh, breezy and relaxing throughout. The somewhat soften sense of urgency surely is to be credited for the phenomena.

RATING: 7
I would say I enjoy this more than Agricola but not as much a Le Havre which is for me the best in this series.  The components are gorgeous and production value is top notch.  However, this game did not evoke much of a thematic experience for me as I was hoping.  For example, all the animals are essentially the same except dogs can watch over sheep and donkeys can work in the mines.  I would rather it be more thematic with being able to focus on different animals to produce goods.  The game forces you to focus on a bit of everything rather than specializing.  What if I just really wanted to be a sheep herder and be the best damn sheep herder ever?  Nope, still going to get negative points and only 2 or 3 buildings to really support this play-style.  Someone who diversifies will most likely out score you.

I like games where I can have a specialize in a certain aspect of the game and follow a specific strategy.  Agricola actually lends more to that playstyle with the cards you start with.  In Caverna, everyone starts out essentially the same and everyone is encourage to do the same stuff.

For most of the game I didn't care what anyone else was doing as long as they didn't take my spot or didn't buy my buildings. We were all doing pretty much the same stuff so it didn't really matter.

RATING: 9
Before playing Caverna, I viewed it as being another Agricola clone. However, after playing the game twice, my perspective changed. In fact, I added it to my wish list and put Agricola on my trade list.

In many ways, Caverna is more forgiving and approachable than Agricola. I liked that. It also got rid of the random distribution of cards, which in Agricola can heavily tilt the game toward a player if they got great occupation cards or cards than synergized very well. In place, there are now room tiles that are available to all players from the very beginning of the game.

RATING: 8.25
+Mini Expansion
+Water Expansion
+Christmas Tree PnP

An excellent advance on Agricola, replacing occupations and minor improvements with a set collection of rooms.  This game seems more forgiving than Agricola and with the mining appeals more thematically to my family.  The art and design of the pieces and board are superb and worth the investment.  

Adventures/quests seem less thematic and have continued a non-spatial action in Uwe Rosenberg's work.  I'm not quite sure what I think of that.  Every action usually has a spatial representation:  build fences builds fences.  But the questing takes the worker/player "off the board" in a way that the original "collect any resource" kind of action from Agricola did not.  At least in that situation you could imagine, as the iOS port of Agricola does, that there is a market stall or basket of goods for you to collect and then put in your supply.  

Our kids have come to call adventures "shopping" and I think the flavor text in the rulebook that describes what a dwarf does on each quest, for instance, finding an abandoned baby animal, fits very well with the game itself.  

But something's not quite right for me.

Edit:  many geeks worry about re-playability.  I don't share that worry, though I'm not sure why.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Caverna has an intimidatingly heavy box, lengthy rulebook, and number of components, but the actual game is involved without being complex, surprisingly easy to learn and teach, and intuitive to play. I love it.

It's full of charming little touches, like being able to eat your dog but not getting any food from it, dogs wandering around the farm, the Cuddle Room for snuggling with sheep (or "snuggling," if you like), "wishing" for children, and just the whole notion of happy dwarves farming, mining, furnishing their caverns, and going on little adventures. It's a fantastic mishmash.

I can't imagine trying to play this without the Broken Token inserts. They make it a breeze to play straight out of the trays, and tear down right back into the trays, and transform a huge heap of components into pure delight. And those components! The little vegemeeples and animals are adorable. And that rulebook! Huge, but easy to navigate, full of examples, and a joy to reference in play.

The game is meaty without being dense. Needing to feed your family is stressful, although I suspect another play or two will make it second nature, but nothing else in the game has that edge. It's pleasant without being bland or boring. And the meat is excellent, with many paths to victory, every action being useful, a limited number of turns, and no possible way to do it all.

After just one play, I don't think I'd need to reference the rulebook more than occasionally during a second play. Caverna is sublime.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I have a lot of games so that is a tough order to fill.

RATING: 8.5
So I finally got to play Caverna! How was it? REALLY good! I've now played Agricola, Le Havre and Caverna, and the latter is far and away the best. That says a lot, because I like Le Havre quite a bit. 

More interesting though is the argument as to whether you should play/buy Agricola or Caverna. No question--you should find a way to get your hands on Caverna. Different strokes, I guess, but I almost feel that Caverna is *unquestionably* the better game. It takes everything good about Agricola (theme, interesting choices, excellent game design, etc. and adds interaction (tiles), more options (adventuring) and less restrictions on end-game bonuses. 

Overall: An excellent game that works really well with 2

*FIMO miniatures

RATING: 8.5
I prefer Agricola but this is one of my favorite heavy games too.
Caverna is less stressful (feeding-wise) and more open, but way more complex version of Agricola. It's deep but not dry, bit like Farmers of Moor 2.0 that completely gets rid of cards (Fotm had possibility to play with Improvements, which I personally haven't even tried yet(!)). Dwarves make the game more light-hearted. Takes ages to setup and is a pain to learn even if you've played Agricola, but I guess on some level it's worth it, yes. This is a game I probably won't play more than once a year (if even that!) but will probably enjoy it every time. But I must say that this game might also be the end of my Uwe Rosenberg fandom. I like the way he brought actual stories into heavier, victory point hoarding games, but if this complexity level is the direction he's going to, I won't follow. 

2-Player Comment:
* Like most Uwe games, Caverna scales well and is a great 2p game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is far and away my favorite game!  When you open the box it can be a bit overwhelming, but once you play your first game you will be hooked.  There is deep strategy, a great theme, world building, and a million options to consider on each turn.  

Each player starts with a game board where half is a forest and half is a cave.  Both sides are unexplored and undeveloped.  You then take turns sending your workers out to perform tasks that allow you to turn your forest and cave into farmlands, mines, or rooms.  Each choice you make slowly develops your board and opens new options for creativity.  By the end of the game you have pastures full of animals, mines with donkeys hauling ore, and bedrooms full of more workers to send out.  Players compete for tasks, as each task can only be chosen by one worker per round (some exceptions exist).  So if you wanted to build a pasture, but your friend got there first, too bad!  But there are a ton of things to do, and strategies to try, so it's fun to experiment and change things as you go.  At the end, each player tallies up their scores based on their personal development, and a winner is chosen.  I can't recommend this game enough.  It takes some time to learn, and it is certainly not a fast game, but if you enjoy worker placement games and have 2-4 hours free, it's a great choice.  

Honestly, just typing this right now makes me want to leave work and play this game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is a solid (also solidly heavy, with tons of wood) update to Agricola. The game is not very different from Agricola, and if you know how to play that, you'll basically know how to play this. I feel like the designer made real improvements and addressed my three chief complaints with the original game, resulting in one of the best hardcore strategy board games I've played.

Problem 1: Intimidatingly complex. I like complex games, but sometimes I didn't want to play Agricola because I just wasn't in the mood to think _that_ hard. Caverna isn't quite so taxing, and I think that's a good thing. It means it will hit the table much more often.

Problem 2: One path to victory. I felt like Agricola had one main path to victory, which was firing on all cylinders and achieving everything perfectly. Caverna has more flexibility and allows you to pursue options that your opponents are not also pursuing.

Problem 3: Requiring perfection. In Agricola, if you made one wrong move, you could be screwed in the later game. The existence of rubies and a more lenient trading schema means that in Caverna, there's some room to learn while you're playing and still be successful.

RATING: 1
I find Agricola mediocre and bland.  I like fantasy themes, and the idea of replacing the random cards with tiles seemed like an improvement to me.

Unfortunately I hate this game far more than Agricola. The artwork on the components does not evoke the feeling of being a dwarf clan. The art feels even more boring than Agricola because at least Agricola had pictures on the cards instead of little boring tiles with furniture on them. While playing the game there are no pictures of dwarves on anything. The rulebook has some buried in there, but all you look at while playing is boring farms, caves, and furniture.  

Aesthetics aren't everything, maybe the gameplay would be worthwhile. No, I hate the gameplay. It is a mess. There are exceptions to rules everywhere. Donkeys in mines, dogs with sheep, and on and on. Couple that with the billion options for what to do every turn and it's overwhelming. I can't hold all that in my head properly while also playing the game against other players. There is going to come a time when somebody wins because nobody got in their way when they where building some combo that everyone else wasn't paying attention to. Having to figure out your best move and take into account the other players is just too large a game space for my brain to comfortably hold.

The thing that put the nail in the coffin for me was the Spare Parts, Trader, and Seam infinite combo. There is an official "fix", but it is inelegant and clunky. There is no reminder that you can't have Trader and Spare Parts except buried in the appendix. So, if I haven't played the game in a while and/or a new playing is playing you could easily end up in a situation where that combo gets rediscovered only to have to say "oh, sorry, you can't do that" and fuck the whole game session up.

In short, bloated convoluted unthematic mess with a band-aided hole.

RATING: 7.1
I had my first and, so far, only play of this new ‘hotness’ with six players (one of who was instructing) at Cancon.  I quite enjoyed the game and found it to be pretty good for what it was. The obvious comparison here is with Ag (both are designed by Uwe) and it is pretty similar but with enough difference for it to be able to stand on its own as its own game.

The premise of the game is that you are dwarfs who have access to both a cavern to mine for resources as well as the great outdoors to cultivate fields and raise livestock. The same system of play as Agricola is in effect as once an action is chosen by one player on other player can choose that action; more possible actions are revealed as play progresses. The difference is that there are two ‘duplicate action space’ where for a two or four food a player can choose to take an already chosen action. The difference here is the possibility for arming your dwarfs and sending them on missions (also on player actions) where they can obtain a variety of resources depending on the type and duration of the mission(s) as well as the quality of weapons they wield. These missions are risk-free in that the adventurer will always return home safely at their completion.

The familiar tension between expansion and being able to feed your dwarfs exists here as well as the requirement for rooms for them to be house in. There is also something of a ‘Soviet style’ mentality to expanding in the cavern in that most of the sorts of rooms one can build are severely limited. For example, there is only one ‘double room” (that can house two additional family members); there are also a limited amount of spaces allowing family growth. I can imagine the local area Commissar forbidding construction of a double room as the region has already met the allowance dictated by the central party and vetoing nay reproductive activity for the same reason.

That aside, C is not a bad little game. One that I would play again but possibly not one that I would buy as it’s too similar to A, which we have with most if not all expansions, for me to be able to justify the purchase. Further play may change my mind and if circumstances were right I would indeed play it again with little hesitation.

RATING: 8
Initial impressions after one play is that I prefer Caverna to Agricola mainly because it just feels more fun and less stress than Agricola. Caverna is more fiddly in terms of the number of rules and options available, though. Like Le Havre, Caverna also feels more of a sandbox than a gruelling efficiency race for the same objective than Agricola. However, in Le Havre there are really just two dominant strategies - shipping or building that both rely on getting steel production up and running as quickly and efficiently as possible. Caverna, at least initially, feels like the scoring options are far more open and therefore a player's strategy seems a bit less dependent on their opponents actions. 

The adventuring rule in Caverna is an interesting innovation but in the end it seems to mainly be just a means for just getting more resources. If there's any agony in Caverna its in the decisions over developing an emergent strategy from the many options available.

Overall, I'd happily play Caverna again. Its a very good game. I still prefer Le Havre because that game offers more player interaction through needing to visit other player's buildings. Like Agricola and Le Havre, be prepared to play for at least 30 minutes per player.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
My dislike of the painful feeding and randomness of power card elements of Agricola has driven me towards Caverna. So when offered the chance to play it, I jumped at a 3 player game. The set up does take a while (think Hawaii and then quadruple it!). The power cards are replaced by building tiles - all of which are visible. Your dwarves can either mine or farm OR they can go exploring. I concentrated on mining and exploring which meant that I had little food generation - you would have thought that I would see what was coming next……. there is still some feeding required BUT (and this I really like) you can use your strategy to deal with it. I had a building which enabled dwarves to eat minerals that I had mined! My exploration quests could return with food and animals…. SO there are ways out of the dead-ends of Agricola.
For runtime reasons (30 mins per player) I would not want to play with more than 4 players.

RATING: N/A
- Full access to all the upgrades turns this into Analysis-paralysis nightmare. 
- At the same time, it negatively impacts the replay-ability in the long-run. Very little prevents you from going for the same strategy every time.
- Ruthless for the new players. Even for those who played Agricola. The game assumes one will play it multiple times, before starting to enjoy it.
- With the exception of tile timings, it's a complete information game. It's basically chess, except a more convoluted one.
- Too many wildcards for a worker placement. Not many options to actually deny other players.
- Stressful and at the same time long.

+ Credit has to be given for the way the mechanisms are implemented and the amount of play-testing which obviously went into this.
+ Texts on the upgrades and such are simple to understand and don't seem fiddly as was sometimes the case in Agricola.
+ Building your own dwarf settlement is fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Designed by Uwe Rosenberg, this game is very similar to Agricola. You start with two dwarves and place them on spaces to take actions, such as building, gathering grain and vegetables, rubies. Unlike Agricola, there are no cards to choose from, the buildings are all on display for anyone to build. Ore is a resource that can be used to arm your dwarves. Armed dwarves can go adventuring, and take rewards (for instance, gaining cows or other resources). New animals, dogs and donkeys are introduced, and you can also build mines on your display. 

It's a big box game, with many pieces.

There are a lot of things going on, but it is fun. It's similar to Agricola, but the feeding is a bit simpler. There are no ovens and such, you simply convert goods to food. I had fun and look forward to more plays. The sweet spot for the game is probably around 4 players, otherwise it will be too long between turns. 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I often played the family game of Agricola and love the way the furnishing set-up in this game is similar. It makes it much more accessible for the crowd I played a lot of Agricola Family Game with.

Also delighted that the 4-player "family growth queue", loved by some but for me the main flaw in Agricola at that player count, has been removed.

Really keen to play this some more and get to know it better.

RATING: N/A
czekam na PL / Lacerta
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I posted in the Agric and Caverna forums a while debating this but finally took the plunge when I could get this at a good price of $60.44. In my opinion after 3.5 games, this is better than Agricola; Caverna is like a deluxe Agricola! The only possible advantage Agricola has is the variety of cards for replayability, but I personally don't like the cards much since they overload me and are hard to maintain personally.

Here's an example of a possible first turn in 3P Agricola and its comparison in 3P Caverna.

Agicola:
1) Get 1 grain
2) Plow 1 field
VS.
Caverna:
1) Plow a field and get a grain and get 2 food.
2) Plow a 2nd field and sow up the grain.

I like the Caverna version more because each single action will give you multiple things, which lets you build up your farm more. That feels better to me and is more fun to boot. There are still difficult decisions to make like in Agricola, but I never saw much the "fun" of Agricola's feeding stress. The furnishings also feel more thematic to me than the 14 cards in Agricola since you actually place the furnishing into your cavern.

Even though Caverna is 12 rounds versus Agricola's 14, I like that you can do more per action. Every tile is double, so even though Caverna's board is about twice Agricola's, you can fill it up much faster. Your board fills up pretty much all the way and you feel good. You're not forced to do Family Growth to stay competitive, which leaves you open to farming rubies, ore, stone, dwellings, bonus rooms/mines/dwellings/etc, sheep, boar, cattle, donkeys, dogs, veggies, grains, or any combination of the above.

Though there might a marginal bit more maintenance every round compared to Agricola, its still about as quick since its a lot easier to grab the wood, wheat, and other pieces since they're thicker and not round disks.

The lack of the 14 cards hands obviously hurts replayability, but in exchange, you have 24/48 tiles available to build and make your strategy. Admittedly, its actually less tiles per player, but in exchange, you get a more fair situation without lopsided cards. There's also no early pre-game time to draft and pass out the cards, so some extra time is saved there. I have only played 4 games at this point, but I suspect that playing with some variants on the tiles and such will increase this game's replayability (since only the harvest tiles and order of round cards are the things that change sadly). I'm not crazy about using variants, but I do generally agree that the game may get a little old around 15-20 ish plays or so, at least until the expansion comes out.

Despite all the positives said though, I still see some possible improvements to Caverna in my eyes, but I still rate this above Agricola and look forward to more plays. Here are some ways I think it could've been improved still though.

Caverna possible improvements:

- scoring cheat sheet and round order of what comes up cheat card, in addition to the cheat cards of how a harvest works, what a ruby converts to, and the loot charts that are given out.
- instead of alternative artwork of dark caves, put asymmetric backs like in Eclipse or Cuba for variety. I dislike that a lot of tile placements are similar, placing tiles in the direction of food and animals. Having a space to hold resources on your board like in Agricola would be nice too.

Considering trading since lack of variety in setup and also large tablespace and setup time with 48 tiles
- I wish they could've put the singular (1 column) cardboard thing as a piece of the other ones. This is my own nitpick since I like having everything the same size and symmetric. They could also put a bit of text instead of the parenthesis restocking () for when a resource is at 0.
- setup is quite the chore - I suppose a better storage solution or a way to manage the resources better would have helped. Minimizing chits like the harvest tokens would have been nice too.
- I kinda wish they had alternate cards like boar/cattle cards too that you could mix in every so often for different rounds. It would increase the variety.
- I wish they could've added some playtested variants, like rotating the tiles or having extra cards to sub in.
- wish the weapon tiles were Level 1&2 front and back, 3&4 etc, which would make it easier to manage. That may also make it confusing if you flip one by accident, but that should not affect the game too, too much. An alternative is using D20s as the dwarves, though you would need about 35 D20s then and would have to be careful about tipping them over.
- wish the table footprint wasn't as large. Given how much space it needs, I suspect 7 or maybe even 6 would be too much to fully enjoy the game. To see all the pieces on the table and the tiles would be quite the pain. Maybe if they had a cheatsheet of all the Basic Tiles and all the Full Tiles, that'd be nice. I kinda wish we didn't need the tile setup boards too. Anything to empty the table and box size a bit more, like not having 7P material. (I'll probably use the 7P one day I think, but I still would've preferred just having up to 6). In my opinion, 1-5P is manageable and you can keep track of and enjoy the game with everyone. At 6P+ people should break into two teams of 3P+ or more.
- wish the rulebook had an index, though I wish this for most rulebooks
- wish they could make the arrow stockpiling actions all in a single row, so we can see and do them in sequence of say, all woods then all stones, etc etc. For the later flipped cards, maybe make the arrows bold too to see more clearly.
- the colors of yellow/white players can be a little harder to discern on the surface of this board's color scheme, so different colors may've been better. Same with cattle and wood being the same color.
- more tiles!

Anyway, despite what it may seem, I say these nitpicks of mine because I'm picky and because I actually like the game so much, so there's tiny ways I think it could've been even more awesome. I still quite like it as it is. The components are easy to pick up and move (unlike Agricola's disks), and there's more stuff to do between turns while waiting for others: decide where to place tile, get loot, etc.

Agricola's major advantage is the variable setup/different options for each game. (I don't count the feeling of struggle/tightness as much of an advantage) Overall, this is also the only avenue I'm concerned with, but I definitely see Caverna playing out with a lot of variability still.

Overall, awesome game where you build a cave/farm and the production is great and gameplay and decision spaces are solid. Better than Agricola in my opinion, though Agricola is more replayable admittedly (its only advantage IMO). I think the only true weakness of this game is its relative lack of variation, for which an expansion or official variants would be needed; all my other gripes with it are relatively minor/inconsequential given what it does better.

Further thoughts at: https://boardgamegeek.com/thread/1338982/quick-first-impressions-caverna-positive-review-wi

RATING: 9
Possibly the best game by a great designer (Uwe Rosenberg has affirmed that Caverna is his best game, someone thinks it is just advertisement, I think it can just be simply true!).

It is very similar to Agricola, but so much better in so many ways. And I think I like it even more than Ora et Labora, that was my favorite of Uwe's games until I tried Caverna.

Agricola was more influenced by cards. Here everything is available to everyone from the beginning, but in a very limited quantity. And I agree with a post here on BGG, the better players know this game, the more it will be a real fight for the most valuable things. So much interaction, a lot of nice tension. And very personalized playing is possible. There are many paths to go (I think even more than in Ora et Labora that I loved for this). Strategy is more important than tactics (as in the other two games mentioned, maybe even more).

It is very thematic and realistic (as Agricola, maybe even  more): this makes the game more intuitive to play than Ora et Labora where the effects of buildings were quite weird (in Caverna there are very few things that sound a bit strange, most of the time names of items are easily connected to real things in the dwarfs settings). And the theme is nice also for children at the point that I have never before seen my daughter as excited to play a game as her first game of Caverna. 

One (tiny) thing that I am missing is the rondel for goods that was offered in Ora et Labora that reduced the bookkeeping (but I do see that probably in this case it would have been impossible to adopt it without losing some of the flexibility in goods grow). 

Caverna is great game in itself, it doesn't need comparison with its predecessors: not only it is better than Uwe's other games, but there are very few games, if any, between the many that I have played in my life, that I would prefer to it.  Eventually the most important thing is not the many good attributes of the game, but something that can only be appreciated by playing: this game is incredibly beautiful to play, satisfying, engaging. Gaming experience at his best. It should belong to any gamer's collection, both if already owning other Uwe's games or not. 

Masterpiece. Very recommended. 

UPDATE November 2014
I like Caverna still a lot, but I prefer Fields of Arle. Caverna is still a 10, but Arle has taken its place in my top ten.

UPDATE October 2015
Rating moved from 10 to 9. Although I still consider it  a great game, I think that I would prefer Fields of Arle to it, any time. And this should be reflected in my ratings.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Is it better than Agricola? No.

I'm not going to under-score the game as an Agricola fanboy, because it's a perfectly good honing of Agricola's central premise and mechanics, and the box is just one big treasure-trove of gamers' crack. But there are a lot of flaws:

1) Flabbiness. Too many spaces, too much to do, too little focus.

2) Focussed game-end scoring. People say the Jack-of-all-Trades scoring is a flaw of Agricola, but it is a totally necessary part of the game when you think about it for more than ten minutes. You have to be given clear and many objectives in a game like this. It's nice to be able to formulate your own in Caverna, but this also leads to a lack of competition and dynamic interaction.

3) Prescriptive building. Any belief that there is more freedom to develop your board in Caverna is an illusion. You actually have significantly less freedom than in Agricola because your board will develop the way the game dictates.

4) The hierarchical worker-placement means you can't plan round-to-round strategies.

5) The random harvest tokens feel like a fudge.

I think Ora et Labora did all this better.

RATING: 9
En voilà une belle surprise que Caverna! Je pense qu'il est assez difficile d'accès, dans le sens où même si l'on est amateur de boardgames, on ne va pas forcément se diriger vers lui au premier abord. Et ce serait bien dommage de passer à côté d'une telle pépite! (jeu de mot facile :p) Ce que l'on remarque tout d'abord c'est le volume et la qualité du matériel. Les illustrations bien que basiques sont bien colorées et agréables à l'oeil. Les règles peuvent paraître complexes au premier abord mais une fois que l'on a compris la logique, tout va très vite. Plus on avance dans la partie, plus on a de possibilités et plus on calcule dans tous les sens pour coiffer au poteau nos adversaires. Un mode solo est également au rendez vous pour les fanas du scoring. La partie peut être un peu longue mais elle passe vite tellement on ne s'ennuie pas. Les icones que l'on retrouvent dans le jeu sont bien explicites, rendant le jeu encore plus accessible et agréable.
Je ne suis pas "adapte" de ce style de jeu "kubenbois" sans dé, sans aléatoire, mais j'avoue que j'ai été bluffé par les mécaniques et l'intérêt du jeu.
Un très bon jeu à posséder :-)

RATING: 8
[b]Caverna[/b] was traded away for At The Gates of Loyang, Ninjato and Carcassonne South Seas (4/30/14).

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]Wow was I excited to get this game. I actually played it before Agricola and thought it was pretty good. Then I played Agricola and realized how much more I enjoyed the card play and difficulty found in Agricola. In the end I did not like the fact that at the beginning of the game every building is available, you are never forced to try a different strategy based on you having limited access to buildings (which is the case with the occupation and minor improvement cards in Agricola). 

I'll mention this again later, but I definitely prefer worker placement games that are more punishing and force you to make difficult choices (which has really prevented me from just buying The Fields of Arle, I want to play it first). I don't think Caverna is a bad game by any stretch, but it is my least favorite Rosenberg game. 

I agree with many that you probably don't need both games in your collection, but Agricola is the game for me. I would play Caverna again, but I won't seek it out.

Having now played Agricola, I much prefer Agricola and do not regret this trade at all. I much prefer Uwe's more punishing games.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

*When I originally wrote this I hadn't played Agricola yet.

[i] I like Caverna, but I prefer Glass Road and for a lighter game Agricola ACBaS. I can't really explain it, it is fun to play but I do not feel the same sense of accomplishment at the end of the game that I get from Glass Road. In my opinion I feel like Glass Road is getting pushed to the side because it came out at the same time as Caverna. I much prefer the card play and resource management found in Glass Road. Maybe Caverna just feels like other WP games I have already played, albeit with a cool theme/wrapper and really nice components.

Will be interested to see how this game fares a year from now. Overall a solid game, but I am not on the hype wagon, go try Glass Road instead![/i]

RATING: 8
I prefer Agricola but this one is more suitable for a different type of player - a little gentler. Should you own both? I say that's fine, they play differently enough and they are both works of art. Rating may rise after more plays (2 so far).  Robert Florence summed it up well: "Where Agricola is a knife fight, Caverna is a choose-you-own-adventure farming/mining/development game."

Also get some component boxes for all the bits, consider it an adventure.

RATING: N/A
Like it's predecessor Agricola, this game is a lovely choice for Thanksgiving and maybe even more fun to play with all of it's plethora of choices you have every turn.
Who wouldn't enjoy Thanksgiving with the Dwarves?
After all they are hearty eaters.

In Caverna your Dwarf can become an adventurer. Provide for his/her family by means of adventuring and mining. Or he/she could go the old fashioned route and farm veggies and raise animals. Or why not a little of both...so many choices and so many resources to play around with.

There are no cards to choose from and create interesting game strategies in Caverna. But the wealth of choices and options open to you every turn really make this game fun!

RATING: 9.5
心得:
目前最貴桌遊阿
比農家樂規則多但是輕一點
不用看卡牌 老婆果然比較愛
餵食壓力減輕後
策略相對多元
不過同樣人數下
玩法可能會久了後就固定了
因為建築物是固定的
打獵時會造成down time比較長
規則:
*起始食物 1-1-2-3-3-3-3
*隨機放置七個收獲標記，兩人移掉一個綠的
*兩人遊戲不用第九格(LV4 探險不用)
*飼養 平地馬廄一隻，森林馬廄一隻豬，礦場一隻驢，起始房間一對一樣的，牧場一格一對，有馬廄乘二，狗可放牧羊，比羊少一隻
*分數 基礎動物少一種扣兩分，空格一格扣一分，小麥0.5，菜矮人紅寶石所有動物都一分，乞討標記-3分少一個食物一個
*派矮人一定要從武器等級最低的，可用紅寶石取代這規則
*行動拿到的動物可以直接轉食物，繁殖的則要有位置生，不能直接轉
*遠征等級代表可以選幾種不同的獎勵，遠征武器會生一級
*單人遊戲:行動格變成固定順序，資源累積格超過六格會移除，可用紅寶石保留

RATING: 9.5
One of the better games of the five in the trilogy :-) 

This lacks most of the things that make Agricola a poor game, but keeps the few good things and builds on that. 

After a few plays: This is a typical Rosenberg. As intended is has a lot of common with Agricola, but is more open and is more about choices than desperation. It also have some in common with Ora et Labora. 

First play had very little food and felt too much like Agricola: A lot you wanna do, too few actions, then you eat and the game is over. Dunno if it it was a coincidence or just poor beginners, but the next few plays were better. 

I like the variation in possible winning strategies. 
I would have liked a little bit more variation in actions though. I mean more variation between games, now you know all that will come and can, if you want to, use the same strategy over and over again.

RATING: 8
Caverna is very, very similar to Agricola, to the point where it kind of feels like you're playing some sort of bizarre Agricola expansion.  In addition to the normal crop raising and animal husbandry (complete with the Farmers of the Moor idea of clearing your fields), you have the options to mine gems out of your cave, and give your workers "weapons" to go on adventures for your choice of a bunch of different resources.  In this game, it feels like it's a lot easier to get what you need, which for me removes some of tension that I enjoyed in Agricola.  It does seem like the various paths are well-balanced, though.

RATING: 4
It's a worker placement game, like Agricola. There are many options in the game, as you can go towards farming, furnishing your cave or go for an expedition. I've played just one game against people who had played Agricola before (me being a completely noob) and I won the game; still, I didn't enjoy it.

This game is really LONG and very FIDDY. I don't mind with games that have many components, like Puerto Rico, or games that last long, like Caylus, but this game exceeds my limits in both categories. I did find it interesting in terms of choices and strategy, but I had the feeling that the game was playing me and not vice-versa. Especially the "you have to constantly feed your people" made me feel that I was being played; Stone Age has a similar mechanism, but eventually you can control it.

Bottom line: too long, too fiddy, too much analysis-paralysis. Interesting choices, but hardly to play it again.

RATING: 7
Let me start by saying the Agricola is one of my Top 10 games. I love the tension and challenge of that game and I was initially dismissive of an updated version that eschews that tension. Then I tried the game…and I really liked it. There is still tension, as family members still need to be fed, but there a lot of interesting things to do. One of the things I particularly liked is the ability to arm my dwarves. This allows them to go on adventures, sometimes an addition to taking other actions. However, the dwarves with the highest level must be played last, so they are often limited in what actions are available to them. This sets up a trade off in the game that forces players evaluate the cost-benefit of having highly armed dwarves vs. peaceful people. In our learning game, the winner actually never took the family growth action, but made his two dwarves highly skilled warriors and benefitted strongly from their adventures. It is appealing that keeping a small family may be a viable strategy. Will I miss Minor Improvements and Occupations? Yes, I think so, though I can’t comment on the variety of building that are available and how they change. Perhaps that will provide enough variability to compensate. I’m really looking forward to playing this again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Better than Agricola?  Time will tell.  Certainly a worthy addition to the Harvest series.

RATING: 8
Mixed feelings about this - Agricola is still one of my favourite games, and I really thought I would enjoy this even more. On the one hand I think it's an interesting redesign and I can see why some feel this is an improvement. But to me it also feels overwrought, too fiddly, and just too much like an optimisation puzzle, losing much of Agricola's tension and charm.

In Agricola, I often get a real sense of achievement, whether it's growing the family, baking bread, building a plough, or even just managing to feed everyone, but in Caverna most things are comparatively easy to come by, so there is no such sense of accomplishment, and the feeding phases of the harvest feel like minor inconveniences instead of important milestones that need to be carefully planned around.

Many of the mechanisms are very similar to Agricola (and even more similar to Agricola: All Creatures Big & Small) but with several extra layers of clutter and fiddliness. I feel it would have been better if Caverna had stood a bit further apart, maybe dropping the grain/vegetables, having fewer animals, and focussing more on the digging/gem mining/expeditions. As it is, it falls foul of my increasing distaste for sameyness in games, and I don't think I'll want to bother with it very often. I have a sense of nostalgia for Agricola, remembering how excited I was to play it when it first came out - Caverna does nothing of the sort for me.

Not sure how to rate it - in terms of game quality it's a 9 or 9.5, but in terms of how enthusiastic I am to play it it's only a 6 or so, so I'm settling on the middle ground of 8.

RATING: 8
This game is a modernization of Agricola, and it does a fantastic job. Every issue I've had with Agricola is wiped away by this game. It delivers on all the things I liked about it's predecessor and adds to it some really genius innovations such as the way the workers level up and the way that you can follow through with different strategies and still get high scores.

In particular, one of the things I disliked about Agricola was the way that getting more family members was a bit of a dominant strategy. In caverna I get the same core farming mechanisms, minus the flip luck of the cards plus the game has a perfectly viable strategy for having fewer, stronger workers.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::nostar::nostar: Raw enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I enjoy it.  The components are undeniably awesome, and set up is generally faster than Agricola.  The adventuring is interesting and gives your workers a bit of personality.  The random harvest idea is a cool one (and I think borrowed somewhat from the "Four Seasons" postcard expansions for Agricola), and there's lots of neat little ways the game is interesting and distinct from its predecessor.  
Complaints are few: the box is a little flimsy, and the rules have some minor ambiguities.  
That said, I'm happy to have this in my collection.  It is a fun, fairly easy-to-play worker placement game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I really like this one, but I'm not sure I like it better than Agricola.  It's harder to compare than you might think because they often feel very different.  There are so many similarities, but the major pieces of the game seem completely distinct.

On one hand, all of the ways in which the game has mitigated getting screwed out of your spot is really nice for a friendlier game.  But those rubies allow for so many extra options that it can really create some serious Analysis Paralysis where none might have previously existed.

The houses replace the Agricola cards, which again allows people flexibility to do what they want and not get screwed.  But at the same time it reduces replayability and I worry players will stagnate either in their favorite strategy or in group think.

So it's greatest assets are also it's greatest flaws.  Tough to say which way to go, but for now I like Agricola better.

Thoughts after first play: I liked it, but have some reservations. Took four of us (one experienced player and three noobs) three hours to play, so 45 mins. per player vs. 30 mins on the box. We were very rushed at the end--right when the tendency is to slow down and make sure you are doing the most to maximize your score--because we had to end our session at 3 hours. I'm not a huge fan of games where you are analyzing a dozen options to figure out which one gets you the most points, and in this game, "a dozen options" is a euphamism, since ALL of your options are publicly available--you have no hidden agenda options like you do with the cards in Agricola. So on the one hand, I liked that you could set up the game and start playing without having to examine your cards and possibly decide which ones to keep, but I didn't like that there were SO many options to choose from. A little overwhelming. I can imagine that for a super-competitive group or one where analysis paralysis was permitted to run wild, it would be a very slow game. We all had "if I had done this instead of that" regrets at the end. There are also A LOT of bits in this box. It's that fat for a reason--filled to the top with no insert. It also takes up a lot of table real estate, born from the fact that everything is public, so it all has to be laid out. I do want to play again, and soon, because with more plays will come better knowledge of the possible options (especially the cavern upgrades) and more confidence. Better than Agricola? Less punishing on the feeding aspect, in large part due to the rubies, which act like wildcards that you can spend almost any time you need them, so if the feeding in Agricola turns you off, then Caverna provides more flexibility without removing the mechanic entirely. For me, that's better. Thematically, I prefer Agricola. You are a human farmer in pre-industrial Europe. In Caverna, you are a cave-dwelling, mining, farming, adventuring dwarf. I can't relate as well to that (but I do like the addition of adventuring). Overall, I liked it and want to play it again. I won't rate it higher than Agricola (yet), but no lower.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.3
This game pulls a lot for the mechanics from Agricola but the strategy and tactics are quite different.  There are no cards in this game but instead there are building tiles.  I like that all the construction is done on the player board  and has a nice tile laying feel rather than having a tableau of cards.  It just feels more thematic.  Since there is only one of each dwelling tile this creates competition for tiles.  Thus, there is more player interaction as strategies collide and tactical decisions have to be made once your tile has been bought.  As a solo game, it's pretty good but i worry since there is no variation with setup and no random elements from game to game (not true for multi-player games) that this becomes more of a puzzle as you just try the same things over again.  Overall, fun game that I think would play best with 2 or 3.  Anymore would be too long of a game.  I'm still not sure if I like this as much as Agricola but more plays will help. Update:  played again and this really doesn't seem as much like Agricola but more of a cross between Agricola and Ora et Labora. I liked it a little more the second time around and I've only play the basic game.

RATING: 6
Another game by Uwe Rosenberg. Take resources, combine them, move them, breed them, grow them and transform into other things. Use them to build tings, or feed people, and ultimately make victory points. This is quite overtly a redesign of Agricola, but since Le Havre and Ora et Labora were also redesigns of that game it feels a little like the designer is doing their play testing on the consumer. The problem here is that I still don't feel like the game has quite cracked it. The masses of bits and many exceptions are still too fiddly, while the addition of the expeditions as a parallel way of developing and collecting resources actually detracts from the competitiveness of the game. These are good games but they demand more than casual play and I can't imagine anyone choosing to do that with four different games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A complete reworking of Agricola - itself already a great game, of course - and in my opinion an improvement, this replaces much of the "how do I get my food?" tension of the original with a sense of abundance (it's relatively easy to get food as everyone can get food from animals without having to buy a cooking implement first, most actions give several benefits at once, and there are also rubies as wildcard resources), but puts the real decisions elsewhere. It's much more like a standard Euro now, in that there are multiple routes to the victory points (no longer does every farm look identical at the end of the game) and you have to make the same sort of tricky decisions as in any other worker placement game - what to prioritise at this moment? Do I need an extra worker now, or is it not worth the extra food it'll cost me (unlike its parent, here my feeling is that it's not absolutely necessary to grab a couple of extra workers as soon as possible)? Which building (technically "furnishing") do I build now? (And there is only one of each, apart from the basic dwellings for extra workers.)

These furnishing tiles are another big difference from Agricola - essentially replacing the cards of the original - giving, as I have said, a wide variety of approaches to take, and giving a dilemma as to whether you spend your precious resources on the furnishing early before trying to build up its value, or leaving it till later (when your economy is more able to handle it, but risking not getting it at all if you've telegraphed your intentions to others). There are also weapons to build to go on "expeditions" (essentially just another way of getting several resources or other benefits at once), and new types of animals and more complicated rules on how you can keep them.

I must say that for me, and most others in my gaming groups (although interestingly not so much my wife, who prefers Agricola for being more streamlined, which it undoubtedly is), Caverna is a big improvement on Agricola. I still don't find myself loving it though, so I can only give this a rating of 8 - the same as my rating for Agricola, despite me thinking this is an improvement. I do think there is room in a collection for both, they are actually quite different games in feel despite one clearly being an evolution of the other. Great game, but not for me a "must play".

RATING: 6
Não me conquistou. Tudo bem que jogar com 6 provavelmente está longe do recomendado, mas minhas questões com o jogo acho que perpassariam qualquer número de jogadores. E a comparação com o Agrícola é cruel.

Primeiro, se é pra fazer uma variação do Agrícola, tem que ser melhor. Entre jogar Agrícola e um outro jogo parecido e pior, por que não o original? O Caverna pode ser um bom approach pra quem nunca jogou Agrícola, por que é bem mais fácil de aprender, mas pra quem já aprendeu, não sei se faz sentido.

Antes de começar a descascar, vamos às vantagens:

1. As regras são mais fáceis de aprender mesmo. Não tem forno, não tem padeiro, não tem occupation, minor nem major improvement. Quase tudo dá um ponto, não tem aquela tabelinha, mais fácil de memorizar. Cercas e estábulos foram simplificados.

2. Por não ter tudo isso, a fritação de cérebro é menor, o que leva, me pareceu, a menos demora para escolher a jogada que você vai fazer.

Mas pra compensar tem várias desvantagens tb.

1. Muitas casas tem ações múltiplas, como a das expedições. Você chega a poder fazer quatro ações de uma tacada só, e a demora para isso pode ser grande, compensando a rapidez para escolher a jogada.

2. Não sei se gostei de o jogo ser tão abundante. A interação do Agrícola se dava pela escassez. Você tinha medo de deixar de pegar o Bake Bread e se estrepar no Harvest, por isso adiantava sua jogada mesmo sendo sub-ótima ou ia no Starting Player pra garantir sua alimentação na próxima rodada. Ou você pegava o Bake Bread só de sacanagem pra marcar o adversário. A interação do jogo estava aí. No Caverna, alimentação é bem menos dramática, crescer família não é obrigatório. Tem várias ações que te permitem alcançar quase o mesmo objetivo, e mesmo quando não tem, existem duas ações que te permitem fazer a ação que o amiguinho já fez. Quase tudo é possível, quase o tempo todo, então a interação e o bloqueio são quase irrelevantes. Ficou pra mim multiplayer-solitaire demais.

3. O autor diz no manual que o jogo foi projetado para que em partidas com muitos jogadores seja necessário haver um equilíbrio entre quem aposta em expedição e quem não aposta para não não despontar um líder. Diz ele que se todos apostarem em expedição menos um, esse um certamente vai vencer. E se só um apostar em expedição, esse um vai vencer. Pode ter sido só o nosso jogo, pode ter sido noobice nossa, mas na nossa partida, a única jogadora que não fez expedições ficou longe de vencer. O jogo parece estar desequilibrado em favor das expedições. Mas foi só uma partida, difícil dizer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It is virtually impossible to honestly comment on this game without comparing it to Agricola. I'll start by saying while I do like Caverna, it feels like a less focused, less tense, more wide open, more forgiving and more... meandering Agricola.

One of the biggest changes in this game is the move from a private stash of cards to buildings available to everyone. In theory, I like that change, but in practice... I have mixed feelings about it. The cards helped give focus to Agricola and helped drive your actions down a different path than the other players. Without this initial nudge at a direction, Caverna, especially for new players, can feel like wandering aimlessly for a while. There are so many options, it's hard to even know where to start. 

In Caverna, there are so many "good" choices that the tension for optimizing the best choices feels lacking for much of the game. I think part of that is because of it's loose food conversion rules, part of that is due to the fact that every animal is worth the same points at the end, and part of it is that any particular resource is extremely easy to come by. After three plays it seems that often a players choice seems to be: pick one of 3-4 equally good things; it doesn't really matter what you pick. A ruby is as good as a vegetable is as good as a pig.

I'm not sure how I feel about the expeditions. The mechanic seems tacked on, and it's almost as if the designer thought players needed an even easier way to get the resources they need. It just further takes away from the tension of the game. 

All that probably sounds like I don't like Caverna. If Agricola didn't exist, and/or I had played this first, I actually would probably like it a lot. As it is, though, it didn't grab me initially as much as Agricola did, nor does it have the staying power Agricola has. There is really no reason both this and Agricola should be in my collection; Agricola is clearly the superior game and I can't ever see reaching for this game instead. To justify keeping both, Caverna would either need to be more approachable, play in less time, or offer a better challenge- and it does none of those things.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Except for the newly added adventure action, this is an amazing game. Agricola is no more.

RATING: 6
Feels more flexible than Agricola as there are more options for choice of actions.  Players can launch into various paths early.  No hidden or asymmetric information as one got from the cards in Agricola as all the options are open from the start, the variation comes from how players develop their tableaus.  I am concerned that this might lead to set opening moves after a while as a result.  The only real randomizing element is how the round cards come out.  The downside (perhaps only initially) is that the numerous options can make the first plays a bit slower as players consider options.  

A game with a lot of bits (cardboard and wood) that needs a large table to play - even with a low number of players.  In the end an interesting game, but one that can drag on (even for those who know Agricola - I would feel sorry for the absolute tyro who doesn't have the references to Agricola to fall back upon.)  Need to be in the right mood to play this one, so rated a 6 as a result.  Not the first game I would rush to play, but one I might enjoy sporadically throughout the year.

RATING: 8
Nice game but not as good as Agricola.

Positives: Components are great! 
I like the weapon system and having to send your weak dwarfs first.
Negative: I do not like the removal of baking/cooking animals. It's too easy now and it makes grain really boring since it's just 1 food. 
The point system - Many people love this. I hate it. Since all animals are equal, the different types of animals are less important. It doesn't make sense that point wise, the cows are worth the same as sheep. 
Resources - I felt like I had way too many resources in the early game. I didn't even know what to do with all of my wood that I picked up. I was spending it with reckless abandon and I wasn't able to run out until the last game. It's not as tight as I would like it. Rooms are too cheap and there isn't a resource that is hard to get. I really like that reed in Agricola is hard to get and everyone wants to get it. In this game, wood and stone is abundant so it's not difficult to get a lot of it. If anything, the only hard thing to get is ore and that's if a bunch of people are going the weapon route. In short, the game is too open. Since there are completely different routes to go, there is less competition for each resource and that's a bad thing.
Same rooms available each game - I can see the game getting very samey in the future. There's very little in the game that changes from game to game. 

With all that hate... I still enjoyed the game and an 8 is a good rating. Besides being different though, it's a strictly worse game than Agricola + expansion for me.

06-09-2014. Rated after 1 play. I played Agricola about 1 week before trying out Agricola 2.0, Caverna. Both are very similar games, with the mechanisms basically carried over almost 100%. The largest perceived drawbacks of Agricola are the fixed scoring, which Caverna fixed by decreasing the severity of punishment and rewarding specialisation.

While I currently rank Caverna lower than Agricola (7 vs 7.5), this could be because I played Agricola with 2 making for a speedier game that I enjoyed better.

RATING: 9
I go back and forth whether I prefer this one over Agricola, but for me personally, Agricola is still #1.  The cards just force me to do something different every game, whereas with Caverna I can tend to go after the same combination of rooms that I know served me well in past games.  Plus I love the Farmers of the Moor expansion and the great variety of major improvements.  But for fun factor for the whole table, Caverna is better - it is less stressful to feed your family, there's a lot more interaction than you get in most WP games with all the competition over buying weapons/going on the best expeditions/using rubies to cut in line, and there just always seems to be good spots to go on, whereas the Agricola end-game often traps you into either risking planning a specific 3 or 4-step sequence of actions that you must follow to score some big points but can be easily blocked, or having a few actions at the end that get you 0 or maybe 1 point.  Plus it's much easier to examine and digest the variety of rooms while you are playing the game, whereas new players to Agricola need a significant amount of time before they start to examine their hand of 14 cards.  So I think for new players to heavier WP's, I would recommend going straight to Caverna.

I think that in my case it depends on my mood.  Caverna feels more laid back, and is definitely more open; the options are all there for all the players, every game, rather than being dealt or drafted at the start.  One of my favourite things is that you are free to specialize here; I always feel that Agricola's capped point system pushes players to branch out and "do everything" a bit too much.  On the other hand, some players may feel that the fact that all options are available and don't vary from game to game will result in stagnation over multiple plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
3500g of cardboard and wood. Slightly concerned my shelf will collapse. There's a lot in the box and thus the high price. All the cardboard and wood barely fits back in the enormous box. It feels like I'm getting good value for money though - lots and lots of good quality bits.

A good game in its own right, but overall it's just a tweaked and rethemed Agricola. Add adventuring, change humans into dwarves, plus a bunch of other little changes. Most of the same stuff is still there: Feeding two food per family, setting yourself up for family growth, more spots to place your family members each turn being revealed, etc.

Okay, Caverna has some really good things going for it - three standing out in particular:

1.It's a lot less stressful and forgiving than Agricola. Not a lot of brain power is spent on scraping enough food together to feed your dwarves. Instead any food shortfall is made up for with a variety of resources. So you're spending more time working out how to score points than stave off starvation.

2. The adventure mechanic is an excellent addition to the mix. Here you can pick up a variety of resources or perform actions with more choice available to dwarves who've invested more in better weapons.

3. Scoring is simplified. Gone is the band of animal and vegetable points. Each animal is now worth one point regardless of type. Wheat is half a point and vegetable one point. So again you're not wasting time thinking about whether eating an animal is going to drop you a point or not. The fiddly scoring is gone and the game is better for it.

Decisions are in no way the same as Agricola, but for the collection for most people it feels more like it'd be a one game or the other decision rather than both. I'd probably choose Caverna if I hadn't already fallen for Agricola. With Agricola I prefer the individual minor improvements and occupations over Caverna's common pool of building tiles. Games feel more different with a different set of cards in my hand each outing. I'm used to the fiddly, samey scoring too. But damn, I like those dwarf weapons and their adventures in Caverna. I like that I don't have to think quite so much in Caverna and can chill out a bit more while playing. Luckily I don't have to choose - my wife tells me I can have two girlfriends. She's a peach.

RATING: 9
This is every bit as much fun as Agricola, one of my favorite games of all time.  I don't agree with many people who say it has replaced Agricola fully.  They really are two different games.  I give the slight edge at this point to Caverna, simply because I'm enjoying exploring the options there right now.  

Caverna Advantages:  Easier to feed your family as almost anything can be readily converted to food.  This results in a slightly less stressful experience, which some gamers will appreciate.  The questing part is pretty cool also.

Agricola Advantages:  TONS of cards to make the game different each time.  And playing with a card draft keeps players from being dealt a hand of duds.  The static setup of Caverna could possibly get old.  

Update 2/17:  When I think about whether I wan't to play Agricola or Caverna, I just have to say Agricola.  The cards just win it for me, and the extras of caverna are interesting, but not as fun as this.  Yes, I think farming is more fun than questing!

RATING: 9.5
The cards from Agricola have been replaced with cave furnishings which are open for all to buy from the begining of the game (These give you bonus options and VP's just like the cards in Agricola). No secrecy - everything is public knowledge!

Played quite a few games now (all 4, 5 or 6 player) and thoroughly enjoyed the games. I really like Agricola and this is a variant that works very well. 
The main diffference is that in Agricola you have to get the resources you need before others. In Caverna there are so many good choices the anxiety is over making the right choice!
Still not achieved 100 points in any game which is my goal!
The introduction of expeditions adds another twist and seems to be a necessary path to take to win the game as it allows you to accrue so much stuff! Never yet seen a player win without ever arming at least one dwarf ((Scrub that. I won a 5 player game without arming!))

Crucial to allow expedition accruals to be chosen while the game continues, otherwise the game takes much longer.

RATING: 9.5
One of the best eurogames in existence. It removes or improves upon everything I didn't like about Agricola, which is a great game in its own right. Caverna is relaxing and fun (which is not to say it doesn't challenge you and make you think - it certainly does) where Agricola is tense and feels like work. The addition of expeditions is fantastic, and I love watching my dwarves build up their farm and dig the caverns into the mountainside. You really feel like you're invested in creating this little kingdom for them.

The component quality and artwork is first-rate, and the sheer quantity of bits makes me positively giddy.

The only downside is the long set-up and take-down time, but this can be greatly reduced with an adequate storage solution.

Highly recommended.

This Agricola-clone tries to change gears with rooms that do specific things as opposed to Occupations and Minor Improvements.  It also adds an adventuring mechanic, which I think is wonky, since some of your loot can be a stable?  Anyway, I didn't mind playing it and you get a lot for the $90 price tag, but the set up is a bear and I don't care for games that require you to memorize, or at least be familiar with 30-40 different tiles.  I prefer the cards in Agricola by a long stretch.  Playing Caverna confirmed for me that I don't need to buy it.

RATING: 9

RATING: 8
I borrowed this game from a local store and played with my wife and twin 8-year old daughters. Everyone is very experienced with games like Catan so we felt this would be a step up.

The Good:
This game struck a nice chord.  It was just so fun to play, and had so many choices on how to develop and get to the end goal. It was fun seeing the choices everyone made on how to develop and expand, and the game stayed engaging for everyone.

The Bad:
I have 3 complaints, but they're pretty minor.

1.There are approximately 1.3 million pieces to this game, and the board has a different configuration for every different number of players. Setup and takedown were a bear, and took as long as playing a short filler game.

2. The game was very long. 3 hours for four of us to play, albeit we had some pretty young players. Nevertheless we were a bit tired out by the end.

3. While I was delighted by the plethora of choice and option, my wife often froze up with AP as she tried to work out how to proceed after actions she wanted were taken by other players.

Overall:
The good far outweighed the bad, and I would absolutely recommend this game. My family is looking forward to playing again with a bit more experience under our belts, but at the same time we're not looking forward to the long setup of the play area  :)

RATING: 8
[3 plays]  This is basically a large and excellent variant of Agricola. The theme is tweaked to a land where dwarves mine, farm, and forge weapons to bloodlessly collect treasures. Feeding your population is slightly simplified. Rubies act as wild cards for other resources and foraging expeditions also act as an outlet to obtain a choice of needed resources. Card abilities from Agricola are replaced with a vast array of rooms for your cavern that grant special abilities and scoring options.

I enjoy Caverna both for its similarities to Agricola and for its unique twists. It can be both more bewildering in its choices at times and less rigid than the cards of its parent game.


Most recent play Oct '17.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This game definitely looks, feels, and plays like the second iteration of Agricola.  It streamlines several of the features, but it's still necessary to basically do everything in order to score positive points.  The theme of Agricola was fine, but I HATED the cards and how they were used in the game.  Feeding is still a concern in this game, but somewhat less so since food conversion is simplified and there are more ways to handle the food situation.  I like that all of the Furnishings are available from the start and that there is only one of each since this puts everybody on an equal footing to start, but also makes it such that all players will head down an individual and different path.  This helps make the game interesting between the players, but I'm also concerned that it enables players to basically take the same strategic route each and every time.  I guess that will be proved for disproved over time.  Overall, this game is good and I like it much more than Agricola, but I still don't love it.  The Quests is an very interesting way mitigate Action and Resources, but there are almost too many ways to do things which can lead to analysis paralysis.  I would recommend that all players new to Caverna play the Introductory game several times to learn the mechanisms and inner-workings of the game before throwing in all of the available Furnishings since that can be overwhelming and confusing.

Likes:
+ No cards!
+ Food can be converted at any time.
+ Simplified pasture/fence building.
+ Rubies (wild resources and some actions).
+ Only 1 copy of each Furnishing forces players to take different paths.
+ Not as much negative scoring.
+ Action and Resource mitigation through Quests.

Dislikes:
+ Full game with all Furnishings is a bit overwhelming to start.
+ Need to do EVERYTHING or score negative points.
+ Plays a bit too long overall.
+ Too many options/paths?

RATING: 7.5
In direct comparison to Agricola:
+ More harvest phases but simplified food acquisition result in a much less frustrating gameplay
+ The (possibly unbalanced) hands of cards have been replaced by room tiles that are available for everyone.
+ Streamlined scoring: nearly everything is worth 1 VP and reduced/removed penalties for missing resources
+ Rubies act as a wildcard for goods, thus no actions have to be wasted to e.g. acquire that single missing stone.
+ The different buildings allow you to score a variety of goods in the endgame scoring, allowing for specialization.
+ Improved animal husbandry (dogs can herd sheep, boars can be stabled in the woods, 1 donkey per mine)

- Due to the fixed setup, there isn't a lot of variety in gameplay (limited replayability, esp. compared to Agricola)
- Not a good game for 2 players (not enough competition; some strategies are not viable, others overly powerful)
- Strategy is strongly influenced by player order

This game desperately needs some sort of random setup before it really shines.

Update: Apparently an expansion is now in development. Hopefully it will add some replayability / variance to the game.

RATING: 7
[2014-08-02]
Rosenberg megint elővette a nagy másológépét és lemásolta az Agricola szabályát pár példányban, kiegészítette pár oldallal, átcímkézte a dobozt, kicsit megnagyobbította, dobált bele még pár állatot és némi ásványt, kivette az összes kártyát és kiadta Caverna néven. Röviden ennyi Uwe új játékának története, de hosszabban se sokkal több. Újdonság az embereink vadászati tulajdonsága, amik tulajdonképpen joker akciók, ahogyan a rubin is a játékban. Sokat gondolkodtam, hogy a rubin jó vagy nem jó. Talán azért rakták bele, mert így minden helyzetből ki lehet jönni. Kicsit olyan bármire van megoldásként került bele. Nincsenek kártyák, ezzel a random faktor kisebb, cserébe rögtön van 48 fajta épület, amit meg lehet venni. Szóval sokkal több lehetőség, de emiatt nem mélyebb a játék, csak nehezebb játszani és szerintem emiatt hosszabb is jóval. Míg az Agricolában más köre alatt is tudok azon filózni a kártyáimat nézve, hogy vajon melyiket játsszam ki, itt nem tudok előre gondolkodni sokat, mert több játékos esetén nem biztos, hogy adott akció még elérhető lesz-e, mikor rám kerül a sor. Nem értem a Caverna nagy sikerét. Egyszerűen annyit csinált Uwe, hogy a mai trendnek megfelelően baromi sok lehetőséget tett a játékba, de amíg ez a Le Havre-ban a fantasztikusan zseniálisan egyszerű lépek egyet és akciózok egyet dolog mellé tökéletesen illik, itt az akciózok x törpémmel x*x-et mellé már annyi lehetőséget ad, hogy nincs esély átszámolni. Ha meg nem számolom át, akkor egyértelműen az nyer, aki gyorsabban kalkulál vagy több időt használ el a játékban. Ketten 2-2 órás volt a parti, 7-en esélytelen, hogy belátható időn belül befejeződjön. Minden előző mondatomtól függetlenül egy jól működő játék a Caverna, gyönyörű komponensekkel, érthető, szájbamagyarázó szabállyal, jó segítőlapkákkal, szép, érthető táblával, csak hát volt előtte egy Agricola és ezt nehéz felednem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Complexity:  Medium-Hard
Players: 2-7

Agricola's bigger, friendlier younger brother.  Caverna is a rich and deep worker placement game that is surprisingly intuitive once you get over the initial shock of the myriad of wood and cardboard in the very heavy box.  The neat thing about Caverna is that the various engines are interconnected, meaning that while there may be increased competition for certain components, no single component is essential for any individual strategy.  The result is a game that manages to be at once very tactical and interactive and very strategic.  The time commitment is substantial, but it's quite worth the investment.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Caverna is certainly on par with Agricola, and it feels like a redesign of it.  I like the dwarf theme, and the addition of the cave adds a lot of options to the gameplay.  The sheer amount of buildings is daunting and benefits experienced players.  The components are exceptional.  It will take quite a few plays to mine all the strategies in Caverna.
