=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Initial thoughts from a single solo play: I like it!  

I had been hesitant on getting this game for the longest time, because I was convinced Agricola was the better game due to the Minor Improvement and Occupation cards.  Those cards provided a lot of variability from game to game, and gave each player individual goals to work toward.

The more I thought about it though, the with less variability from game to game, that allows players to focus their attention more on each other than trying to solve a new puzzle of MinImp/Occ cards each game.  Granted, it's not the most interactive of games, but it frees people up a little to pay attention more to what other people are doing, and makes it a little less multi-player solitaire.  Plus, Caverna feels less punishing, which in of itself makes it a more enjoyable experience.  

The solo game is a fun optimization puzzle, but sadly one that can potentially be solved, since there's no variation or randomness to it.  Despite the limited replayability, it is still a fun puzzle while trying to solve it!  My plan is to try to solve it on my own, without any help/spoilers from forum posts.

RATING: 7.5
1 play, 3er

Rather than a new game, we have yet another refinement from Rosenberg. Basically, it's Agricola made faster and easier, and now with a vaguely fantasy theme (dwarves). I'm not the biggest fan of Agricola, although I'm getting to like it more now that I finally bought the iPad version for this trip (solo game series is pretty cool -- nicely done, Dale!). I've always found the struggle to get food a bit too much for me, and it seems like the game ends when you FINALLY get your little farm working! Maybe I'm just not a very good player? Probably. Anyway,  I prefer Le Havre and Ora et Labora. 

For me, Caverna is a good thing: I'd be happy to replace our copy of Agricola with Caverna. Or not ... whatever. But I'd certainly be willing to play Caverna again, this time actually looking at all the little building tiles in this game (which have replaced all the cards)!

[b]--- Summary ---[/b]
Like Agricola, except:
- dwarves instead of humans
- you have a mountain into which you carve space used for dwellings (one/person; no upgrading) and buildings
- no cards; instead, there is a display of tiles from which EVERYONE can choose
- more animal/resource types
- harvest occurs at semi-random intervals
- can make food, at fixed ratios of resource:food, at any time
- "fighting" dwarves: used to send people on expeditions (you don't fight other players)
- slightly different point rewards for people, space, etc., and lower penalties for missing stuff; and SIMPLER scoring (most things are worth 1VP)
- less oppressive pressure to get your stuff done before someone else takes that action spot!
- more streamlined

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Agricola is my favorite game.  So, as you might imagine, I was excited to play this successor looking for a similar but also different feeling experience.  To that end I was a bit disappointed.  Like the other Agricola follow-up, All Creatures Big and Small (which I completely bypassed and have never played), it got rid of my favorite part of the original game; The Occupation and Improvement cards.  And this isn't the only detail that I really don't care for in this new formula.

Behind the scenes, Caverna is just Agricola, in fact the rulebook itself tells you if you know how to play Agricola you can skip huge sections of the rulebook because they will just feel redundant.  However, where they implemented changes I feel like I dislike a lot more than I like and makes me feel completely skipping this title would have been the way to go.  But because of my love for Agricola I just felt like I couldn't.

First of all, food is a LOT easier to come by in Caverna.  Almost anything can be converted into food from the beginning of the game without the need for an oven or fireplace, food can be converted at any time, and if this wasn't enough when you build on certain spaces the game will just hand you one or two food or a wild boar as a freebie.  This takes all the tension out of a game that was fantastic because of it, not despite it.  My first game was with 5 players and everyone accelerated to 4,5, or even 6 dwarves quickly and was never struggling to feed their families.

I think the biggest goal in Caverna was to make a streamlined, easier, and ultimately more accessible Agricola.  I already mentioned at the top of my comments that they removed all the cards from the game, much like they did in All Creatures Big and Small, this is egregious in my opinion.  The best part of Agricola was the endless variety and variability the game had that presented new ways to play each time and never had the game feeling old.  Removing the cards is like removing the game's soul.  But what baffles me is they clearly removed them, again like All Creatures Big and Small, to make the game more accessible but they replaced the cards with 47 individually unique tiles.

This is clearly more overwhelming to new players than 7 each of occupation and improvement cards.  There is an introductory game side that uses a lot less buildings but come on, no competent gamer wants to play the introductory game.  Have you played the family game of Agricola, it's horrendous and boring because it ALSO removes the cards, the best part of the game.  And while adding a ton more variety of options in these different tiles to a single game of Caverna it also removes a ton of variety and replayability from the game overall.  There may have been 47 different furnishings to buy but after seeing them in one game I already know what they are and have an idea of what is and isn't good in what situation.  Not only that but having them all available to all players every game they can pick and choose a strategy and better ones will usually be dominant as opposed to the brilliance of having to make the different cards you got every game work for you and being able to play the game differently every time.

Now the one thing I did quite enjoy with Caverna is the new expedition mechanic.  In Caverna you can equip your dwarven family members with weapons and go on expeditions to bring back items.  The better your weapon the more and better stuff available for you to bring back with you.  This is an interesting new layer to the game that works really well.  There's even an opportunity cost to higher level dwarves since they always need to be played last and the best spaces for them may be gone by then.

Overall Caverna is a big disappointment and anyone who accuses me of wanting it to be something it isn't is blind if they don't think this was MEANT to be compared side-by-side with Agricola.  So much of the game is actually just copied and pasted and they tried to make a game with wider appeal to get more people playing it and, I'm sure, make more money.  And honestly, that's what makes me quite enjoy this game despite it's shortcomings.

What?!  After all that criticism I like it.  The problem with Agricola is that, for example what I see as exquisite tension in trying to find a food engine to feed my family every turn many others see simply as a stressful and brutal game experience.  To that end I don't get to play Agricola NEARLY as much as I would like to.  This game still has a lot of Agricola in it and there's more I like about the game besides the tension and huge variety of cards.  Plus it does have more appeal to a broader audience so if I want that kind of a game fix, I'm more likely to get this played than Agricola.  For those reasons I think it has legs and this gigantic box also has a spot on my shelf.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Comparing this game to Agricola is mandatory as they are similar in many aspects but that's not necessarily a bad thing, mainly because I'm a huge Agricola fan. That said, I can only be (again) a huge Caverna fan.

The production is amazing. I've played with 4 players only (which seems to be the hot spot) but the game can get the 7 player experience!!! The wooden bits are great and so are all the counters, individual and common boards.

Rules are more streamlined and there are a few advantages over its predecessor, like all buildings are displayed giving each player the same possibilities of getting them, unlike the cards in Agricola which may unbalance the game giving more or less luck.

I would prefer Agricola in 2 main aspects. The first one being the cards for the opposite reason on why players may prefer Caverna. Each time one plays an Agricola game the feeling is different depending on the cards you get. This means that, despite there are many reasons to play Caverna lots of times, obviously it's more closed than Agricola as it has fewer building options. The second reason why I prefer Agricola is related to the different stages that each player is at during the game, depending on the type of house one has at a giving moment. For me it's one of the most interesting things in Agricola as one player may be playing in the wood state, another in the clay state and even a third one playing in the stone state, thus making the gameplay quiet diverse from player to player in different stages during play.

Overall and once again, a great game by Uwe. Commercially a huge success too, which is also a reason to congratulate the publisher(s), Lookout Games mainly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Not often does a game skyrocket as quickly to the top 5 of my all-time favorite games as did Caverna after playing it a grand total of...one time! I've since played many times, and I am in love.  

Caverna has the perfect amount of variations to win. Do you focus heavily on planting and harvesting crops along with breeding animals out in the open? Do you concentrate your valuable (and limited) actions to dig deep underground, creating caverns, ore mines, and even a rare ruby mine? Do you invest heavily in weaponry to reap the awesome loot that comes from high-level weapons? Do you horde rubies to instantly turn them into valuable goods? Or do you plan a coordinated, balanced strategy to maximize your return on investment? I've seen every one of those tactics win.  

Here are a few random and fun small-scale things that I love about Caverna that will be particularly intriguing to those who have played Agricola: the dogs can "watch" sheep in open meadows without a fenced-in pasture; you can store a single donkey in each underground, unfurnished cavern; there is some randomness embedded with the harvest (you don't always know if there will be a harvest or not); you can store a single boar in a forest space with a stable; lastly, you can convert animals and harvested grain/vegetables into consumable food immediately! There are many other reasons why I prefer Caverna to Agricola, but these are just a few. 

Another awesome aspect is the furnishing of caverns. Don't overlook them! Once you build a cavern, you can "customize" it to suite your particular strategy and reap many benefits immediately, over several rounds, or at the end of the game. For example, one furnishing gives you one coin per two sheep at the end of the game. Another gives you one ruby for each of the next several rounds. Another gives you discounts on subsequent furnishings or pastures. There are many combinations of furnishings that can provide powerful enhancements to your gameplay. 

Set-up and take-down are a bit of a beast. There are, as I count it, 43 different types of playing pieces, tiles, boards, cards, and tokens for a grand total of 851 individual items (not including the cards) in a 9-pound box . But don't let that deter you! It is well worth it. The gameplay itself moves along at a good pace, and after a couple of games, you can keep the time under the estimated 30 minutes per player. 

Overall, I give this game a resounding 9.5 out of 10. I've never given a full 10 before (I'm saving it for that one special game!), but this is as close as I've come!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Picking a copy of up off the shelf at the store feels like it should come with some bit of fanfare. Just a small, musical theme hinting at grandiose finales to help imprint the moment in your mind. The game is weighty, coming in at a hefty 10 lbs. letting you know immediately that this is Uwe Rosenbergs flagship and the game feels like it is a pinnacle effort with its Agricola roots and its beautifully rendered components.
The game plays a lot like its revered predecessor, offering streamlining in some key areas, and adding some more options in its expeditions and the caverns side of the player board. The expeditions add another option for players to use to gain access actions that might be limited on the main board but at the expense of having to level up those explorers to a point where they are capable of provided those services to your growing family. This pretty much set the tone for the entire game.
All of the strategies in the game are very much so a practice in give and take. Wanna be a rancher and raise animals? Then you probably aren't going to own a crib worthy of MTV's Cribs. Wanna be a hero adventurer? Have at it, but you probably aren't going to have a huge farm full of wheat and veggies. The game allows for at most one good sideline in addition to your main strategy, but the need to feed your dwarves, fill up the player board, and get enough animals together not to be penalized in points at the end of the game will override any other side strategy you might want to invest in.
All in all, the game is a blast. Easily one of my favorite games I've played this year. Despite its high entry price, the game delivers on many levels and I have a feeling I'll work working to try to earn more and more points in it for years to come. 10/10

RATING: 10
The best successor to Agricola yet! You go, Uwe!  An 8 on the first play, a 9 on the second.  The feeling that you're getting to do so much more on each turn than you're used to doing on an Agricola turn makes the speed of Caverna seem exhilarating.  Most of the squares where you send your dwarves (what we called family members in Agricola) give you two rewards at once.  For example, take this piece of grain AND clear two squares of forest; take this pile of stone AND dig a tunnel and a cavern on the mining side of your board.  You can get vegetables and grain or sheep and donkeys making food for you in nothing flat.  So... how do you wield this great power?

RATING: 6.75
Fun game but lacks the tension that I feel when playing Agricola. It is fairly easy to feed your people (converting resources is easy) and it is hard to block someone's actions (multiple spots for the same thing and the "mimic" action which kills nearly all tension in the game). The adventuring questing thing is also a bit strong in that a few people have to do that every game and whoever gets first dibs on it will do much better than others.

Plus, the lack of randomness in buildings and lack of "variable player powers" leaves it feeling dry. There's also a lot of fiddliness with some of the rules, like dogs can hold sheep and stabled woods can hold boar but you need to clear the woods for any other animal? Weird.

Want to play it a few more times before I finalize my opinion, but right now Agricola + expansion (removing Occupations) is by far better.

The only thing that I like about Caverna more is the struggle in waiting to place workers with weapons (since it goes from lowest weapon to highest) which is really interesting. Oh, and the scoring is a lot easier and more forgiving which is kind of nice (never liked how workers gave 3 points in Agricola when they also give more actions -- it is like a double whammy that you need to max out on workers).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A little time consuming to set up, and a little time consuming to put away, but otherwise beautiful in every way. I've had fun teaching this game to my friends, and I've even gotten newer gamers to play it AND have fun with it despite its heaviness (figurative heaviness, though the box is also pretty heavy). Yeah, it can be a pain to get it set up and even find the room to play it, but the actual game itself is just pure fun.

A few (okay, quite a few) things:

1. Though I would classify this as a medium-heavy Euro, it has little to do with rule complexity and more to do with depth. Caverna's rules, though they are long, are incredibly intuitive with reminders everywhere- your board tells you how much gold is needed to purchase resources, and how much food those resources make, while the individual actions on the board tell you almost everything you need to know about that action. The furnishings can be a little too much for some people, so stick with the "family" version that more or less halves the amount of rooms available until everyone is ready for even more depth. 

2. People who liked Agricola's card drafting will not get the exact same experience. There are no individual goals written for you on the cards- you have to create your own goal. Many fans of Agricola claim this makes the game stale and limited in it replay-ability. I would say that this is only true if you personally decide to pick the same strategy over and over again. Yes, the lack of cards means that there is a certain series of actions for each game that would end up being the "best", however if you decide to look up what these are or spend the hours required to figure them out by staring at the 7 different player iterations of the board, then you are only spoiling it for yourself- besides, outside of the solo game, people will block your strategy at some point. If you decide to go the grain route and build the beer parlor every single game, then yeah, it's going to get stale. Mix it up for yourself, try a different strategy. It doesn't have to be stale, it's only as stale as you make it, I mean SERIOUSLY there are a TON of different avenues to pursue. The world of Caverna is your oyster.

3. Keep in mind the company you keep. Even though this sits in the medium-heavy euro category, this game could appeal to casual gamers more easily than competitive gamers (possibly because of the reasons in paragraph 2 above). The thing with Caverna to remember is that it really doesn't matter if you win or not- it just feels good to be at the end of the game and see what you've built and see what your neighbors have built. Everyone will play this game differently- some will be very competitive and block you at every junction, while others will go on their merry way harvesting and re-sowing vegetables all day long. Too many competitive gamers may make the game too stressful, while too many casual games might make the game feel too solitaire. This is something that's true for almost every board game out there, the only thing to keep in mind is that, as good as this game is, it doesn't eliminate either problem (and I can't think of a game I've played or seen that can).

4. I will say that, for 100 dollars, you could maybe get a few other great games instead from Coolstuff or Amazon. I wouldn't ever say this game isn't worth 90 dollars, but I will say that you should try and get it as cheap as you can without compromising the integrity of the box and its components- I got it for 62 dollars from Coolstuff, and, hell, 28 dollars is almost enough for another game!

5. When choosing between this and Agricola for my game collection (And you could argue there's room for both), Caverna won out almost entirely because of theme. Caverna, I'm sure it has been said, is very close to Minecraft in a board game- mining caves, getting ore, raising animals, farming, harvesting, etc. This appeals to people, I've found, more often than "you're a couple trying to raise a farm". Don't underestimate the power of the Dwarven, Minecraft-y theme, as it will peak the interest of others who would otherwise be more reluctant to play with you.

6. Finally, I implore you to actually TALK to the people you're playing with. This game requires a lot of thinking to do well, sure, but any down time (and often there isn't too much of it) is a good time to make up stories for your dwarves and their lifestyle. "Why do you have all your dwellings in the front and then the one dwarf kid has his bed in the back of the cave?" "You know how it is. 'It's just a phase' he tells me." This game is made way more fun if you talk and make up a bit of a story every now and then of your dwarf family's lives. Again, this is something I try to do with all of my board games during down time- too often I find playgroups who dislike certain games because they're too afraid to converse during down time, which is strange to me since board games are meant for you and those around you to TALK. Otherwise it'd just be a clunkier video game you have to have friends over to play.

Is this game for everyone? Probably not. Should you buy it? Look up what people have to say about this versus Agricola to see which you'd prefer. Quite a few people say this game is trash compared to Agricola, so maybe they know more than me. This one, however, has a place in my game collection for eternity.

Pros:
+Components are perfectly done- mostly wooden but also high-stock cardboard pieces.
+Game boards and cards have beautiful artwork and are also very durable.
+DEEP strategy between the furnishings and the different actions possible.
+Fun, appealing theme.
+A good sense of having done something by the end. Great for lovers of resource collection or building games.
+Plays 1-7.
+Requires you to actually think.

Cons:
-Literally heavy
-7 player game might be tedious (haven't tried), but there's enough components in there for 7 (The game could have easily been for 6 or even 5 and would still get played almost as often by about everyone who owns it). Hence, why it's so heavy, and also why...
-As a result of having so many components, set up/put away time is quite long [~10-15 minutes each] (though can be shortened greatly by cooperation... which you wont get in a solo game).
-Somewhat AP susceptible, but with more players often turns will be planned out.

All the cons mentioned have little to do with the actual game, which I find to be nearly perfect. Despite all my quibbles, Caverna gets a 10.

*Minor edit* I've found set up/put away time can be decreased significantly by keeping each resource and tile type in their separate bags (the game comes with plenty of plastic bags to use at your leisure). This works better for me than dumping them out and putting them back at the end.

RATING: 7.6
Solid game. Reiterates Agricola but does away with the cards. That is both an upside (less reading, more playing) as a downside (no variety in setup, risk of favorite strategy lock-in). Also a little friendlier and scoring is easier.

Components are superb, solo play is good as well and there are much, much more choices at any time as in Agricola which is nice if you felt constricted for choice in that game. Another plus is the variety from player count to player count: each player count has it's own set of starting actions available, so the game will differ with each player count.

In short: a huge action space and variability by player count does lessen the pre-set nature of it all but it is still there (favorite lock-in strategies). So with repeated plays with the same group this game might become stale earlier as Agricola.

That being said, an expansion is on the way which probably adds a lot of modular expansions and at least one variable setup (don't know if it does but would be surprised if it would not).

Which game is better? Tough to say. Not burned out on the strategy of Caverna by a long shot, so for now I drift towards it a little more as towards Agricola. In the long run, I see Agricola taking back its spot as it is more variable. But the expansion changes all that and I'm fairly sure I won't hit saturation level with Caverna before that, so for the moment it is Caverna.

For new players this is definitely a friendlier game to pick up, but they need to be experienced gamers as the action space is pretty large. All the playing pieces do really give you a sense of tableau-building, having your nice little farm/cave. My kids loved that feeling, even though the actual game went way over their head. As such, it is a lot of work for dad so not something I'm really eager to break out with them again. Then again, if they request it, how can I say no?

RATING: 8.5
Few, I finally got to play the big one, the perceived nr 1 from last years Essen.
Let me start by reminding you that I like [thing=31260][/thing], but I prefer to play it without the cards. My favorite [person=10][/person] game is actually [thing=35677][/thing]. And I chose that last tile over Agricola every single time. Which is why I don't play Agricola almost ever. Which is why I didn't get Caverna. I wasn't ready to fork out all that money for a game that is based on a game that I like a lot, but never play because an other game just pleases me a lot more.
Caverna does away with the cards and replaces them with a fixed set of buildings that are available to everyone. This will reduce the variability between games, but since I don't play with the cards in Agricola that often, that probably won't bother me in the long run. The biggest difference for me, therefore, was the vastly simplified scoring. A few other things have been streamlined as well.
At heart this game is very similar, but it's not just more of the same. It is a very solid game in it's own right. And the buildings and different strategic possibilities, still offer a very good amount of replay value I expect.
So, do I like it more than it's predecessor? One play isn't enough to know that, but I do like it quite a bit. I don't expect this game to kick Le Havre from it's nr 1 spot for me though.
Production wise this game is top notch, just like all Lookout titles. But why oh why did they include all that material that I'll never use? We play with mostly 3 or 4 players, almost never more. So I'm paying a lot of good money for cardboard that is of no value to me. That is a very sad thing, because I expect this to be the case for most people.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
31-Mar-2014: Well, I can safely say that Caverna is not Agricola. It's strange that Agricola and ACB&S taught us the rules to Caverna. I mean, most of the stuff going on is what we've learnt from before. i.e. Harvests, Feeding family members, what Wood 2 (1) means, etc.

The interesting bits are the randomly allocated Harvest indicators at the end of rounds 6+. So you know that there are about 4 more Harvests coming, but you're not sure when exactly after Roound 6. The "Expeditions" are interesting as well. Each dwarf member can carry a Weapons Token valued between 1-15. It's a little cardboard disc shield that sits on top of your dwarf disc. If you choose an Action space with an Expedition symbol with a dwarf that is armed with a Weapons Token, you get some extra resources based off your Weapon Token value. So it's cute to see your dwarf discs with little weapons on them.

But what's missing, really, is the angst. Although there are more Harvests, Carverna sports even more ways to get Food and simpler ways to build food engines. I built a single Room that gave me +1 Food for each animal I slaughter for Food. Note that you can slaughter animals for Food at any time, similar to how Grain and Vegies are converted to Food in Agricola (i.e. no Stove requirement)
My single room became my Food engine for the rest of the game.

Most of us were hardly in any position to starve. The fixed number of buildings available also lent the air of Puerto Rico buildings. Eventually, you'll get familiar with all the buildings and will try out various strategies around different buildings.

But that simply can't contend against the vast number of Minor Improvements and Occupations that Agricola has.
To me, Caverna was a nice game, but I don't feel the need to own it immediately. Perhaps a few years down the line, if I score a cheap copy.

In conclusion - it's hard to do badly in Caverna. Angst is missing. But it's still a good worker placement game.

RATING: 8
First off, this is very similar to Agricola - to the point where the rules were designed so that if you know Agricola, you are told (in the rulebook) what you need to read to understand the game and what sections you can skip. That being said, this is better than Agricola.

RATING: 7
EDIT: Changed my mind.  The cards in Agricola make it so much better.  This game is fine, but not on par with many of the others above it on my ranking list.   

ORIGINAL COMMENT:
This game is just as good as Agricola, but I think the two games fit different types of players.  I would play Caverna with a group that dislikes aggressive blocking or intense maximization.  Caverna feels more fluid and carefree in terms of gameplay compared to the nervous apprehension in Agricola (which I enjoy, but some may not).  Agricola I would play with a highly competitive group who want to maximize every action they take. In both games you can set yourself up for great actions that feel like an accomplishment, but in Agricola it is much easier to get blown out by opponents blocking you or simply poor planning. The highly competitive group would also enjoy maximizing Caverna, but it is much more friendly to beginners due to the abundance of action spaces and resources. 
I love both games and I feel like their styles are different enough to warrant owning both.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
First Impression:

A "sister" game to Agricola, with a surprisingly different "character".

This game at first glance appears deceptively similar to Agricola. Both are worker placement games with a puzzle aspect, and the designer did little to hide the core mechanics.

However, based on how different they play, the target two distinct sub-audiences in the euro genre.

Here's a brief summary of the gameplay differences:
1) Caverna is not nearly as tight and mean as Agricola. Feeding is a breeze, as if starting Agricola with 4 kids eating half the food and a super fireplace already build. Actually, it's even easier than that.
2) In Caverna you never struggle for resources, filling your farm or getting this and that. You can achieve everything easily, even in your first game. The question is how efficiently you'll fill your farm and how much you'll manage to accumulate (there is no "upper limit" in anything). The reason for that is that, 
3)appart from the super easy feeding, there are two joker categories in the game, rubies and expeditions that can be used to do just about anything and a bit more. And mind you these are not just a couple of action spots, these are the core elements of the game and take up half the board.
4) The actions spots are all double (or triple or more) compared to agricola and as a result, there is no such thing as blocking in Caverna. For example there are three spots for plowing in Caverna straight from the start (4p game) compared to 1(+1 in late game) in Agricola. And plowing in those actions is not the only thing you do, actually it's 1/3 of the action. And that is without counting the jokers. Unless you choose to fail in a category, the minus points written on the scoring sheet are nothing but decorative.
5) Another consequence of the multi-action action spots is that they increase the complexity. This is great news for those who love to count points 4 times their turn, but for me it just creates serious downtime for no reason. Playing one dwarf in Caverna is like playing 2-4 workers back to back in Agricola but masquerated to make it look like it was just one - but a ninja. 
6) In Agricola one look at another player's farm got you all the info you needed to see if he's doing well, if not and most importantly what he needs (to maybe block it). In Caverna you have no idea, everyone is so full that unless you pause to start counting donkeys you have no clue who's ahead. And basically, since there is no blocking and no risking, you have no reason to bother. Caverna is five steps closer to multiplayer solitaire which is great news for some, and terrible news for me.
6) In Agricola you have a small common pool of very important major improvements, and 7+7 private cards that differ from game to game. In Caverna every game is similar to the next, but there are some 50 rooms to choose from straight from the beginning. The difference is mind blowing. Agricola is easier to play and at the same time very replayable. Caverna is twice as complex straight from round one, but plays the same every time. Those who like to play a game just a few times before trading it/passing on to the newest one will love Caverna for it's "depth and complexity". Those who study late at night for optimal strategies will love Caverna as it gives them a huge spreadsheet to work on. Those who like luck-free games will probably like both, although a small subcategory will prefer Caverna for being even more luck free. Those who like to keep and play a game for life (like me) will dislike Caverna, sooner or later.
7) Caverna has no sense of accomplishment. Since everything is so super easy to achieve you really feel nothing for your efforts. It's like a Feld game actually, all actions will give you VPs one way or another. Four round orange markers in your farm in Agricola means you maxed your vegetables after a long struggle with both the game and the other players. Four beutifully sculpted veggy-pumpkins in Caverna means just 4 points -it's almost sad.

Caverna is not for me. In Agricola I enjoy the interaction, the struggling environment (which creates very tough decisions from realatively simple actions) the replayability and its' excellent sense of accomplishment. Cavarna trades most of these for things I don't care nearly half as much.

RATING: 7
Ah, the [thing=31260][/thing] feels! This will mostly be about comparing [thing=102794][/thing] to [thing=31260][/thing], if that is something you want to know, carry on.

[b]Rules of dungeoneering:[/b]
There are several similarities between the two games, but the most noticeable is the absence of cards for the players to use. They were all replaced for buildings, all available, at first, to everyone. So, the set up is the very same for all. Then, in spite of having several feeding moments, some that could be one after the other (with only one round of preparation of it, like the final round of [thing=31260][/thing]), it is much easier to avoid hunger, as is possible to transform animals, grains, vegetables in food without the requirement of a fireplace or other cooking implements. Furthermore, the game has money, which can also be used to buy food.

There are several intricacies to the game rules, but the basics are: you have a cave space, where the dwarves live, and a forest space, outside the cave. In the cave players can excavate to open tunnels and new areas for new living quarters or buildings, and can mine, to obtain ore and rubies. In the forest is possible to cut the trees down to get spaces to farm and grow crops, or places to surround and turn into pastures for animals. The actions [thing=102794][/thing] are mostly similar to those in [thing=31260][/thing]: get resources (ore, wood, stone, rubies, food, animals), make buildings, fences, plow fields, excavate, making the family bigger, etc.

And also making weapons and go into adventures. Yes, this is the main different part of the actions. Though it is less "adventurous" than it might sound. In game terms it works this way: there are spots in the board in which is possible to forge a weapon by spending ore - the more ore spent, the bigger will be the level of the weapon. With weapons, the dwarves can go into adventures - they are always successful, and there really ins't an adventure phase, in which the dwarves form parties, fight monsters, loot rooms, and these sort of things. What do happens is: there are places that, coupled with a normal actions (like gather wood) the dwarf can also go into an adventure - these go from level 1 to 4. The dwarf gets rewards accordingly to the level of the weapon he has or bellow - so, if a dwarf has a level 7 weapon, he can get rewards from the 7th level or lower. The level of the adventure is equal to how many rewards the dwarf will get: one level 3 adventure gives 3 different rewards. Therefore, using a dwarf with a level 7 weapon, after going to a level 3 adventure, can get one reward level 7, one 5 and one 4, for instance. After getting the rewards, the dwarf rises the level of the weapon in one. Finally, it is worth mentioning that several of the spaces on the main board have two options of actions to use - in most spaces, is possible to do both, and in some, only one or the other. In any case, the duo possibility allows for more to think in the moment of choosing which space to use, and this is a good thing.

Buildings, as one can expect, make certain actions better, allow for others, make for room for more dwarves, to gain points at the end of the game, etc. All the buildings start available for anyone to build them, but most are unique, and once built by someone, you won't be able to get an equal replacement, which makes the fight for them more fierce and the timming to get them all that more relevant.

The goal of the game is to make points, but is different that that of [thing=31260][/thing]. In [thing=31260][/thing] players are required to cover well all areas, first because of the limition in points each of the things have, so, usually, there is no way to make a "grain strategy" or a "cow strategy", as neither of these, alone, will allow to make a whole lot of points, enough to lead to a win; and second, due to the negative points given by lack of things. In [thing=102794][/thing], players can focus their play in certain aspects of the game and do pretty well. Yes, there are still negative points for missing types of animals and unused areas of the player board, but these aren't enough to completely prevent a winning strategy that takes these negative points into consideration. This is because there is no limit of points something can give, so is possible to take one element - rubies, animals, adventures, etc - and boost it to maximum, maybe enough to lead to victory. In [thing=31260][/thing] the winner is usually the best generalist. In [thing=102794][/thing] the winner can be the best specialist.

Oh, and there are shepherd dogs. I love them. I want all of them.

[b]Decisions:[/b]
The set of decisions to be made in game are as complex as those in [thing=31260][/thing], but my take is that, in the first plays, there will be more difficulty to play well, as the spectre of options is broader - there are 48 buildings, while in [thing=31260][/thing] the player has to deal with less than 10 cards and 10 major improvements. Sure, is still possible to play a simpler version in the first games, in order to get used to the ins and outs; to me, though, playing this way, as in the family version of [thing=31260][/thing] (without cards), half of the fun is missing, and this can harm the prospect of follow-up plays. Still, for those familiar with [thing=31260][/thing] I guess it will be easy (as it was for me) to enter stright into the full game of [thing=102794][/thing].

[b]Interaction:[/b]
As in [thing=31260][/thing], in [thing=102794][/thing] the main interaction between players is "someone got what I wanted". And that is it. Is even smaller than you can get in [thing=31260][/thing] when using some of the more interactive decks. [thing=102794][/thing] is definitely one of the type of games in which you do your thing, I do mine, and we meet in the to compare scores. The game requires some attention to what others are up to on their boards, however, it likely won't be something decisive in most plays, and will rarely get into a point that will make someone get their feelings hurt, mad or chased by the others. It is possible that, at a point, losing a key space or building might harm the momentum of a strategy, to the point of making a player lose. But then, this can pass by separated issues (if it something was that important, one should have got it sooner, for instance). Overall, [thing=102794][/thing] can be easily recommended for partners that play often head to head, as the focus stays more in making your own strategy work and use your board better than to throw a wrench in the strategy of others. And, no, this isn't a good title for persons that haven't a good experience in playing board games.

[b]Playing time:[/b]
Perhaps by not having as much pressure to feed the family, [thing=102794][/thing] allowed for a briskier game - in 3 players, we finished the game in around 80 minutes. There were some moments of slowness, pondering about the several options, specially when choosing the reward of adventures - it was common for the game turn pass around and return to the adventuring player and he still didn't choose all the rewards, as everything is good and is of use, and is not always easy to know what is best in that precise moment.

[b]Components:[/b]
The components are of good quality, with the usual great art by Klemens Franz, to the point of having a "night" board, that really doesn't do anything, but is there. Also, the game comes with 2 metric tons (conservative estimation) of wood inside the box, with enough pieces for 7 players. Now, I don't get why someone would want to inflict such a torture on himself and others by playing with 6 or 7. Well, in one hand, it does give more color options to choose from; and, on the other, it makes the price steeper and a pain to carry the game around, as the weight of everything is an absurd.

[b]Final words:[/b]
Overall, [thing=102794][/thing] showed itself as a good game, filled with options for the players, in a level of complexity that will require some plays to be fully understand, and yet, even in the first play, is possible to reach some success in varying degrees. [thing=102794][/thing] does fill the same spot as that of [thing=31260][/thing], with several similarities between them, and even in feel of gameplay - and I can't see why owning both. For me [thing=102794][/thing] isn't superior to [thing=31260][/thing], nor is inferior. [thing=31260][/thing] seems more demanding and cruel - even more when playing with certain decks, or with the great expansion [thing=43018][/thing]. [thing=102794][/thing] is more open, more loose, without so many restraints. However, the potential interrelations of the hundreds of cards of [thing=31260][/thing] allow, if not a really much different end to the farm, at least an almost infinite amount of ways to reach that end. In [thing=102794][/thing], what worked once, can probably work again, as the set of options is always the same at first. It is up for the players to challenge themselves, to seek new paths.

[thing=102794][/thing] is, in the end, a good game, with enough to stand by its own merits.

RATING: 8

That being said, I personally like Caverna a little better because of (a) the theme (fantasy, Dwarves), (b) its tempo (most actions are really 2-in-1 in Agricola terms), (c) the building tiles that replace the cards, (d) the streamlined scoring (very clear and simple) and (e) the more forgiving feeding phase (making it easier to actually get things done and make progress). Because of the overal stress reduction, my girlfriend likes it a LOT better. So I sold Agricola :-).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
At first, this game seemed like it was Agricola 2.0 as many claim it to be. You will enjoy it and have plenty of fun for a series of plays. However, overtime you will get bored of it and it will get extremely repetitive. It will get to the point where you simply just don't want to play anymore, or at least that's how I feel. I was planning on buying the game but my cousins beat me to it. At first I felt pretty bad because my Agricola game seemed impervious to this updated game, but not anymore.

First off, there is very little variance in the game. It's the same rooms every time and therefore you'll be going for the same strategy, every time. Sure, your strategy may change as plays are made and people end up taking your rooms and resources, but you still move onto other strategies which are also the same thing every game. The game is also way too easy, after all the games I've played, no one has gotten the beggar token. It's like there is no reason for it. Moves just aren't as meaningful as they are in Agricola, you're just never on the edge and while Agricola could of been a little more forgiving, this game is just intensely forgiving.

As for the expedition concept, it sounded great and was fun for a few, but eventually it became annoying and it's too powerful to avoid. This idea adds unnecessary length to the game as do all these additional actions we get when we compare this game to Agricola. The length of this game is just way too long, the more people, the worse it becomes. It's just so hard focusing in this game, makes me wanna do something else as I wait for everyone to take their turn. As for our player boards, I'm not sure why the developer would not allow them to nicely store resources on and to have them not be generic. Everyone has the same board for crying out loud, why?

In the end, this game is pretty bad and I no longer think it's better than Agricola. It was a direction that the developer shouldn't of went in with these farming board games, it just didn't work. I'm really not surprised why no expansions are being developed for it. The board also wasn't very colorful or appealing to look at, while that has no real importance, it's something to mention when the gameplay doesn't make up for it. Now instead of adding this game to my collection, I rather just purchase some expansions for Agricola.

As for right now there is only one way this game can be fixed and that's to add a major expansion which would add many more new rooms to the game where we can put in a bag, mix up for each game, and draw them out on the table so things are different each time, giving us different strategies to work with, which would greatly increase replayability in the game.

RATING: 6.5
Nice game that feels like Agricola and glass road mashed together. Comparisons to Agricola heavily used because game claims to be an improvement of it and is regarded as the next messiah. However, it does not drop Agricola from its throne. Here there is no urgency to feed your family so it feels like a point optimization instead of a survival battle. This will appeal to the mamas-boys who feel that Agricola is an evil game and don't like it when the game itself is a challenge, reducing it to pure points collecting. I have also started losing faith in the boardgaming community seeing this be praised as an improvement over Agricola, it saddens me that we seem to want our food ready chewed.
Note that this does not mean that Caverna is bad, it's just mediocre and never my first choice.

RATING: 8
Not sure if I enjoy Caverna more than Agricola.  So tough to choose!  I love how open Caverna is (vs. the always starving-to-death restrictedness of Agricola).  Always having really great options is definitely a plus: you can take numerous different routes to developing your settlement and earning points.  Clearing your board and cave gives you a real sense of accomplishment.  Adventuring/leveling up your dwarves is also a fantastic addition to this type of game.  Where Caverna suffers in comparison to Agricola is: not all of the heaps and heaps of rooms available are as interesting and/or useful as others.  Some of those rooms have never seen play in my games.  Having a bunch of rooms available pales in comparison to Agricola's occupation/minor improvement decks.  Those are way better.  Either way, I'm always happy to play this or Agricola.  They're different flavors of an otherwise delicious treat!    
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent "building" game. Lived up to Agricola 2.0 moniker.
Pros: Tile laying to build your cave and farm is fun! Lots of valuable spots and upgrades, feeding doesn't feel like a horrible chore but finding a food solution is more fun. Many options for converting resources to food if needed. No more fiddly wood stick fences! Plays 2 players - 5 players well, but supports 6 and 7 if needed. 

Cons: Biggest complaint is there are extra components for a 7th player but I feel like I'll never actually go that high! Game is expensive, never would have bought it w/o Amazon credit. SOOOO many pieces between resource token and tons of tiles, I had to build my own insert. Relying on bags alone is terrible. I hate sorting through ~40 tokens to find the weapon level you need 5-10 times a game.

Overall, the game is great, but pretty expensive and yet STILL requires a few upgrades (Which takes time or money or both) to negate a few component downsides.

RATING: 9
Maddening in a different way than Agricola. Here you're  paralyzed by too many choices and actions vs competition for scarce resources/actions. The game feels very Agricolaesque this way and there are many similarities. A bit long for 4 players but probably as long as Agricola with 4. Enjoyed it more with 4 than with 3. Also eliminates the "unfairness" of occupations and minor improvements for people who complain about that in Agricola. Overall, a more "easygoing" and less tight game in terms of less direct competition with opponents for actions and resources (that changes at higher player counts) and less pressure to feed your people. More of a sandbox game as there are several strategies you can adopt to win. 

After playing with 2, Caverna is a better, meatier game than Agricola for that number. There is more you can do and more paths to victory. The game also lasts a bit longer.

RATING: 7
Caverna feels like a less "tight" version of Agricola (due to the massive number of double action spaces and the ease of getting food). It's a good game with many viable paths to victory and interesting decisions, but it really suffers from static setup. I miss the huge variety of cards from Agricola - the puzzle of making strategy on the basis of unique combination of cards made each game unique and special. 

The "sandbox" nature of Caverna may appeal to many players, but for me it just kills most of the tension and the sense of real struggle that were crucial to the fantastic gaming experience in Agricola. That sense of freedom is just an illusion anyway - you still get punished for all the things you didn't do so you'll have to do a bit of everything. Still,  I would pick Caverna over Fields of Arle any day - that game is way too open-ended for my taste.

And just a word of warning - the number of information on the board to process before the first game is rather daunting. This feeling goes away after you understand what you do, but still there's a lot to take in.

RATING: 9
I enjoy Agricola quite a bit, but I always considered adding an additional round before harvest to give myself and the other players a bit more time to do more, just a little less struggling and a bit more building. So if I had one complaint about Agricola it's that pressure that I'd rather not deal with.

Caverna fixes that problem and removes some interesting parts of Agricola while adding some new mechanics that aren't any more or less interesting than Agricola, just different. 

- We lose the card play that Agricola is lauded for: no occupations or minor improvements. I'm okay with this and I think it actually helps with new players.
- We gain a building mechanism that simplifies building fences, rooms, and fields, in addition to gaining other rooms that give you the same perks and benefits that major and minor occupations once did. 
- We have more resources and less to worry about come harvest time, but we know less about when harvest time is.

Overall, I prefer this to Agricola, but it's not a replacement. I worry about Caverna's long-term prospects and whether or not you'd get as much play out of it compared to Agricola. However, for now it's a fantastic game and one worth paying the premium price for.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Caverns is a fun spin on a worker placement game. There are a lot of ways to win, and my strategy continues to evolve across each round depending on my opponents play. My biggest complaint about this game is that the length of the game increases with the number of players, and even at the end of a long game it can leave you feeling like there is still more to do.

RATING: 8.5
After playing my friend's copy of Agricola (which I originally avoided due to its 18th century farming theme), I ran out and picked up a copy of Caverna (as to avoid overlap with my friend's collection of games).  And while I am in the minority, I like it better, and here's why: Caverna has no luck to it.  There's no chance that one player get a killer hand of Minor Improvements and Occupations while another gets crap.  All of the Furnishings (this game's equivalent) are available to all players at the start of the game and are first come first serve.  You have to prepare a good strategy and a viable backup strategy in case someone grabs the Furnishing you were hoping for.  And if you want it to be like Agricola, fine, then ignore the rulebook and hand out Funishings to each player at random at the start of the game.  It's much easier to add a luck aspect to this game than it is to remove it from Agricola (unless you want to play Family Version every time).  There's also just more you can do in this game than Agricola, and the parts that needed to be simplified have been.  Fantastic game, should be in everyone's collection.

My ONLY complaint about this game is that it's just as deep, if not deeper than Agricola and so turns can take a really long time if players really consider all of their options.  In games of 3 or more, expect to see players get distracted by their smartphones.

RATING: 9
The (main) reason I rated Caverna that high is the fact that I love(!) boardgames designed by Uwe Rosenberg - I love the theme and I love the mechanics (and I love the fact that there are cute little donkeys involved in the whole thing). Another thing I really like is the artwork made by Klemens Franz - especially all the little details appearing only on some of the tiles. Allthough it appears to be a heavy, complex game, the whole theme makes it really easy to understand what e.g. to do to get food for the dwarfs, what the dwarfs need weapons for and why you have to dig through the mountains or cut down the forest. I think the story itself makes the game so wonderful and allthough it sometimes takes 3 hours to finish a game, I have used it as a gateway game for friends of mine who rarely play (and sometimes never even played "middle/heavy weight") board games and the fact that they loved it almost as much as I do, is proof that it doesn't take games like Ticket to Ride (which I have used as a gateway game before - but unfortunately some people got stuck on it) to get people to play "great" board games. Thank you, Uwe!

5 after 1 play

Artwork and game components quality are very good. The game mechanic is however let down by the "looseness" of the action spaces. This reduces player interaction as viable alternatives are almost certainly available and also increases the multiple player solitaire feel. On top of that, the tableau of 48 different tiles to consider throughout the game increases downtime drastically. Lastly, the strength of the expeditions makes taking other actions decidedly weaker and the rewards of the expeditions are not thematic.

The area I feel like Agricola does better is in the card play which leads to greater variability. RATING: 9
Love this. It's a heavy game, but approachable enough that I am able to play with my 9 and 11 year old daughters. 

Every time you have to make a decision there are difference factors to consider, the board is constantly changing and there are many paths to points. I love the amount of choices you have and the interestingness of the decisions you have to make (you can never do everything).

Also, the pieces are adorable and breeding animals, growing pumpkins, etc. and watching your farm/family/home grow is really satisfying.

The only downside is that it can take a long time to play, especially since some players can experience AP and take too long on their turns. I recommend having some sort of time limit just to keep things moving.

But aside from that it's a great game, I find it engaging all the way through and it's great with my kids.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
To me, this game is Agricola perfected.  It removes the more punishing aspects of food generation, the punishing aspects of random card dealing and the stress involved with playing Agricola and replaces them with fun, fun, fun.

In Agricola, I always felt I was playing against the game first and the other players second, with Caverna, I feel like I am in a race against other players to accomplish what I think will win me the game.  While Agricola punishes you hard for failure to pay attention to food, Caverna just sets you back a bit.  A minor penalty vs. a game misconduct (to use hockey terms).

The components are fantastic, much better than most other games, and the theme of farming and developing your cave feels integrated and immersive.

While I still believe that Agricola has a place in the gaming world as a truly difficult game and does not need to be removed from print, for most people, Caverna is going to give you more bang for your buck and be a much more enjoyable game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love worker placement and engine building, and even though the theme on this is kind of 'bleh' (farming, come on Uwe, use a different theme!), I can forgive it, as the theme does shine in this game, more so than in Agricola, and other games by Uwe Rosenberg, where the theme seems to melt away behind the mechanics. At the end of the game, even if you didn't win, at least you can look at the cool farm/cave system you built.
